## Editorial note

This is the complete text of *The Deportations Delirium of Nineteen-Twenty* by
**Louis F. Post** (1849–1928), published in 1923 by Charles H. Kerr & Company. Post was
[Henry George](/wiki/henry-george/)'s ablest lieutenant — editor of the single-tax weekly
*The Public* (1898–1913) and the movement's most senior federal officeholder — and he wrote
this book as the [Assistant Secretary of Labor](/wiki/louis-f-post/) who, in the winter and
spring of 1920, personally reviewed the backlog of Palmer-raid deportation cases, dismissed
roughly 71% of them, cancelled well over 1,500 warrants as unlawfully obtained, and then
demolished the impeachment brought against him for doing so. It is at once a memoir and a
documentary indictment of one of the worst civil-liberties episodes in American history,
with an Introduction by the civil-libertarian lawyer **Moorfield Storey**.

The wiki holds it because it is the primary record of a Georgist statesman carrying
single-tax principle — the same commitment to justice that made land monopoly intolerable —
into the defense of due process for immigrants. See the wiki page
[about this book](/wiki/deportations-delirium/) and the biography of
[Louis F. Post](/wiki/louis-f-post/).

**Public-domain status:** first published in the United States in 1923 by Charles H. Kerr &
Company; long out of copyright under the US 95-year rule (pre-1931). The author died in 1928,
so it is also clear under life-plus-70. Reproduced in full below.

**Text fidelity:** this is a mechanical transcription of Harvard's page-level OCR (see the
`provenance` field above). It has not yet been proofread against the page images; residual
OCR artifacts remain and a proofreading pass is queued. Quote against it with that caveat.

---

## The Text

**THE DEPORTATIONS DELIRIUM OF NINETEEN-TWENTY**

*A Personal Narrative of an Historic Official Experience*

**LOUIS F. POST**, Assistant Secretary of Labor of the United States, From 1913 to 1921

*With an Introduction by Moorfield Storey*

Chicago: Charles H. Kerr & Company, Co-operative. Copyright 1923, Charles H. Kerr & Company. Printed in the United States of America.

### Contents

I. The Sensational Voyage of "the Soviet Ark"
II. Culpability of "the Soviet Ark" Deportees
III. Deportation Raids in Anticipation of the Voyage of "the Soviet Ark"
IV. The Bombs That Begot the Deportations Delirium
V. Preparations for the "Red" Crusade
VI. Beginnings of the "Red" Crusade
VII. The January Raids — A Nation-wide Glance
VIII. The Raiding in New England
IX. The Raiding in New York and Vicinity
X. The Raiding in Philadelphia and Vicinity
XI. The Raiding About Pittsburgh and Buffalo
XII. The Raiding at Cleveland and Southwestward to the Coast
XIII. The Raiding in the Region of Chicago
XIV. The Raiding at Detroit
XV. The Assistant Secretary of Labor Takes Charge
XVI. Communist Party Members
XVII. Communist Labor Party Members
XVIII. A Clutter of "Red" Records
XIX. Decisions in the "Red" Cases
XX. Principles of Decision
XXI. Routine Methods
XXII. Deportations Directed
XXIII. Deportations Denied
XXIV. Distinctive Cases
XXV. Impeachment Proceedings Initiated
XXVI. An Angel from the Business Skies
XXVII. Opening of the Impeachment Fight
XXVIII. Before the House Committee on Rules
XXIX. Attorney General Palmer on the Defensive
XXX. Collapse of the Impeachment Proceedings
XXXI. An After-Play
XXXII. The Salsedo Homicide
XXXIII. Case of the Russian Ambassador
XXXIV. Lawless Meddling with the Mail
XXXV. Indictment of the Department of Justice by Twelve Eminent American Lawyers
XXXVI. The Department of Justice at the Bar of the Senate
XXXVII. Summary and Conclusion

Appendix — Fundamental American Principles

---

### Prefatory Note by the Author

The following story of that delirious outburst in the United States against resident aliens which closely followed the World War and was generally known as "the Red Crusade, " is a careful condensation, by the author, of an unpublished manuscript of his in which the events, described in greater detail, are verified by citations in support of every important statement. The personal phases of the narrative are necessitated by the circumstances, as readers will readily see. Its appalling facts are pregnant with wholesome lessons in the fundamental principles of Americanism.

L. F. P.

Washington, D. C, October 1, 1923.

### Introduction by Moorfield Storey

When the United States was conquering the Philippine Islands, a leading citizen of Massachusetts said "The question is whether the people of this country really believe in their own principles " and the question was never more pressing than today. We read with pride the Declaration of Independence, and we boast of our wonderful Constitution with the security which it affords to the meanest citizen. We pride ourselves on our freedom of speech, our protection against unwarrantable searches and seizures, the bulwark against oppression afforded by our right to due process of law, and we comfortably assume that in practice the provisions of the Constitution are respected. We resent the charge that we are a lawless people, and close our eyes to lynching mobs whose members go unwhipped of justice. As an abstract proposition it does not seem possible to us that the officers of the United States should under pretense of enforcing the laws trample on the Constitution, and we drift on complacently assured of our safety, in the confident belief that all is going well under the best of all possible governments.

There is however a body of law-abiding people who think that it will not do to be too solicitous about the rights of men who break laws which they approve, while they are not too careful themselves to obey the laws which they do not approve. Many excellent persons would deal severely with those who embezzle the funds of the government or are guilty of election frauds, but do not hesitate on returning from Europe to smuggle in dutiable goods, or to evade payment of their just taxes. So to-day on the one hand many of society's leaders condemn the Volstead law and do not hesitate to violate it, while on the other hand the officers charged with its enforcement take such steps as they think best calculated to get results, without the least regard to the constitutional requirements of warrants and due process. Not only that, but they abuse the high officers of the law who respect those requirements. The first class say the 18th Amendment ought not to be the law and should not be obeyed. Their opponents say that those who disobey so salutary a law should not be allowed to shield themselves by provisions manifestly intended for the benefit of honest men and not of professional criminals. They do not admit that there can be a question as to the guilt of the men they attack, or that the latter are entitled to insist that their guilt shall be proved according to law before they are punished. Between these two lawless bodies the Constitution suffers. Theodore Roosevelt's famous story of the question "What is the Constitution between friends?" is still told, but the more pressing question to-day is "What is the Constitution between enemies?" The general feeling is that in a country where liberty is so secure, we can afford to ignore its safeguards if thereby what seems a desirable object can more easily be obtained.

There can be no more dangerous doctrine, and this volume should bring its perils home to the citizens of this country and make them think. Mr. Post was the Assistant Secretary of Labor under President Wilson, and had a direct personal knowledge of the facts with which he deals, and his revelations are most astonishing.

During the great war there was an evidently concerted attempt to hinder the aid which we gave the Allies by selling them arms, ammunition and such other things as they needed to prosecute the war successfully. Everyone must recall the explosions and conflagrations by which factories and stores were destroyed, the explosives placed on ships, and the other attempts to hamper the production and delivery of supplies. There was every reason to suspect that these outrages were committed in the course of a deliberate campaign planned and carried on by representatives of the German government. Here was certainly an opportunity for detectives to discover the men responsible for these crimes and bring them to justice.

In only very few instances were the perpetrators or their accomplices detected. As a whole the campaign was not interrupted and the criminals were not punished.

There was the famous explosion in Wall Street, and several minor ones of which one injured Attorney-General Palmer's house. A number of bombs on another occasion were sent through the mail, but in only one case did the bomb reach its destination, thanks to the vigilance of an official in the post-office. The men responsible for these crimes were never discovered. So far as these serious matters were concerned, our police system failed, but the explosions terrified the community and produced a general feeling of uneasiness. The country felt sure there was a body of dangerous criminals at large, and this feeling was justified. When therefore the Department of Justice alleged that there was a very large body of "Beds" in the country, aliens with the principles of Russian Bolsheviks, who were organizing here to overthrow the government of the United States by force, and followed the statement up by raids all over the country in which men and women, aliens and citizens, were seized and imprisoned, we did not disturb ourselves about questions of constitutional law. The men responsible for these arrests, the detectives and those who employed them, filled the newspapers with lurid accounts of what the "Reds" had done and were planning, and produced on a small scale a "reign of terror" in which some thousands of innocent people were very cruelly treated and exposed to much suffering and loss. The statements in the newspapers were false and misleading. There was no conspiracy to overthrow this government and no evidence was ever produced which excused the action of the government. The safeguards of the Constitution were ignored, and any true American must blush at what was done and at the indifference with which he and all but a handful of his countrymen tolerated it.

Mr. Post's book gives the facts briefly and clearly, is very interesting and will repay careful reading. It is in part a defence of his own course in the crisis, but as he was attacked and threatened with impeachment and dismissal from office because he tried to protect the innocent men who were arrested and imprisoned, this is an important part of the narrative. It shows how far official tyranny was willing to go.

It is no part of an introduction to summarize the contents of the book, and it is enough to say that it is a very important contribution to the history of this country, and a warning to us all. We cannot wrap ourselves in the comfortable belief that our government will run itself, and that in our fortunate country "eternal vigilance" is not "the price of liberty." We cannot always control the violence of the mob, but we can at least insist that the men, who are charged with the enforcement of the law, shall not take the lead in breaking it and in ignoring the fundamental principle that every man is presumed to be innocent until he is proved to be guilty, and until his conviction is entitled to all the protection which the law gives to an innocent man.

We must awaken to the very serious condition which confronts the country. Never was the law so generally flouted. Not only does the South deny the Negro his right as a citizen to vote, but it counts him as a voter in order to determine how many representatives in Congress and how many votes in the Electoral College it is entitled to, and casts his vote for him. Not only does it deny him protection for his life and his property, but it does not try to punish the inhuman cruelties which he is compelled to endure. The leaders of this people, political, religious and educational, have so long been blind to these courses that we have not realized how they have gradually undermined regard for the law all over the country. And now we find a large body of our countrymen, respectable men, many of whom are doubtless inspired by the spirit of lynching, organized to disregard the law and the constitutional rights of their fellowcitizens, and arrogate to themselves the right to punish at their pleasure any man or woman without trial for any cause which they think proper.

They adopt all kinds of childish titles, they are careful to disguise themselves, and add to their influence by the assumption of secrecy. They openly assert their right to war in various ways upon Catholics, Jews and the Negroes. The organization cannot last, but its mere existence shows how slight a hold respect for the law has on our people. No gift of prophecy is needed to foretell what disastrous consequences must follow unless our lawabiding citizens rouse themselves and insist that all laws must be enforced and that whoever breaks any law must if detected face certain punishment. Moorfield Storey.

### Chapter I: The Sensational Voyage of "the Soviet Ark"

Christmas drew near, the exact date being^December 21^ 1919, and the hour, as one newspaper picturesquely described it, "in the blackness of the early morning. " A sensational sea-voyage was about to begin at the port of New York on board a vessel belonging to the Government of the United States. As to destination, she was subject to sealed orders. Her passengers were 249 aliens in process of deportation under the American immigration laws. They were Russian residents of the United States whose banishment from this country, adjudged by no court, had been decreed under " administrative process. "

This arbitrary process of autocratic governments in the past is to-day with reference to alien residents a prerogative of the Executive branch of our Federal government. But for the writ of habeas corpus it would be as absolute as the power of the King of England was to imprison his subjects before the revival of that ancient writ, or the like power of the King of France before the French Revolution. In virtue, however, of this ancient English writ of right, this judicial guardian of personal liberty, our "administrative process " for expelling resident aliens is subject to examination into its regularity in open court.

In every alien-expulsion case there must be lawfully-enacted Congressional legislation authorizing the expulsion, together with some degree of proof that the obnoxious alien is within its scope. If either the legislative authority or proof in the individual case be lacking, and the case comes before a competent court on writ of habeas corpus, the court may, and in obedience to its lawful duty must, disregard the "administrative process, " and, by sustaining the writ of habeas corpus, release the protesting alien from Executive custody. If, however, there be Congressional authority and the proceedings are regular, and if there be any proof at all to bring the alien within that authority — no matter how slight the proof nor how convincingly refuted by counter proof — the Executive decision to expel is absolute and the "administrative process" effective.

It was pursuant to Executive orders made in virtue of this despotic process that those 249 in-, voluntary passengers — " deportees " in official languag — were about to sail from New York for an unknown port "in the blackness " of that early morning just before Christmas and a few days before the coming in of the year Nineteen-twenty.

By way of allusion to the peculiar character of their voyage, the vessel on which they were to sail was known, journalistically and therefore popularly, as "the Soviet Ark/' — a verbal slant at the then two-year-old government of Russia. Her official name, however, was the "Buford." Bnilt in the latter part of the previous century, she was an obscure army transport with a useful service-record in the Spanish-American War of the eighteennineties and, two decades later, in the War of the World. She had a burden rating of about five thousand tons and the reputation among sailors of being a " sea-roller. " The War Department had loaned her, at the suggestion of the State Department, to the Department of Labor for an experiment in effecting deportations of Russians which for lack of regular communication with Russia at that time was otherwise impracticable.

There being no regular communication between the United States and Russia, aliens expelled from this country could not, without some kind of special arrangement, be deported "to the country whence they came/' as the law required, if they happened to have come from Russia. So the Secretary of Labor, the Cabinet official charged by law with administrative responsibility for deporting aliens, relying upon an arrangement by the State Department with Finland — then at war with Russia, — whereby his deportees could be transferred to Russia under flag of truce, borrowed the "Buford" for a deportations experiment.

The sensational sailing, its sensation character fostered by private detectives in the employment of the Department of Justice, was described by one of the New York newspapers as having "brought mingled emotions of pride and resentment to those who watched the vessel pass out of sight, " for "America had risen in her might and delivered a blow that reached the very soul of the incorrigible fanatics" who had "sought every means in their power to overthrow the United States government and bring a chaos satisfying to their degenerate minds." They were characterized as "249 blasphemous creatures who not only rejected America's hospitality and assailed her institutions, but also sought by a campaign of assassination and terrorism to ruin her as a nation of free men." These were typical expressions of the sentiment to which the American press, with but few exceptions, gave voice when the "Buford" set sail.

In editorial comment as well as in news reports, and especially in conspicuous and sensational headlines, most of the daily papers of the United States made that shipload of deportees appear to excitable readers to be a collection of convicted anarchists of the bomb-throwing variety, caught in destructive plotting against the Government of the United States. And this atmosphere which the daily press generated was with few exceptions inhaled and exhaled by the weekly press. So it came to be generally understood, quite contrary to the fact, that the 249 "Buford" deportees had been convincingly adjudged guilty of murderous activities.

The conduct, however, of the administrative officials in lawful control was for the most part humane. From this considerate policy which Secretary Wilson had ordered there was but one serious variation. The wives and young children of some of the deportees were cruelly left behind. To their consequent suffering, and quite irrespective of such mental anguish as the bewildered husbands and fathers may have endured, only a hard-boiled alien-hater could be indifferent.

None of this^was Secretary Wilson's fault. He had no funds for sending their families along with the deportees, for Congress negligently leaves the families of deported aliens in the plight of families of convicted criminals — and this notwithstanding the legal commonplace that alien deportees are not convicted criminals. So Mr. Wilson directed that no deportee having wife or child in this country be placed on board the "Buford." His instructions were observed and obeyed at all the Immigration Stations but one. This was the station at Ellis Island, New York. If they reached that station they were either overlooked or disobeyed. Consequently there were many pathetic instances of family separation.

Notice of only a few hours was given to the impounded deportees at Ellis Island that their voyage was about to begin. They had no opportunity to notify any one; and not until the "Buford" was far out at sea were their relatives or friends or even the lawyers of those who had lawyers aware of any intention to deport them at that or any other approximate time. It is not strange, therefore, that the deportations seemed to bereaved families left behind, and to their friends, to have been vicious kidnappings instead of regular, even if harsh, administrative proceedings according to law.

Most of the women whose husbands were thus secretly shipped away from them were dependent upon their husbands for support. Some had saved, out of scant wages for hard work, a little money "against a rainy day"; but the money of all was beyond their reach, mostly in postal-savings or bank accounts subject to their husbands' drafts or in the form of unpaid wages. Many had sold their household goods, expecting to go along with their husbands. Some had small children to care for. Most of them were in abject want.

To mitigate all this harm, measures were taken promptly upon the "Buford's" arrival at her destination. Although it was then too late to avoid the weeks of suffering from want by family dependents left behind when the expedition sailed so suddenly and so secretly, facilities were finally afforded for executing and forwarding to the United States powers of attorney for the collection by American dependents of moneys that might be owing to any of the deportees. About 100 such documents were executed and forwarded to the designated persons in America. The individual sums varied, but the aggregate was $45,470.39. It included postal-savings credits, credits on checking accounts at commercial banks, uncollected wages, personal debts, and Liberty-bond holdings.

Those considerate measures were immediately due to the good management of F. W. Berkshire, the Supervising Inspector of Immigration on the Mexican border, who had been detailed by Secretary Wilson, at the suggestion of Commissioner General Caminetti, to manage the "Buford" expedition during its voyage. To support him in a mission apparently so perilous, a strong guard of soldiers had been furnished by the War Department. It consisted of a colonel, a lieutenant-colonel, 4 lieutenants and 58 enlisted men.

Notwithstanding the circumstances which seemed to necessitate these military precautions, and quite contrary to published statements of contemplated ill-treatment — for instance, that except at meal times on the voyage the deportees would be confined to their staterooms with only occasional and supervised exercise on deck, — Inspector Berkshire treated his charges humanely in every reasonable way from the beginning of the voyage to its end. And this was not without beneficial effect; at any rate it had no ill results. The deportees complied unprotestingly throughout the voyage with all the rules prescribed for their conduct. On one occasion they did organize to complain of their food. But their complaint was well founded and Inspector Berkshire so recognized it. It related to the unsatisfactory quality of their bread. Investigation showed that all on board were getting poor bread, and that this was due to the fact that the two assistant bakers upon whom all responsibility for bread-making on board had fallen in consequence of a detention of the chief baker in the ship's hospital, were incompetent. When this explanation had been made to the authorized spokesman for the deportees, Alexander Berkman, he offered to supply two bakers from the body of the deportees to take the place without pay of the incompetent assistant bakers of the ship's crew. His offer was accepted, and from that time until the "Buford" entered the Kiel Canal there was no complaint nor any disorder.

At this stage of the voyage, however, Inspector Berkshire modified the considerate policy he had until then pursued. He summarily and without provocation from them locked the deportees below decks and subjected them to military guard. That the temptation to make this change was great may be inferred from the immediate circumstances. To chance a riotous abuse of privilege out upon the ocean and the North Sea was quite a different thing from chancing such an outbreak along a narrow waterway in a German province only a year after the World War. There were therefore better reasons for suspending the prior policy while passing through the Kiel Canal than vague fears, stimulated by recollections of the delirium at home, that the deportees might "do something " to somebody if "something" were not first done to them.

In this emergency the deportees were not as considerate of the Supervising Inspector's responsibilities as he had until then been of their liberties and comfort. Instead of protesting in good temper against their temporary imprisonment below decks, they protested with a medley of discordant noises — fearsome noises to hysteric imaginations. This demonstration might have made matters worse had the deportees been subject to military discretion. But upon the mind of the competent and sane civilian who had them in his official charge, its effect was to revive and intensify his confidence in the policy he had pursued from New York to the Elbe. Recognizing a reasonableness in the objection of the deportees — however unreasonable their mode of making it — to being placed in close confinement under military guard after their exemplary conduct in freedom throughout their voyage thus far, he ordered their restoration to the liberties of the forward deck.

By substituting that treatment for the repressive one he had temporarily adopted, Inspector Berkshire checked the disorder and with it every tendency to revolt. He thereby obviated all seeming necessity for what might have led on, excusably if not justifiably, to a military assault upon the locked-in deportees. After this they made no more discordant noises, unless possibly by good-humored singing of harmless songs on the "Buford's" deck as she glided by the banks of the Kiel Canal.

Her passage through the Canal did not end the "Buford's" voyage — nor its dangers. She had still the perilous Baltic Sea to cross.

In the English Channel she had been joined by a " destroyer" which, after escorting her through the North Sea, remained with her the rest of the way. This gruesome companionship was not to protect the "Buford's" crew from riots by her deportees; nor to awe them, as some of them might reasonably have suspected. It was for the purpose of giving immediate aid in case the "Buford" were unfortunate enough to collide with a floating mine in the North Sea or one of the yet anchored mines in the Baltic.

After she had passed through the Canal, and while in Kiel Harbor for repairs, our "Soviet Ark" received orders from the War Department to cross the Baltic to Libau, although none of the War mines in the Baltic, except along the coast of Sweden, had as yet been removed. Between the rows of the rest there were roads about two miles wide, marked at intervals along their borders with buoys showing red lights at night; and along those risky sea-roads the "Ark" had yet to sail.

She weighed anchor early in the afternoon of January 13, 1920, and making her way out of Kiel Harbor headed for Libau, on the west coast of Latvia. When nearly two days at sea she was reached by wireless with orders that shifted her course to Hango, on the south coast of Finland. These altered orders were probably due to disturbed conditions in the vicinity of Libau — possibly to demands by the Latvian government to which the American State Department was reluctant to accede.

From Cuxhaven, at the North Sea end of the Canal, the "Buford" had carried a German pilot who remained with her until her arrival at Hango. He directed her course along the mine-bordered roads of the Baltic to her destination, using for guidance a German War-mine map of War-time manufacture. If all on board had known the peculiar perils they were passing through, the discipline of the ship's company might have been seriously disturbed. Amateurish indeed, in those circumstances, must the anarchistic deportees almost certainly have felt. The few officials on the "Biiford" who at that time were in the confidence of the German pilot may quite pardonably have thought of themselves as living chips in a poker game with Fate.

This phase of the voyage lasted slightly longer than three days. On the 16th of January, 1920, at five o'clock in the afternoon — four full weeks after leaving behind her the Statue of Liberty in the Harbor of New York— " the Soviet Ark" entered the Harbor of Hango in Finland. At about noon on the following day a military officer of the General Staff of Finland, accompanied by a representative of the Finnish Foreign Office, came on board.

They explained that they were prepared to take charge of the deportees and to pass them over the Russian frontier.

Immediately following their arrival, a special railway train drew up at the dock to which the deportees were conducted. And at 4 o'clock in the afternoon of January 19, 1920, — just three days after their debarkation in the Finnish Harbor of Hango — all of them were transferred by the military authorities of the Government of Finland to the military authorities of Soviet Russia, under the protection of a flag of truce.

### Chapter II: Culpability of "the Soviet Ark" Deportees

Among the 249 deportees by "the Soviet Ark"

some had at one time or another expressed opinions which most Americans justly regard as hostile to American institutions. Others were members of an association of Russian workingmen the organic law of which, in one of its clauses, contemplated the destruction of all governments. The great majority, however, were innnocent of any hostility to the United States.

The most dangerous in public estimation was Emma Goldman — unless her friend and fellow agitator, Alexander Berkman, be coupled with her in that category.

This popular estimate of Miss Goldman sprang out of a newspaper notoriety which began in 1893, when she was a young woman in her twenties, in consequence of a sensational speech she had made to striking garment-workers in New York City.

For making that speech she was convicted of "inciting to riot and unlawful assembly, " and sentenced to a year's imprisonment on Blackwell's Island.

During the next twenty-five years she was several times arrested by the police in various cities for making public speeches which they regarded as unlawful and which the newspapers exploited as menacing to public order. Apparently, however, her arrests were not followed by convictions in the courts after the first one in 1893.

Efforts to deport her by administrative process, beginning as early as 1907, were also unsuccessful for a decade. This seems to have been due at first to the fact that she was the wife of a naturalized American citizen and therefore a citizen herself. But even after her husband's citizenship had been revoked, as it was in 1908 — twenty-five years after having been granted — the pressure to deport her remained ineffective until her conviction in 1917 for conspiring to obstruct the selectivedraft law when our country was entering the World War.

Although her record as an objectionable agitator had been conspicuous for a generation, little or nothing was known to the public about Emma Goldman's everyday life. Her acquaintances, however, spoke of her as a trained nurse from early womanhood and as active in that service, though often unidentified by her patients because she found it necessary, owing to her newspaper notoriety, to practice her profession under an unfamiliar name. It was for the same reason, probably, that she used an alias when managing "the Russian Players"

during their notable visit to the United States in the early years of the present century.

The first official act looking to Miss Goldman's deportation after her husband's citizenship had been revoked, was taken in consequence of her sentence in 1917 for violation of the selective-draft law. Nearly two years later, when this sentence was about to expire, new deportation proceedings were instituted, and for final decision her case came before me as Assistant Secretary of Labor. She rested her residential rights upon a claim to American citizenship in virtue of her husband's naturalization, a defense that would have been conclusive against her banishment but for the revocation by judicial proceedings of her husband's naturalization nearly ten years before. For that reason the only question to be decided with reference to her citizenship was whether the revocation of his could operate as a revocation of hers, especially as she had not been made a party in court to the revocation proceedings. I decided that the revocation of her husband's citizenship, upon which hers depended, operated automatically to subject her to the disabilities of an alien. If I erred, my decision was jurisdictional and would have been reviewed by the courts in habeas corpus proceedings. But Miss Goldman did not take her case to the courts.

There was only one other question. Had she been proved to be an anarchist within the intent of the Act of Congress of October 16, 1918? That Miss Goldman was an anarchist she confessed. But there are various kinds. At one extreme are malignant conspirators and destructive revolutionists; at the other are apostles of peace, preachers of the principle of non-resistance, of " turning the other cheek", persons supremely harmless except to those perverted imaginations which anticipate violent revolutions as consequences of non-resistant propaganda. Considering philosophical anarchism as an extreme intellectual or emotional reaction from the inequalities of economic opportunity which governments conserve, and violent anarchism as the extreme militant reaction against those inequalities —somewhat as in other connections one might contrast Quakers with Koundheads, or John Greenleaf Whittier with "Ossawatomie" Brown — Emma Goldman may probably be placed somewhere between the two extremes.

Perhaps I ought not to venture this or any other characterization of Miss Goldman, for I have very little acquaintance with her propaganda.

Eigorously opposed to it as I am from what I have understood of it, I might almost fairly be accused of indulging a tendency of mind akin to that which Lowell attributed to "Parson Wilbur" with reference to William Lloyd Garrison's anti-slavery writings in "The Liberator, " to which that mythical parson referred as a print whose heresies he took every opportunity of combatting and of which he thanked God he had never read a single line.

Some, however, of Miss Goldman's declarations I have read. I had to do so while considering her ease in deportation proceedings. And from such as I have read I venture to infer that she is one of those disbelievers in violence who anticipate forcible resistance to peaceable developments of anarchistic policies, and therefore and in support of those policies justify the use of force as a defense against force.

But I did not have to decide officially what kind of anarchist Emma Goldman was, for all differences between the widely divergent varieties of anarchism, from terroristic to pacific, had been ignored by the Acts of Congress by which I was bound. The sole question before me was whether or not she believed that no government would be better for human society than any kind of government. If she did, she was an anarchist. And if she were an anarchist her deportation (she being an alien) was mandatorily required by the law. As the record of her hearing showed her to be an anarchist, even though it did not disclose her kind of anarchism definitely, I made an administrative decision in behalf of the Secretary of Labor, whose official alter ego I happened at the moment to be, which ordered the Commissioner General of Immigration to deport her.

No question of sympathy on the one hand nor of antipathy on the other was involved. Whether or not I liked the law did not enter in. I was not a maker of laws but an administrator of a law already constitutionally made. To administer it fairly and effectively, though humanely, was my only function. And this law was mandatory. I had no choice but to measure by it the facts presented in the record of hearing. In any case in which the recorded facts brought the alien within the manifest intent of the law, my refusal to execute its requirements would have been in violation of my oath of office, treacherous to the Government which I administratively represented, and essentially repugnant to the developing democratic principles of our Republic. Similarly perjured, equally treacherous, and quite as undemocratic should I have been had I in any case decided to order deportation without evidence reasonably convincing that the alien was lawfully deportable.

In making the decision which ordered Emma Goldman's deportation, I directed the Commissioner General of Immigration, as the alien-expulsion laws required, to deport her to the country whence she had come. That country was understood to be Russia. But Russia was then dominated in some regions by the Bolshevist party of the Soviet government, commonly known in the United States as "Beds," and in other regions by Czaristic reactionaries, commonly known as "Whites." Inasmuch, then, as the deportation of resident aliens from the United States is in law not a punishment for crime, even in cases in which the cause for deportation may be a crime, — else the deportee, though an alien, would be entitled to a judicial instead of a mere administrative trial and judgment, — but is only for the purpose of terminating the residence in this country of classes of visitors who are catalogued by Congress as undesirable, and inasmuch also as it is no part of any lawful or decent function of American officials to connive at persecution in order to gratify any person or to promote any interest, I made what I regarded as a reasonable reservation. My reservation provided that the place to which Miss Goldman must be deported would be determined later by the Department of Labor upon a specific recommendation from the Bureau of Immigration.

About three weeks before the sailing of the "Buford," the Commissioner General of Immigration made such a recommendation in comprehensive plans for deportations to Russia. His plans, which doubtless contemplated the subsequent " Buford " experiment, seemed to meet the requirements of my reservation in the Goldman case.

They had the appearance of being well designed to prevent the delivery of our Russian deportees to probable persecution by reactionary military authorities, and yet to secure their return to their home country, not as convicted criminals, which they were not, but as visitors among us who had worn out their welcome.

So I directed specifically, in conformity with my previous more general reservation, that Emma Goldman be deported to Soviet Russia — to "Red Russia " as that part of Russia was then commonly called, — and not to "White Russia, " where at that time she would probably have fared worse than a "White" spy in a "Red" camp.

The other "Buford" deportee who was heralded along with Emma Goldman as extremely dangerous, Alexander Berkman, had lived in the United States since 1888, having arrived while he was still a youth. In 1892 he attempted to murder Henry Clay Frick, a distinguished American "captain of industry," as men of his type and business accomplishments were at that time called. Berkman committed this crime as an anarchistic " propagandist by deed." His victim was a millionaire leader of steel manufacturers organized in the Carnegie interest. The bitter industrial conflict notable as "the Homestead strike" was on. Denouncing Frick as a tyrant, Berkman appears to have made the deadly assault upon him in the spirit in which Charlotte Corday made hers upon Marat, but without achieving a like result or suffering a like penalty. His pistol-shot wounded but did not kill. Consequently, instead of being hanged for his crime, as he declared he had expected to be, Berkman served a term in a Pennsylvania penitentiary.

Sentenced for twenty-one years, he was paroled at the end of fourteen for good behavior as a prisoner. After his release, the police occasionally subjected him to arrest for public speeches, and upon one of these arrests he was committed to a workhouse for disorderly conduct.

Berkman had no further criminal record until his conviction, along with Emma Groldman in 1917, for obstructing the selective-draft Act upon the entry of this country into the World War. But neither his conviction and sentence for his old-time murderous assault nor for his violation of the temporary War-statute figured at his deportation hearing. The only legal reason for Berkman *s deportation was his belief in " anarchism, " that is, in the abolition of governmental force. For this reason, and this alone, he became one of the involuntary passengers on "the Soviet Ark."

His variety of anarchism as described by himsel — and there was no other evidence on that point in the record, — was called out from him by Immigrant Inspector Eugene Kessler, at the Federal penitentiary in Georgia, in the course of a singularly philosophical and acute cross-examination.

Answering one of the Inspector's questions, Berkman said that as an anarchist he believed neither in government nor in violence, terms which he regarded as synonymous, arguing that the World War was "a demonstration of the principle that government is founded on violence, " which was his reason for opposing it. "What the League of Nations tries to accomplish for the future," he added, "I advocated thirty-five years ago — the abolition of war." Following those introductory observations, Berkman made a formal definition of anarchism as he accepted it, saying that it is "the philosophy of a new social order based on liberty unrestricted by man-made law, the theory that all forms of government rest on violence and are therefore wrong and harmful as well as mercenary." In the Berhnan case as in Miss Goldman's, it happened that I made the decision. His case, like hers, had been pending ever since these two aliens were sentenced to imprisonment for their War offenses of more than two years earlier. Along with the application for a warrant of arrest for Miss Goldman — which I refused to issue because it was not accompanied with proof of her alienage — there had come a like application for a warrant for Berkman. His alienage and his culpability under the statute were both shown by accompanying proof, and I issued a warrant for his arrest. This was in July, 1917, just after he and Miss Goldman were sentenced to imprisonment and fine upon conviction in the Federal court at New York of having violated a War law. Before the expiration of their prison sentences, however, a more stringent alienexpulsion law than the Act of 1917 was enacted by Congress, and under this, Secretary Wilson ordered new warrants for the arrest of both.

The subsequent proceedings in Miss Goldman's case I have already told. Of the proceedings against Berkman there is nothing more to tell than that in his case, which came to me for decision nearly a month before the "Buford" sailed, I decided upon the evidence before me to direct his deportation to Bussia, the country from which he had come, and that I did so with the reservation I had made in the Goldman case, that he must not be deported to any part of Russia dominated by the "White Guards."

Among the other deportees of the anarchist class who sailed on the so-called "Soviet Ark" were two young women. They were characterized in one of the newspaper descriptions of the "Bnford" embarkation as "female terrorists who must leave America because of their plotting against the Government. " But the records of their deportation-hearings do not show any "terroristic" activities or purposes on the part of either. One of them, the younger, was Ethel Bernstein. The other was Dora Lipkin. As they asserted that they were anarchists, and admitted membership in an organization the organic law of which brought its alien members within the proscription of Congress, they were mandatorily deportable on two grounds. But in neither case was there any proof of terroristic purposes, propensities or beliefs.

Besides Dora Lipkin, Ethel Bernstein, Alexander Berkman and Emma Goldman, there were 47 passengers by "the Soviet Ark" whose deportation had been directed by the Department of Labor upon proof of their having made "no-government"

utterances or entertained "no-government" beliefs. This proof alone made their deportation mandatory. The name of the proscribed organization in which Dora Lipkin admitted membership was "The Federation of Unions of Russian Workers of the United States and Canada"; and for mere membership in this organization, no less than 184 out of the 249 "Buford" deportees were deported.

It appears to have been organized in 1911 by Russians then residing in the United States, whose purpose was to promote the overthrow of the government of the Russian Czar. Its first convention was held in Detroit in 1914. When raids were made upon its schools and club-rooms in 1919, it had about 7,000 members. There were branches in many parts of the United States. For the arrest of its members about 600 warrants were issued by the Department of Labor. Of these, 452 were executed by the arrest of aliens supposed to be members of the organization, and a total of 246 of those arrested were deported.

The clauses in the organic law of that Federation which subjected its alien members to banishment from this country were two. One was to the effect that "the struggle between" the working and the propertied classes " continues at the present time, and will be finished when the toiling masses organize as a class" and, understanding their true interest, "take possession by forcible social revolution of all the wealth of the world." The other was in the nature of a proclamation that the members, "organizing themselves into unions of Russian workers," would, "as a part of the workers of the world," strive to promote "the principles laid" down "as the foundation of the Federation," together with "the work of organizing the great mass of Russian immigrants for the speedier liberation of Russia and all mankind." This clause was followed by one to the effect that "having accomplished such an overthrow," that is, "having destroyed at the same time all institutions of state and authority, the class of the disinherited will have to proclaim a society of free producers aiming to satisfy the needs of each individual person, who in turn will give to society his labor and his knowledge."

Some of the 184 "Buford" deportees who were deported only for membership in this Federation must have been well aware of its revolutionary policies. Conspicuous among them was Peter Bianky, a Russian of twenty-seven, with a family in New York consisting of his wife and their little son. Bianky had come to the United States at the age of twenty-two, while the Czar's government yet held sway over his native country; and at the time of his deportation he was the secretary of the Federation and had been for nearly a year. He was also editor of the Federation's organ, " Bread and Freedom." His knowledge, therefore, of the revolutionary policies of this Russian society was a reasonable inference. There could, indeed, be no doubt of the fact, quite apart from any inference, for he testified that he knew the principles of the organization and believed in them. The inference alone, however, would have been enough to justify his deportation, even if the expulsion law had required proof of guilty knowledge. But neither inference nor proof was necessary on that point. Membership alone made the case against him complete. And so of the whole group of 184 members of that organization in the total of 249 "Buford" passengers.

Of an enormous proportion of the 184, however, it is a reasonable probability that they were totally ignorant of the objectionable clauses in the organic law of their organization. And this probability is confirmed by parts of the testimony of its secretary. Asked by the examining Inspector if the Federation admitted members without their knowing the principles of the organization, he replied: "Yes; we accept them upon the statement of two other members that the applicant is an honest worker and lives by Ms own labor. " Referring then to those declared purposes of the organization which subjected alien members to deportation for merely being members, the examining Inspector asked: "Does he sign this statement 1" "No," replied the secretary; "we never swear them and they don't sign. If he wishes, he can familiarize himself with the principles later on." Aliens coming into a proscribed organization in that way, with no knowledge of its revolutionary purpose, supposing it to be a legitimate labor union or social club of their fellow countrymen in a foreign land, must by the law be held responsible to the extreme for their indiscretion; and in this instance probably no less than 175 such persons, out of the total of 249 deportees by "the Soviet Ark" — about two-thirds — were held to the full measure of that responsibility. That any of these were dangerous conspirators would in no wise be a rational inference from the evidence in their cases.

In that group of innocent "members" of the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers was a young Russian who was in fact not a member. He had lived six years in this country, earning his way by hard work — useful and not over-waged. Eager for education, but precluded by his variable working-hours from attendance at public night-school, he looked about for a substitute school and found it at the headquarters of the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers in New York City known as "the People's House." While he was working at his mathematics one night at that school, "the People's House" was raided — under the circumstances narrated in the next Chapter — and this young man was "run in" along with his fellow pupils and their teachers.

At his deportation-hearing there was a total lack of evidence against him. He denied being an anarchist of any kind, and there was not so much as a pretense of proof to the contrary. He denied being a member of any organization except a Methodist church in New York in which he was active, and there was no counterproof. His statement in this particular was fully corroborated after his deportation. At his hearing he proved affirmatively as Well as a prisoner prevented from communicating with his friends, as he was, could be expected to prove, that ever since his arrival in this country he had been a law-abiding resident. But he was one of "the Soviet Ark's" involuntary passengers.

The "Red" deportees on board "the Soviet Ark, ' J of which that young man was one, numbered 235 instead of 249; for 14 of the 249 were deported for residential defects wholly unrelated to "anarchism", "bolshevism", "sovietism" or any other kind of "radicalism". They were types of "undesirable aliens" whose residence here becomes objectionable from quite commonplace infirmities. It may be useful, therefore, to summarize the official statements which classified all the deportees by the "Buford" with reference to the reasons for their deportation respectively. In the summary of 26 THE DEPORTATIONS DELIRIUM totals that follows here, the classifications of the official report are preserved:

Anarchists (persons who do not believe in any form of government) 51 Members of an organization which teaches the overthrow of government by force (Federation of Unions of Russian Workers)... 184 Total " radical" deportees by "Buford". 235 Aliens convicted of crime involving moral turpitude 3 xlliens likely to become public charges at the time of arrival in this country 9 Alien procurer 1 Alien who had entered this country surreptitiously 1 Total deportees by "Buford" 249

### Chapter III: Deportation Raids in Anticipation of the Voyage of "the Soviet Ark"

Those of the "Buford" deportees whose banishment was for membership in the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers had been arrested in raids upon meetings of that organization which they were attending. The raids were made at night and simultaneously in eleven cities. They had been staged by the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice to be "pulled off" at approximately the same hour in each city. The date set was November 7, 1919.

In the night of that day, accordingly, and under the general management of the same detective auxiliary, the local police of the several selected cities "rounded up" all persons found at Russian Workers' meetings regardless of every consideration except their presence there. Officials of the organization, professional teachers peaceably leading their night-school pupils in legitimate studies, members of the organization and non-members, men and women, aliens and citizens, were indiscriminately and in many instances brutally swept into custody.

As these raids were alike in purpose and execution, a description of the principal one will sufficiently serve for all. It was made in New York City. Its objective was the Russian.."People's House, " a four-story brown-stone building at No. 133 East 15th street — the headquarters in New York of the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers.

The same "People's House" had been recently raided under the direction of the "Lusk Committee " of the New York legislature. On that occasion, according to the New York World of November 8, 1919, "a large number of prisoners were taken, " and "an even larger quantity of literature V was seized than at the later raid about to be described; yet the "Lusk-Committee raid was without result " as "to prosecutions or convictions." The impulse of both raids appears to have sprung out of those mysterious indications of a bombing conspiracy which had startled the country in May and June of the same year and of which my next Chapter will tell. Alluding to one of the results of that conspiracy, the New York Times of November 8, 1919, put into its report of the November raid at the "People's House" this explanation: "Since the bomb outrages last June which led to the appointment of Flynn" — the chief of the new detective auxiliary to the Attorney GeneraPs department in the Federal government and a noted private detective — "to take charge of the work of ferreting out anarchy, the police have been watching and cataloguing every act and every utterance of radicals in this city, even the socalle i harmless bolsheviki.' It is hoped that sooner or later one of these raids will yield clues to the perpetrators of the bomb outrages. "

But that hope was ulterior. The immediate object of the November raids was to arrest "red" aliens for deportation. Yet there went with the immediate object an incidental design to terrify Russian residents of the wage-working class whose memories of the Czar's government had impelled them to welcome the soviet regime in Russia.

"Choosing the second anniversary of the Bolshevist revolution in Russia as the psychological moment to strike," — so the same New York paper explained in the same report of the November raid in New York — "the Federal government, aided by municipal police in New York and several other large cities, last night dealt the most serious and sweeping blow it has yet aimed at criminal anarchists. Armed with warrants for dangerous agitators whom Federal agents have trailed for months, the raiders swarmed into the Russian People's House." The hour was nine at night The raid was managed by a sergeant of the New York police — the sergeant of "the bomb squad." He had been co-operating in that city with Federal officials in "the effort to root out the anarchists known to be in New York"; but neither he nor any of his men would discuss the raid they had made, all of them asserting that they were silent by order of the chief of the Federal detectives who had recently been appointed "with orders to leave no stone unturned to end all symptoms of anarchy in the United States."

When the "People's House had been completely surrounded " by the raiders, so the Times report continued, "the men in charge of each section of the raiding party ordered their men to the ground and then went forward." The inmates "had not the slightest idea of what was coming," and the raiders "had penetrated the Russian People's House from top to bottom" before there was any alarm.

On "one of the floors a class was in progress," I still quote from the Times. "Several of those in this class said afterwards that they were merely studying English when the police arrived.

" 'Out into the hall, everybody!' was the terse order.

" 'Line up there, and don't make any noise!'

"At first there" were indications "that the presence of the policemen would be" challenged, "some of the women being especially vociferous in their demands to know the meaning of the raid;" but "the harsh command to 'shut up there, you, if you know what's good for you!' brought silence." The inmates of the building were then roughly "hustled into waiting automobiles and driven away. ' '

That several of the victims of this ruffianly raid suffered physical injury without having furnished any excuse for it, was quite evident. Marks of "roughneck" discipline were upon some of them when they were brought to the Immigrant Station at Ellis Island. One of the more notable sufferers whose condition was informally reported to me by a trustworthy official of the Bureau of Immigration, was a mild-mannered school-teacher whose legitimate inquiry when " rounded up" with his pupils, had been answered with blows that smashed the spectacles he wore and wounded his face severely. Newspaper reporters noticed the outrages and the lack of excuse for them. "A number of those in the building," wrote the Times reporter, "were b^dly beaten by the police during the raid, their heads wrapped in bandages testifying to the rough manner in which they had been handled."

Another reporter, representing the World, observed that "the officers had no difficulty in obtaining admission to the house," and that although "a good deal of noise attended the raid," there was nevertheless "no real opposition to the officers" so far as "those on the outside could determine." This reporter added that "there seemed to be little real basis for the reports that it had been necessary to use force in any instance."

His impression was afterwards amply confirmed. There was a total lack of proof of resistance by the inmates. And though there were categorical denials by the aggressors of any injuries to their captives, these were made in the face of the manifest fact of injuries otherwise unaccounted for.

Moreover, circumstantial statements by captives were not disputed circumstantially. And over all, •there were marked indications that the raid was intended to be terrifying.

v> For "nearly an hour after the raid," said the World of November 8, "men were carrying" from the People's House in Fifteenth Street, "books, pamphlets and other literature which were loaded" upon "wagons and taken to the offices" on Park Eow, the local headquarters of the detective auxiliary to the Department of Justice.

" Several tons" of this literature, said the Times of the same date, "filling several huge trucks" were carried away, "all of it said to be anarchist literature."

If in all that wholesale seizure there was aught which could lawfully have subjected any of the inmates of the Russian "People's House" in New York to charges of conspiring against the government of the United States, or of any other kind of crime, it must have been lost in the confusion of the seizure or in transmission to the Department of Labor, for none got into the record.

If the inmates of that Russian Workers' headquarters on that eventful night were indeed incendiary conspirators against our country, the raid was ill-calculated to discover incriminating facts. Its results, if it were well-conceived, tend to prove the innocence of its victims. Though the raiders went " systematically through the building in search of incriminating literature of any kind," as the Times reported on the Bth, though "doors were taken off," though "desks were ripped open,"

though "even the few carpets were taken up," all "to find possible hiding places for documents," nothing suspicious was found.

That the raiding officials themselves did not regard their captives as probable criminals, and that their superiors who directed the raid had not expected to find criminals in the raided house, must be inferred from the fact, reported by the Times of the next day, that immediately after the raid all the captives "who were able to prove their citizenship" were unconditionally released. Is a dangerous criminal any less criminal, or any less dangerous, for being an American citizen! Is an alien any more so for being an alien? Certainly not. Then why release the raid-caught citizen and hold the raid-caught alien, the evidence against them being in every respect except alienage precisely the same?

Was it for lack of appropriate penal laws?

There was no such lack, either of State laws or of Federal. The explanation could not possibly have related to degrees of criminality or of danger to American institutions. It must have had another bearing. Possibly this was its bearing: Whereas a citizen cannot be punished without substantial cause and after conviction at a judicial trial, an alien may be banished for frivolous causes and by autocratic "administrative process. "

That raiding in New York City was fairly typical of all the concerted raids in different cities that were made simultaneously under central control upon meetings of the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers on the 7th of November, 1919.

There may have been less police ruffianism in some of the cities than in New York; but the raids were everywhere conducted in similar defiance of American pre-War ideals of law and order.

Inasmuch as those November raids of 1919, and also the equally lawless and even more ruthless raids of the following January yet to be described, were supposed to have got their impulse from a bombing conspiracy of the previous summer, the sequence of my story requires at this stage a glance at the circumstances of that bombing crime.

### Chapter IV: The Bombs That Begot the Deportations Delirium

The newspapers of early May, 1919, blazed with accounts of an interception in the mails at New York of sixteen explosive packages. Subsequent investigation showed that these packages had been prepared with deadly and malicious skill. Excitement over this discovery was the more intense because "the first of May" had long been a stock subject for prophecies of evil doings among or in behalf of wage-earning classes. That date, the European Labor-day, had been adopted by European terrorists for hinting at Labor uprisings and for an occasional assassination. In the tEuropean imagination, consequently, ' i May-day ' 9 became an anniversary to be anticipated with fear. And this trans-Atlantic nervousness had spread until annual rumors of " May-day " outrages disturbed American imaginations also; though for many years no "May-day" outrages occurred in the United States to verify the annual rumors. In 1919, however, there appeared to be confirmation in the discovery in the mails of those explosive packages just in time for a "May-day" sensation. More than two dozen such packages were gathered from street mailing-boxes into the General Post Office at New York in the regular course of mail collections. Sixteen of them were stopped there by a vigilant postal clerk. This was not, however, from any suspicion of their dangerous character; it was because the clerk detected a shortage of postage. He sent them to the Short Payment Division in the basement of the Post Office building, and there they remained until their destructive possibilities came by accident to be suspected. They were still there on the 29th of April late at night when this postal clerk left his desk for home. While on his way near midnight he read in a morning paper of the 30th an account of the bursting of a mail-received bomb in Georgia. The reported circumstances excited in him a suspicion of the packages he had held for shortpostage, and he returned at once to the Post Office.

Eeporting there his fears to the Night Superintendent the latter called in the Chief Postal Inspector who segregated the packages. Later on the 30th of April one of them was minutely inspected by the Chief Inspector of the Bureau of Combustibles of the Fire Department of the city and his Assistant, and they discovered and reported the dangerous character of the packages.

Besides those explosive packages, so fortunately detected, several like them had already gone out of the New York Post Office to their respective destinations. One of these, the very one about the bursting of which the postal clerk read in a newspaper on his way home on the 30th of April and was thereby inspired to cause the investigation which probably prevented several catastrophes, went to ex-Senator Hardwick, afterwards Governor of Georgia, whose term as a United States Senator and Chairman of the Senate Committee on Immigration had but recently expired. This package was delivered at Senator Hardwick 's Georgia home. Upon being opened by a colored maid, it exploded and blew off both her hands, severely injuring Mrs. Hardwick also who was standing near. In consequence of this catastrophe and the thoughtful act of the New York postal clerk upon learning of it, the postal authorities everywhere were warned. The warning was effective. Except at the Hardwick home no one was injured by any of those two dozen or more postal bombs.

With reports of the timely frustration of this death-dealing plot, except in the one sad instance of the maid at Senator Hardwick 's home, the newspapers were filled. Whatever the event in itself lacked for exciting horror and fear, they supplied abundantly in daily reports of detective operations. The entire "bomb squad" of the New York City police, the bomb experts of the Fire Department, postal inspectors, private detectives, detectives of the Department of Justice, all were "sleeplessly" at work on the case. Every day there was a multiplicity of "leads," hither and yon, each giving promise of "results" that were never realized. Arrests were "imminent," but none were made except arbitrarily and for "third degree" purposes, the victims being persons of few friends and these without influence. There were "round-ups" that made startling headlines, but at the " showdowns " there was " nothing doing."

One nerve-thrilling discovery pointed to a mythical " Committee of Five" as the "most desperate band of anarchists ever organized in the United States" — according to an "undercover man" in the employment of the Federal Government. One detective who at first was thoughtful enough to suspect that the bombs had been sent by some irresponsible crank, quickly came to the orthodox police-conclusion that "the whole thing was a gigantic conspiracy by an organized gang." And so the hue and cry clattered on, with every now and again a police assurance to the reporters that "the net tightens" or "the lightning is about to strike." But so much tightening of nets and no fish, and so much thunder with no lightning, made the public weary. The sensation had not been dramatic enough in its staging for a lasting scare, and those futile detective tactics hurried it on to an anti-climax. Before the month of May had run much more than half its course the sensation was over and detective chatter about the postal bombs had lost its headline value.

Hardly, however, had that "May-day" terror subsided when the public was terrorized anew by the June bombardments. From no less than seven cities there came reports on the 3d of June, 1919, of bombing assaults upon residences the night before. From Philadelphia two explosions were reported, one in the rectory of a Roman Catholic church and the other at the residence of a business man. In Paterson the reported object of attack was the residence of a silk manufacturer. In Boston it was the residence of a judge — that of a Congressman in one of the suburbs being also noted in the list. From Pittsburgh six explosions were reported, one of which damaged the residence of a Federal judge. At Cleveland the reported explosion was at the home of the Mayor. By none of those explosions was anyone injured. But in New York, where the residence of Judge Charles C.

Nott was partly wrecked, a watchman in front of the house at the time of the explosion was killed by it.

The most sensational explosion of them all was at Washington. It damaged the front of Attorney General Palmer's residence. Nobody was physically injured by it, however, except the wretch who is supposed to have been placing the bomb; and of him, if in truth they were his, there were found only unrecognizable bits of flesh and shreds of clothing.

Public excitement revived and detectives kept it boiling. The newspapers were again ablaze with police reports of rapid progress in solving the diabolical mystery. Sleuths were again hot upon the trail. The " ablest " men in their line were working night and day upon the theory that the explosions 6f June were related to and were a part of the postal bomb-conspiracy of May. The clews to each were shrewdly expected to intertwine, so that the facts ascertained in running down the one plot would be of service in running down the other. Arrests were made quite freely, but no charges were preferred. A New York deputy police commissioner declared that "he would have the culprits in custody in forty-eight hours. " Agitations began for laws to deport all alien " radicals, " to curb American agitators, and to punish persons who were active in "stirring up discontent. "

A noted private detective had been promptly appointed by Attorney General Palmer as Chief of an Information Division in the Department^of Justice. This Division became at once a news-distributing center and a secret-police plexus. The police of the whole country were sensitized, and the bomb-throwers were hourly on the verge of capture. A confusing hue and cry which surcharged the air, stirred the public mind with fear and hate. But panics are like wild beasts in more senses than one. Unless they have something to feed on they die; and for lack of substantial sustenance the excitement over the June explosions, like that of May over the explosive postal packages, began by the middle of the month to languish.

Then a third horror was sighted in the offing. The " radicals " who were responsible for the June explosions and the May-day postal packages were discovered by look-out detectives to be about " launching " a campaign of terrorism to begin on the approaching Fourth of July. Possibly the bomb operators, shocked by the fate of their " comrade " who was supposed to have been annihilated while bombing the Attorney General's residence in Washington, were too timid to play again in the role assigned them. Possibly the detectives were able to stop the terroristic " launching." At any rate, no campaign of terror was " launched" on the Fourth of July next following the explosions of June.

As to the origin of those May and June events, there were conflicting guesses. Detectives and their employers saw in the crimes indications of nothing but a gigantic conspiracy of enemies of law and order organized to destroy the American government and to abolish the institution of private property at a swoop. At the other explanatory extreme were "radical," "socialistic" and "freethinking" persons, some of whom were reported as asserting that "the bombs were the work of an agent provocateur employed by a private organization." An unusual though in its general bearing not wholly improbable surmise was ventured by the postal clerk whose vigilance had frustrated the bomb conspiracy of May. His surmise is especially valuable because his opportuities for observation bad been exceptional, his veracity was manifest, and he had neither the incentive of the detective nor the impulse of a faddist to spring at seductive conclusions. This young man of twentyseven, who had come to the United States from Russia when a boy of seven, referred to himself in the New York American of May 1, 1919, as having "had a little better luck than most Russians," and continued: "During the twenty years that I've been living among these wretched poor and starving ignorant people, I've had my eyes opened. They don't want to do anything wrong, but what can they do? I don't condone the things these fanatics did. Nobody in his right mind would. But I couldn't say what I myself would do if I were desperate." He had preceded those explanations with this statement: " There's too much unrest and too much suffering in living conditions nowadays to keep out of ear-shot of anarchistic talk.

I've heard it in the jammed and sweltering subways to and from my home every day for months. And each day it is more widespread. Of course, there doesn't seem to be much way of getting at the fanatics who did this" — the mailing of the May-day postal bombs — u and if they could be found, what good would that do? When you look at it sanely you know that no possible good could come from acts like that. The fanatics want to kill men like Morgan and Rockefeller because they are rich and the fanatics themselves are starving. But they don't stop to think that men like that never trouble to open their own mail, and some helpless servant or underling is the victim — some one of their own class who, may be, is skimping and starving along on as little money as the anarchists. Men who see their children in rags and starving, and their wives slaving and starving, don't reason things out, though. I know because I've lived among them all my life, even though I've been a little luckier than millions of them. They get mad like starving beasts. And a starving beast has just one passion— to kill. Men want to kill the men who are grinding them down to misery, and they go about it as madly as beasts. "

But that young man's inference, while suggestive of industrial conditions that could inspire such venomous crimes as the one he detected in May, 1919, and the more disastrous one of June, may not be much nearer to a correct explanation than the surmises of "radicals," that detectives " planted " the bombs, or those of detectives, that they were demonstrations of a vast conspiracy of revolutionaries. No comparison is possible, however, for no discoveries have been made.

This lack of real "leads" is remarkable — remarkable enough to be almost significant. How was it possible for so gigantic a conspiracy of revolutionaries, if that is what it was, or so desperate an outburst of proletarian passion, if it was that, to have escaped detection when most of the detective agencies of the country, public and private, regardless of expense and frequently of lawful methods, were pursuing the perpetrators of its crimes with tireless zeal? There is nothing comparable to this enigma except the fruitless efforts of the detectives to fix responsibility for the disastrous explosion in Wall Street, New York, which occurred at mid-day more than a year after the two criminal episodes of May and June, 1919.

Perhaps the most considerate explanation would attribute these failures of the detectives to their fixed mental attitude. Instead of approaching the bomb mysteries with minds set upon solving them regardless of consequences, they were predetermined to seek a particular origin. No clue interested them unless it promised to lead back to an anarchistic conspiracy.

The newspaper reports of that time, dealing as they did so liberally with detective activities, theories, suspicions, surmises, predictions, fake discoveries, agilities and failures calmly described as triumphs of the detective art, recall Mark Twain's comic fable of "The Stolen White Elephant. " On its way from India to England by way of New York, the white elephant of Mark's story was temporarily quartered in Jersey City pending resumption of its voyage. There it got spirited away, mysteriously stolen — an animal 19 feet high, 48 feet long from trunk-tip to tail-tip, dull white of color, and as it walked making a footprint in the ground like the mark of a barrel-end in snow.

Upon notification and offer of reward by the elephant's keeper, the entire detective outfit of New York, uniformed in plain clothes, was put upon the stolen elephant's trail with strict injunctions of secrecy. Then the reporters were called in by the detective chief, and the next day the newspapers were full of the story. Meanwhile the detectives got on the trail of the elephant. One found a clue in upper New York State, another in New Jersey, another on Long Island; and so on and so on came the clues from New York and New Jersey and Pennsylvania and Delaware and Virginia and Michigan. The papers were again and again burdened with detective theories, as new and ever new and never failing clues were discovered. But the never failing clues always failed. At last, however, the stolen white elephant was accidently found in the basement of police headquarters. It was dead — and worse. Had Mark Twain been living and had his absurd story of the stolen white elephant not been published before, one might easily have mistaken it for a carefully studied satire upon the detective operations for uncovering the mystery of the Wall-street explosion of 1920 and that of the bombs of May and June, 1919.

Only one conclusion, not a flattering one, seems possible when the facilities of the detectives of the Department of Justice are considered. They are referred to in the official report of the Attorney General for 1920, where he describes a card index in his Department as "containing over 200,000 cards giving detailed data not only upon individual agitators connected with the ultra-radical movement, but also upon organizations, associations, societies, publications and special conditions existing in certain localities." The work upon this card index, so the official report proceeds, "is carried on by assistants especially trained for the purpose and who are well informed upon the general movements in the territory over which they have supervision. M And while this work "was at first confined solely to the investigations of the ultra-radical movement, it has now [1920] expanded to cover more general intelligence work, including not only ultra-radical activities but also to the study of matters of an international nature as well as economic and industrial disturbances incident thereto."

Consider the relation of that statement to results accomplished. When it is considered that notwithstanding all the kaisaristic police ig£ehanisin which^-i^^describes and implies, no disclosure whatever has yet been made of any responsibility for the so-called "anarchistic" or " ultra-radical " bombing of May and June, 1919, nor for the Wallstreet explosion of 1920 — when that statement and that fact are contrasted, what inference is possible, in all reason, except that the crimes were not of " ultra-radical" origin, or else that the detectives were grossly inefficient?

But the origin and significance of the three bomb episodes are of less importance to my narrative than the psychological effect of the two that occurred respectively in May and June, 1919.
