Whether those crimes originated with revolutionary conspirators, with provocative agents among detectives, or with crazy cranks, and whether the detectives craftily evaded discovering the perpetrators or were grossly inefficient, the psychological effects were identical.

It was humanly natural that Attorney^ General Pahper should have been nervously disturbed by the June explosion in front of his residence. Powerful enough to annihilate the supposed perpetrator, that explosion would have wrecked the building and slain its inmates had the explosive material been better placed for its apparent purpose. Consciousness of an escape so narrow, supplemented with assurances of trusted detectives that "radical" agitators were promoting these crimes on a vast scale, could over-excite even less dramatic men in Mr. Palmer's place. It is not surprising, then, that he begged of Congress an enormous appropriation of money for rooting out the conspiracy with which detectives thrilled his imagination but of which they have been utterly unable to produce substantial proof.

The "red" crusade that Attorney General Palmer consequently organized has been attributed to his political ambition. That this object was extensively and intensively served by the crusade is doubtless true, for popular frenzy over "red" conspiracies rose at one time to such a pitch that there was much political capital in a reputation for trying to smash them; and that Mr. Palmer's supporters for the Presidential nomination of 1920 ignored this advantage in their campaigning is not likely. But though the " red " crusade served political ambitions — and many a politician of both parties besides Mr. Palmer's cohorts made it do so — it probably got less impulse from the Attorney General's political aspirations than from the nervous state into which he must have been precipitated by the bombing of his home.

Nervous apprehension would most readily account for his becoming that easy dupe of detectives which for a time he seemed to be. His weakened judgment in weighing their reports showed at the very outset. Making an appeal for appropriations before a committee of Congress on the 12th of June, 1919, he climaxed with a statement which, childish as it now appears, doubtless seemed true to him when he made it. Although he seemed, as the Baltimore Sun put it, to scent "a bolshevist plot in every item of the day's news," he really depended for his facts upon detectives who knew exactly what they really wanted. So inspired and evidently excited by the terrifying circumstances of the explosion at his house, he felt driven to say: "We have received so many notices and gotten so much information that it has almost come to be accepted as a fact that on a certain day in the future, which we have been advised of, there will be another serious and probably much larger effort of the same character which the wild fellows of this movement describe as a revolution, a proposition to rise up and destroy the Government at one fell swoop." The nerve-racked significance of that climax, made by a responsible Cabinet officer to a responsible committee of Congress, in no spirit of levity but as a solemn disclosure of a really formidable conspiracy to " seize the moon by the teeth," sufficiently accounts for the Attorney General's amazing management of the "red" crusade.

Although Cdngress did not at once make as large an appropriation as the Attorney General asked for, it made one in June and another in December which aggregated more. With these funds at command, the private detective whom the Attorney General had already selected to manage the esoteric activities of the "red" crusade — his name being William J. Flynn — a man whom at an appropriations hearing he described as "the greatest anarchist-expert in the United States," one who " knows all the men of that class" and "can pretty nearly call them by name," turned the Department of Justice into an agency for stimulating the popular delirium which the postal bombs of the preceding May and the explosions of June had generated.

### Chapter V: Preparations for the "Red" Crusade

To the solicitations which the Attorney General made to Congress for funds for his "red" crusade, the only response of Congress was its regular June appropriation and a deficiency appropriation the next December. They aggregated $2,600,000. But those appropriations were explicitly for "the detection and prosecution of crimes against the United States, " and for that purpose only. The sole semblance of authority to spend any of that money for anything else would have to be found in a clause of both appropriation Acts which authorized "such investigations regarding official matters under the control of the Department of Justice or the Department of State" as might be directed by the Attorney General. But this clause, though it would have authorized expenditures for deportation activities if the expulsion of aliens had been "under the control of the Department of Justice or the Department of State, " could" not be so construed inasmuch as alien expulsions were not under the control of either Department. They were, by explicit law, under the control of the Department of Labor exclusively. None of those appropriations, therefore, could be lawfully expended for alienexpulsion purposes unless proceedings to punish <k crimes against the United States" and proceedings to expel aliens from the United States, were in the same legal category — which, emphatically, they were not.

Prosecutions for crimes are judicial; alienexpulsions are administrative. In no aspect of governmental authority may either be identified with the other. Judicial proceedings for the determination of charges of crime and the imposition of penalties, require indictment, jury trial, conservation of all legal rights and strict constructions in favor of the accused; but administrative proceedings for the expulsion of aliens are almost arbitrary, the power of the administrative officer to expel being limited only by his substantial conformity to the deportation statute under which he acts.

Upon information that a resident alien is unlawfully in the country, the Secretary of Labor issues his warrant of arrest, a subordinate arrests the alien, and giving him an informal hearing, reports the evidence to the Secretary, who, governed by it, passes upon the alien's residential rights and nothing but his residential rights. If the Secretary of Labor decides to expel, the courts may challenge his decision upon writs of habeas corpus. But if there is the slightest evidence in the record to justify that administrative decision, whatever the counter-proof may be, a writ of habeas corpus must be dismissed by the court and the alien turned back for deportation. The reason the courts give for dismissing these writs of right whenever there is any evidence at all in support of the expulsion decision by the administrative authority, is that the proceeding is Executive and not Judicial. That proceedings for the expulsion of aliens from the United States are not in the same legal category with proceedings to punish crimes against the United States is a principle of law which our courts have invariably maintained.

There was, therefore, no lawful authority for expending, in aid of proceedings for the deportation of aliens, any part of those Congressional appropriations for "the detection and prosecution of crimes. " That the agents of the Department of Justice did not themselves believe that they were engaged in detecting Federal crimes when they arrests in their raids is evident from their policy of releasing raid-caught citizens as soon as they discovered the fact of citizenship. If they were detecting crimes, it was as important to hold detected citizens as it could have been to hold detected aliens. Nevertheless the Attorney General assumed that those appropriations for "the detection and prosecution of crimes " were intended by Congress to enable him to carry on a stupendous "red" deportations crusade at his own discretion. In his official report for the fiscal year 1920 he found the Federal statutes to be "inadequate to properly handle the ultra-radical situation by criminal prosecution" — the only method, be it remembered, by which any of the appropriations in question could be properly applied to any purpose, — "and consequently the efforts of the" Department of Justice " became centered upon the activities of alien agitators, with the object of securing deportations of such of those persons as were violating the provisions of" the law " familiarly known as the 'deportation statutes.' "

To effect that object, so this official report explained, " close cooperation was established between" the Department of Justice "and the office of the Commissioner General of Immigration, resulting in the arrest and ultimate deportation of some of the leading anarchists of the United States."

These indications of a diversion of Congressional appropriations from the "detection and prosecution of crimes," for which they were made, to "the object of securing deportations" of such alien agitators as were violating the deportation statutes — for which the appropriations were not only not made, but for which appropriations to the Secretary of Labor (the only official legally empowered to execute those statutes) were refused by Congress — are offered here with no intention of setting up a technical legal criticism. Technically the matter was one for adjustment between the Department of Justice and the Treasury Department. Moreover, American sentiment is usually indifferent to legal technicalities when desirable results are obtained. If, therefore, the aliens in question had been proved to be the dangerous characters the Department of Justice accused them of being, any criticism of that Department's diversion of public funds from criminal proceedings to deportation proceedings would be futile.

Neither is personal criticism of the Attorney General especially intended. The bomb explosion in front of his residence, supplemented with the detective stories that were thrust upon him, would sufficiently account for his remarkable attitude toward those appropriations. The point of the criticism is the ineffectiveness of that diversion of public funds. Not only was the money diverted from its lawful purpose; it was shot into the air. The Attorney General had in his Department what was known officially as the "General Intelligence Division" — a euphemism for the detective group. This Division was* within the sphere of influence of such private detective agencies as those of which Sidney Howard tells in his exposure entitled "The Labor Spy." 1 Quite significant, at any rate, is the attention officially declared in the annual report of the Attorney General for 1920 to have been given by his "General Intelligence Division" to the steel strike and the coal strike of 1919, a significance which is emphasized by a statement in the same official report to the effect that although the offenses of the alien agitators were not crimes, they were responsible for "a considia The Labor Spy; A Survey of Industrial Espionage. By Sidney Howard. With an Introduction by Dr. Richard C. Cabot, Professor of Social Ethics at Harvard. Based on a Report for Dr. Cabot, under the auspices of the Cabot Fund for Industrial Research, by Sidney Howard and Robert Dunn." Reprinted and for sale in pamphlet form by The New Republic, 421 West 21st Street, New York City.

erable amount of the industrial and economic unrest."

The whole "red" crusade seems to have been saturated with "labor spy" interests — the interests, that is, of private detective agencies which, in the secret service of masterful cor^qratigjis, were engaged in generating and intensifying industrial suspicions and hatreds. It was under these influences, apparently, that the appropriations authorized by Congress "for the detection and prosecution of crimes" exclusively, were in part diverted to the "rounding up" of aliens, not as criminals but as possible subjects for administrative deportation. Conscious of lacking any particle of authority to pursue the raiding policy of its "radical division," the Department of Justice asked the Department of Labor to "cooperate." What was meant by "cooperate" may be inferred from an incident at a conference on the subject between the two Departments, at which the Attorney General in person was present.

The Attorney General proposed to transmit to the Department of Labor, from time to time, the names of aliens for whom he required warrants of arrest in deportation proceedings.

"When the Attorney General gives orders for warrants of arrest," said he, "the warrants must issue upon his certificate that there is sufficient cause. He will possess the proof but must not be required to produce it."

"But that cannot be done," interrupted a conferee from the Department of Labor — A. Warner Parker, a man of long and responsible legal service in connection with immigrant administration.

"Do you mean to tell me," the Attorney General exclaimed, turning towards Mr. Parker and speaking with disputatious emphasis, "that there is no law under which you can issue a warrant for the arrest of an alien when I certify that he is subject to deportation f "

"Mr. Attorney General," was Mr. Parker 's calm response, "not only is there no such law, but no such law would be Constitutional if there were one."

Very much, therefore, to the chagrin of the detectives, they learned that in order to carry out their program they must make regular applications to the Department of Labor for warrants of arrest against aliens alleged to be deportable, must do so in each case, must back up each application with proof of "probable cause" in accordance with the requirements of the American Constitution, and must follow those proceedings with lawful and substantial proof that each alien was deportable under the alien expulsion laws.

This jurisdictional relation of the Department of Labor to the "red" crusade harks back to a period antedating by many years the very existence of that Department. Under Acts of Congress relating to aliens a mass of statutory law had accumulated since our Government abandoned its "open-gate" policy of almost a century for the "closed-gate" policy which it began to substitute in the 1880 's. These laws subjected certain classes of aliens to exclusion from the United States, and if residents to expulsion. But no attempt to exclude or expel agitators was made until 1894. In that year, however, under the leadership of Senator David B. Hill of New York, a fruitless attempt to exclude "anarchists" occurred. The Senate adopted the measure. It made alien " anarchists" inadmissible, and if residents their deportation mandatory; but as it embodied no definition of the term "anarchist," an effort to rush the measure through the House in the last hours of the Congressional session and on the next day after the Senate's favorable action, was defeated by an objection to considering it out of its calendar order. That objection was interposed by Representative John De Witt Warner of New York. He did not insist upon his objection, however, until the proponents of the measure refused to accept the reasonable terms upon which he offered to withdraw it. What he asked for was the insertion of a definition of the indefinite word "anarchist," so that when the measure became law it could not be abused by administrative proceedings against alien advocates of legitimate societary reforms.

As the comprehensive term for a cult, "anarchism" originated with Joseph Pierre Proudhon about the middle of the Nineteenth Century.

Proudhon 's anarchism predicated social order upon an assumed self-control of free individuals. It therefore rejected governmental force and thus asserted the logical ultimate of the old American doctrine, as expressed by Thomas Jefferson, that "the best government is the government that governs least."

Out of Proudhon 's anarchistic or no-government philosophy there emerged two principal schools of anarchism — the philosophical or pacific and nonresistant, and the revolutionary or militant— each having its own variety of factional groups. The militant school is terroristic, violently revolutionary; its most familiar slogan is "propaganda by deed" destructively. From the methods and activities of this school, the word " anarchist" has in all probability derived that sinister significance which attaches to its indiscriminate use as a verbal brickbat, handy to the tongue,^ for hurling in lieu of argument at peaceable propagandists for social readjustments.

Radically different from the militant varieties are the pacific or philosophical anarchists. They are as different as traditional Quakers are from "fighting Quakers." Instead of promoting revolutionary violence for the overthrow of governments, philosophical anarchists advocate theories of peaceable evolution out of what they regard as governmental bondage and into social conditions of individual freedom. They plead for extreme individualism, even as their opposites in opinion regarding societary organization plead for extreme socialism.

Those two primary principles of human association, individualism and socialism, were impressively contrasted by Henry George. Referring to his having been classed with socialists by individualists and with individualists by socialists, he wrote: "I neither claim nor repudiate the name, and, realizing as I do the correlative truth of both principles, can no more call myself an individualist or a socialist than one who considers the forces by which the planets are held to their orbits could call himself a centrifugalist or a centripetalist."

Alien individualists who, unlike Henry George, had failed to recognize individualism and socialism as social-balancing forces, would have come under the condemnation of that Senate measure of 1894 which Congressman Warner defeated because its sponsors would not specify its application except by the indefinite term "anarchist." If it had been enacted by Congress, Tolstoy would have been included in its comprehensive condemnation. It might have included Henry D. Thoreau if he had been an alien and still living. Possibly even his friend Ralph Waldo Emerson could have been deported had he been an alien with such a law in force in his lifetime. And certainly it would have applied to aliens assenting to some of the opinions of that other American philosopher, the first Henry James, whose greatness Americans may yet be as eager to acknowledge as they are now to acknowledge that of his two justly distinguished sons. For Henry James the elder wrote of republican government, or, as he phrased it, that form of human "government whose only sanction is the will of the whole people," that it removes the possibility of "any more expansive form of merely instituted or inforced fellowship " among men, "and so inevitably gives way itself at last to a free spontaneous society." A "free spontaneous society" is the essential principle of philosophical anarchism. After the Senate measure of 1894 for the exclusion and expulsion of alien "anarchists" had been defeated in the House by Congressman Warner because its supporters obstinately refused to insert a definition of "anarchist" so as to prevent an abuse of the law, its like was not heard of again for nearly ten years. Even the subject matter attracted no attention until an American lunatic with a foreign name who called himself an "anarchist" murdered President McKinley, as another American lunatic with a foreign name — who, however, did not classify his insanity — had murdered President Garfield. Inasmuch as President McKinley 's assassin called himself an "anarchist" and bore an alien name, though a born citizen of the United States, Congress was stirred by his crime into legislating indiscriminately against alien "anarchists." They were listed in the immigration law of 19Q3 as deportable, the term "anarchist" being again undefined.

The ambiguity of this term was observed, though not passed upon, by the Supreme Court in the well known Turner case. The decision of the court in that case, as explained in the opinion of Chief Justice Fuller, upheld the expulsion order of the administrative officer on the ground that Turner "contemplated the ultimate realization" of his anarchistic "ideal by the use of force." But Justice Brewer, while agreeing with the judgment of the Court, he also regarding Turner as "an anarchist in the commonly accepted sense of the term, one who urges and seeks the overthrow byforce of all government," interposed this significant comment by way of guarding against making this case a precedent for the expulsion of philosophical " anarchists": "It is unnecessary to consider what rights" Turner " would have if he were only what he is called by way of differentiation, a philosophical anarchist, one who simply entertains and expresses the opinion that all government is a mistake and that society would be better off without any." So the Turner case cannot be properly cited as a precedent for expelling philosophical anarchists.

As the statute then read, Justice Brewer's cautionary comment might have been reinforced by elementary principles of statutory interpretation. To have considered that statute as condemning social philosophers who hold to the doctrine of non-resistance in its widest scope, would have violated the principle that every part of a statute must be interpreted with reference to every other part of it. In this statute the word "anarchist"

occurred in such close verbal association with expressions of force and violence as to limit the ambiguous word to "anarchists" of the physicalforce variety. But, although the immigration law of 1903 did not exclude alien "anarchists" of the non-resistant and orderly school, the revised immigration law of 1917 did.

In its expulsion section this law required the Secretary of Labor to expel any " anarchist, " including "persons who disbelieve in or are opposed to organized government." After the revision of 1917, therefore, any alien " anarchist, " no matter how pacific his beliefs, disbeliefs, purposes and conduct, was subject mandatorily to deportation to the country whence he came.

Had there been anything lacking in the immigration law of 1917 to terminate the residence here of alien disciples of Tolstoy, or of any aliens who applied the principle of non-resistance to physical force by governments as well as to physical force kgainst governments, the lack was supplied by a supplementary enactment "of 1918. Nor did this Act merely identify alien "anarchists" of the philosophical variety with alien "anarchists" of the physical-force variety. It furnished further pretexts for the crusade of 1920 against resident aliens who were classed epithetically as "reds." Especially convenient was this extension of the expulsion laws for the purposes of the "red" crusade because of its undiscriminating membership clauses. But for those clauses there would have been an almost total lack of "reds."

The law of 1918 went upon the Federal statute books more than a year before the climax of the deportations delirium. It ___was_ enacted under the influence of monopolistic, exploiters of natural forests in the Pacific Northwest, whose objective was the "Industrial Workers of the World," commonly called "I. W. W.'s." Emissaries of those natural forest interests had urged the Department of Labor to deport alien members of that organization under the deportation law of 1917, without proof of more than mere membership.

Secretary Wilson's refusal, which provoked the enactment of the law of 1918, implied no sympathy with "I. W. W." depredations. These were probably as exasperating, as cunning, as criminal and as logically provoked by federated employers as those that Charles Reade brought to book in his famous novel, "Put Yourself in His Place, " which so comprehensively and acutely exemplifies the acid test of its title; and for this lawlessness alien members of the "I. W. W." were arrested at the command of the Secretary of Labor under the law of 1917. They were deported, too, in every instance in which unlawful conduct or assent to unlawful conduct by them was proved.

But Secretary Wilson was of the opinion that no member of an organization can be held responsible for the lawless acts of other members without proof of actual participation or guilty knowledge, unless the lawless acts are authorized, in general terms at least, by the organic documents of the organization. On the question of law here involved he was in complete accord with Attorney General Gregory, Mr. Palmer's immediate predecessor, who h#d been faced by precisely the same question under the espionage law. The enforcement of the provisions of this War-time law having become especially difficult on account of conflicting sentiments in different parts of the country as to the "I. W. W." and similar bodies, Attorney General Gregory, declaring and holding to the principle that " guilt is always personal," determined upon a policy under which "no person should* be prosecuted or interned solely by reason of his membership in any such organization. "

So Secretary Wilson, finding that the organic documents of the "I. W. W." disclosed no lawless purpose but only a legitimate Labor organization, decided that its alien members against whom there was no proof of personal participation in or guilty knowledge of lawlessness by its officers or other members, could not be deported under the law of 1917 or any other deportation law then operative. "Even if some aliens are anarchists and lawless, " he explained, "I see no justification in that for making the Department of Labor so."

After Secretary Wilson had announced his conclusion as to the law of 1917, the forest-region influences of the Pacific Northwest thrust through Congress the comprehensive law of 1918 to which I have referred. The private-interest object of this legislation was to compel the Secretary of Labor to deport aliens for mere membership in the "I. W. W." without any proof of individual culpability. But as enacted by Congress — whether from incompetent legislative draftsmanship or from Congressional reluctance to "take orders " — the Act of 1918 fell short of subjecting innocent aliens to deportation for mere membership in any organized body whose authoritatively declared purposes were lawful, even if some of its officers and members did act lawlessly in its name.

To have deported alien wage-workers merely because they were members of what purported to be a legitimate labor organization, upon no other proof than that some of its officers and members had perverted its declared aims, would have been, as Secretary Wilson suggested, "like deporting alien stockholders in a business corporation, for the rascalities of its directors and their stock-holding confederates."

That is the common sense of the matter, and that is what Secretary Wilson decided after an impartial and careful consideration of the law of 1918 and the facts presented against alien members of the "I. W. W." In his written decision he said: ( We will not arrest, detain or deport any alien simply for joining the 'I. W. W.', but we will arrest and detain until we can deport, any alien, whether a member of the 'I. W. W.' or not, who is found advocating or teaching the unlawful destruction of property or advocating or teaching anarchy or the overthrow by force or violence of the Government of the United States or of all forms of law, or the assassination of public officials, or who is in any manner subject to deportation under the immigration laws."

Those wise instructions, however, could operate effectively only at final hearings in each individual case. As to the original arrests the Secretary's instructions were easily evaded. The requirement, for instance, which the Department of Labor made of the Department of Justice, that there must be proof of probable cause as a condition precedent to the issuing of any warrant of arrest under the alien-expulsion laws, made little difference. The detective auxiliary to the Department of Justice soon turned this Constitutional prerequisite into a farcical formality. Nor in doing so was there much difficulty. The proceedings were on a scale so monstrous, the details were so numerous and so confused and confusing, the arrogance of the detectives was so aggressive, that the Department of Labor, largely dependent as it was upon its Bureau of Immigration, became hardly more than a mechanical toy for the Department of Justice. When the Department of Justice thrust aside all respect for lawful process, and the Bureau of Immigration fell in with this spirit of indifference to Constitutional safeguards, the difficulty of maintaining regularity and preventing oppression rose to the level of the impossible.

Consider the circumstances. Proof of probable cause for an arrest is usually made by an affidavit to the effect that the affiant has been " informed and believes" that the accusation he thereby makes is true. He may in fact have no information nor any reason for belief on the subject. The accusation itself, as well as the oath, may be false. But if this "information-and-belief" affidavit accuses any person of having done something regarding which our laws require proceedings by preliminary arrest, a warrant to arrest the person accused must issue.

Police abuses of that Constitutional guarantee are not infrequent. Professional crime provokes and excuses the abuses, but innocent persons have suffered from the innovation. Such abuses characterized the "red" deportations proceedings. Affidavits of "probable cause" were supplied in "job lots." They were mimeographed forms filled in and sworn to by supervising detectives upon information reported by subordinate detectives. The affiants seldom had any other information; their informants often had none at all. Yet these machine-made affidavits were technically sufficient as a rule to make the issuing of warrants of arrest imperative.

In fact they were often defective. Some were not sworn to. Some were not even signed by the affiant. Some were as blank as when they were ground out by the mimeographing machine, except for names and wear and tear.

Nevertheless, when the Department of Justice delivered what seemed to be affidavits of "probable cause" to the Department of Labor through the Bureau of Immigration with demands for warrants of arrest, warrants were accordingly drafted by an obedient clerical force in the Bureau of Immigration and transmitted wholesale to the Solicitor of the Department of Labor for his signature as Acting Secretary of Labor, the general subject of immigration having been in his charge in behalf of the Secretary for more than a year before the deportations delirium. The Acting Secretary thereupon signed the warrants. Most of them he signed perfunctorily, depending upon the assurances of the Bureau of Immigration. This was quite in accordance with long practice in the Department. It proceeded upon the mistaken theory that action in expulsion proceedings by the Bureau of Immigration was authoritative, though subject for its effect upon the formal approval of the Secretary or his representative by "signing on the dotted line." Even if there had been no such long established practice, it would have been impossible for the Solicitor, Acting as Secretary, to exercise scrupulous personal care in every case in which the Bureau of Immigration recommended a warrant of arrest and vouched for its legality. His other responsibilities were various and exacting, and the warrant-case work was itself at that time overwhelming. The arrests pursuant to those warrants were made with a manifest purpose on the part of the detective force in the Department of Justice to hold the arrested aliens in confinement indefinitely. Three large facts went to prove this purpose. One was the dilatory attention given to the cases in the Bureau of Immigration after the arrests had been made, and manifestly at the instigation of the Department of Justice with which the Bureau was at the time "co-operating." Another was the known impracticability of deporting the prisoners at that time, the country whence most of them came being Russia, and communication between that country and this being then under indefinite suspension. The third was the insistence by the Department of Justice upon amounts of bail out of all proportion to any cause for arrest except offenses of a high grade to be tried in court, and upon conviction to be severely punished.

At that time the detective chatter was not about a horde of harmless members of societies which to most of the horde appeared to be social, or educational, or industrial; it was about bombthrowers, such as those who had fractured the front wall of the Attorney General's residence and put explosive packages into the mails. Nor were representatives of the Departiaent^f impressed with the commonsense thought that as they pretended to have proof of monstrous conspiracies, and identifications of the worst of the conspirators, criminal prosecutions instead of non-criminal proceedings for deportation (criminal prosecutions sustained by real proof of that monstrous criminality, of which, however, no traces afterwards appeared in the deportations proceedings), would have been the upright and downright American policy to pursue.

Their purpose was as clear as it was oppressive, unlawful and un-American. It was to imprison their captives indefinitely. If the Secretary of Labor were too conscientious in his Americanism to co-operate with them in disregarding that implication of the Constitution which secures the privilege of bail to resident aliens and citizens alike, then, in spite even of its explicit inhibition, bail must be fixed at a financial figure beyond the reach of the prospective captives. It must be twenty thousand dollars, or ten thousand at the least, in every case in which the secret-service auxiliary of the Department of Justice made the demand.

But Secretary Wilson refused to set the bail at so high a figure without proof in individual cases as they arose of some necessity for it. He compromised, however, upon one thousand dollars, with leave to the Department of Justice to move for an increase in any case upon special proof, and with leave to each prisoner to apply for a reduction. The spirit in which the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice received this concession may be inferred from the fact that its agents fell into the habit of insisting, with hardly any discrimination, upon bail in the sum of ten thousand dollars; and through undue influence with timid immigration officials, they often succeeded in arbitrarily imprisoning aliens for days and weeks, in some cases for months, in default of bail far beyond the financial limit which the Secretary of Labor had set — aliens against whom there was no evidence of atrocious activities or purposes, and often none of anything more than innocent membership in a proscribed organization — in some instances not even so much as that.

The unwarranted influence of the Department of Justice upon field officers of the Department of Labor in its Immigration Service is impressively illustrated by an incident at a western Immigrant station. An official of the Department of Labor duly authorized and known by the immigration officer in charge to be so authorized, advised this officer to reduce the bail of several of his "red" prisoners from the inhibitive sum of $10,000 to the $1,000 prescribed by the Secretary of Labor. The officer replied that he was not sure that the Department of Justice would accept or agree to a reduction!

He might as reasonably have expressed doubts about the disposition in that respect of the Governor of Oklahoma or the Mayor of Milwaukee. Even that usually level-headed and efficient immigration official, who knew perfectly well that the Department of Justice possessed no authority in the matter, had been demoralized by the sorcery spell. He was not alone. There were few Inspectors in Charge who came in contact officially with the Department of Justice detectives without losing their official poise.

One of the first of the excessive bail cases to come to my attention was that of an alien dealer in " antiques" at Washington. He had been arrested by Department of Justice detectives who searched his premises incidentally. The arrest was made under a Department of Labor warrant, but the search was under the authority of no warrant and was therefore unlawful. The prisoner's bail had been noted in the warrant of arrest by the Bureau of Immigration, upon request of Department of Justice detectives, at ten thousand dollars, and with that notation upon it the warrant of arrest received — perfunctorily and without opportunity for the prisoner to be heard — the Department's authorization, the detectives having represented the alien to be a particularly dangerous man. Ten thousand dollars was twenty times the usual bail in deportation cases and ten times the amount fixed upon by the Secretary of Labor at the conference mentioned above.

Unable to furnish bail in that grossly excessive amount, this alien was detained in close custody for several days, being meanwhile subjected to one of those unlawful inquisitions that are commonly known as the " third degree." Finally, however, he was given a regular hearing before an Immigrant Inspector. The proof against him having been incorporated into the record, he deferred offering evidence in his own behalf until he could secure release on bail. To that end he moved for a reduction of bail, and upon the proof supplied by the Department of Justice and embodied in the record I heard and decided his motion.

The evidence showed that the search of this highly dangerous alien's "antique" store had uncovered an obsolete pistol (loaded so securely that the alien had been unable to withdraw the charge), a modern pistol (unloaded) and an antique dagger. They had been purchased by the alien in a job-lot of "antiques" a year and a half before. No ammunition of any kind was disclosed by the search. There was, however, found, besides the pistols and the dagger, a small library in the alien's living room. It contained "radical" books, about sixty volumes, all isolated publications — none in quantities for distribution. Some were socialistic, some were anarchistic, some were "just literature." George Eliot's novels were conspicuous in this menacing collection. Every other book, also, was of a kind that any thoughtful man interested in social subjects might have in his collection of books for reading. The alien denied being an anarchist. He claimed on the contrary, to be a socialist and to hold a belief against "the form of government as it existed." That he would, if deportation were directed, be released on writ of habeas corpus for absolute lack of evidence bringing him within the scope of the alien-expulsion laws, seemed to me certain, so completely was all such evidence lacking. There was no proof in the record to suggest even the possibility of further proof. If the hearing before the Immigrant Inspector had been closed, I should have cancelled the warrant of arrest. But on its merits the case was still in the Inspector's hands; I had to deal only with the question of bail. Notwithstanding the utter lack of incriminating proof, the representatives of the Department of Justice were insistent, and they were supported by the Commissioner General of Immigration in person, that the bail be not reduced below five thousand dollars. In the slang of the day, "thirty cents" would have been appropriate bail in this case. But as the immigration laws required a minimum of five hundred dollars, I reduced the bail from ten thousand to five hundred.

No sooner had this "antique" dealer been released on bail in that amount, than the Department of Justice detectives turned to the criminal courts of the District of Columbia. At their instance a magistrate issued a warrant of arrest in criminal proceedings, exacting bail in ten thousand dollars, the amount which had been originally required in the deportation proceedings. His exaction was reduced by a local court of record to five thousand, and upon giving bail in this sum the alien was released pending action by the grand jury — deportation proceedings being totally abandoned.

Months went by but no indictment came out of the grand jury room; and when three successive grand juries had failed to indict, this extremely dangerous "antique" dealer was unconditionally released. I recount that experience out of its chronological sequence because it exemplifies the animus of the Department of Justice detectives in urging the Secretary of Labor to favor their crusade by exacting excessive bail. Evidently it was part of their plan to imprison "red" captives, the innocent along with the guilty, pending final disposition of their respective cases.

The case of this alien is an instance in point. In the grossly excessive bail exacted for the purpose of prolonging his imprisonment, and in the poverty of proof against him, it is a fair example of the "red" cases which I decided when later in the deportations delirium I became officially responsible for all Labor Department decisions in proceedings under the immigration laws.

Another method of subjecting their captives indiscriminately to imprisonment pending the hearings in their cases, and indefinitely pending resumption of American intercourse with Russia, came to light as the "red" crusade went on. Its immediate object was to discourage surety companies from giving bail in the regular course of their business, for prisoners in these cases. Gossip had it that the surety companies were in league with the detectives to dam up this accustomed source of bail supply. The matter came plainly into the light, however, in only one instance. A surety company in New York City intimated that it had been significantly warned of official criticism if it wrote bail bonds for alien "reds" in custody of the Department of Labor in deportation proceedings. Having one of these cases under consideration with a view to accepting an indemnity and furnishing bail, this company, through one of its officials, informed the Department of Labor of the warning and asked for authoritative information. In his letter of inquiry the surety company's official referred to "the bitter criticism" to which any one was "likely to be subjected who in such circumstances assists the alien to procure such rights or privileges as he may be entitled to under the law," adding the commonsense reflection that it should be "obvious that if the Department fixes bail for any particular alien that action in itself is a statement that the Department has no objection to bail being furnished."

In response to the inquiry embodied in that letter I officially assured the writer that when the Department of Labor "fixes bail for an alien in deportation proceedings, whether in the course of the proceedings or pending deportation as the resuit thereof, and irrespective of the cause or causes of deportation — either under the general Act of February 17, 1917, or the supplementary Act of October 16, 1918 — there is no official objection to the furnishing of bail bonds;" that "on the contrary, the preference of" the Department of Labor "between bail and prolonged detention is for bail, provided only that it be sufficient in amount and in the responsibility of the surety to satisfy the Act of Congress and to secure obedience by the alien to the requirements of his bail bond."

Financed with their appropriations for "the detection and prosecution of crimes," but under the alien expulsion laws and in "co-operation" with the Department of Labor as they boasted but in fact with its Bureau of Immigration, the detectives of the Department of Justice had promoted preparations for their "red" crusade through the latter part of the summer and far into the autumn of 1919, when they launched their first simultaneous raids over a large area of the country, following them immediately with preparations for a repetition of that spectacular performance over a wider area and on a larger scale.

Plans for this new sensation were complete before they came to my attention definitely. The Solicitor had brought about a conference on the subject with the Secretary of Labor and the Commissioner General of Immigration, into which I was at his suggestion invited by the Secretary. At this conference, which took place on Christmas Eve, statements were made to the effect that the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice had demanded of the Bureau of Immigration warrants of arrest approximating 3,000, to be issued immediately in a batch for early execution. The accompanying affidavits of probable cause appeared to the Solicitor to be so flimsy that he refused to sign the warrants in behalf of the Department of Labor as Acting Secretary, without first scrutinizing the proof in each case, and the proof had not been made available to him. The general grounds for the proposed arrests were membership in the Communist Party and the Communist Labor Party. Evidently the detectives intended to make another sensational " round up" of "dangerous" aliens charged in fact with nothing more dangerous than formal membership in a proscribed organization. Secretary Wilson was not sympathetic with those deceptive plans. He feared that the law of the land would again be ignored by arrests and imprisonment of men regardless of whether or not there was incriminating proof. So he directed that the 3,000 warrants be issued or refused in the usual way, upon recommendation of the Bureau of Immigration according to the merits of each case as shown by the proof of probable cause. Consequently the Solicitor as Acting Secretary signed such warrants as the Bureau of Immigration laid before him with its recommendation.

Thus far the Department of Labor had acted co-operatively with the Department of Justice upon the faith of assurances by the Attorney General and his official co-adjutors in the secret service adjunct of his Department that resident aliens in large numbers and of a desperate type were positively known to be engaged in a conspiracy to overthrow our Government by means of a physicalforce revolution, and that the danger was both extreme and imminent. As the crusade went on, however, faith in those assurances began to wane. If such a conspiracy existed, the Department of Justice produced no proof of it. Nearly all the "conspirators" were wage-workers, useful in industry, good natured in their dispositions, unconscious of having given offense. Some were technically within the proscriptions of the law because they had joined a proscribed organization about the unlawfulness of which persons of more sophistication disagreed. Most of them did not know its objectionable character. Many were " automatic " members, that is, transferred in a body from a lawful to an unlawful group without guilty knowledge on their own part and often without any clear consciousness of the transfer — sometimes in total ignorance of the fact. Others were not proved to be even nominal members. Very few if any were the kind of aliens that Congress could in all reasonable probability have intended to comprehend in its anti-alien legislation. The "red" crusade began to look like a gigantic and cruel hoax, and that is what it finally proved to be.

### Chapter VI: Beginnings of the "Red" Crusade

While preparations for the "red" crusade were in progress the Bureau of Immigration called immigrant inspectors to Washington from various stations in the field to advise, to fill out blank warrants of arrest, and to compose advisory memorandums when the records of hearings should have come up to the Department of Labor for decision in the respective cases. Concurrently the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice drew upon the private-detective supply of the country for "special agents" whom it distributed to various points regarded as strategic, and through whom it made co-operative arrangements with local police officials. The detectives were supplied with warrants of arrest from time to time before their demand for the 3,000 warrants in a batch mentioned in the preceding Chapter, and individual arrests were occasionally made; but the crusade did not begin until November 7, 1919, when the simultaneous raids upon meetings of the Federation of Unions of Russian Workers were 11 pulled off" in anticipation of the sailing of the "Buford." This November raiding, however, was only tentative — in the nature somewhat of a laboratory experiment. The "red" crusade began in earnest with the raids of the following January. It was for this exploit that the detectives of the Department of Justice demanded the 3,000 warrants of arrest which the Solicitor as Acting Secretary balked at giving, and regarding which the Secretary of Labor issued cautionary directions to the Bureau of Immigration.

At the time of the "Buford V sensational voyage as "the Soviet Ark," in which the November raids culminated, the newspapers were given a plenty of publicity-tips from ill-concealed sources. From one of these the New York Times of December 22, 1919, made it appear that the "Buford's"

trip had resulted from a campaign which began when Francis Patrick Garvan " joined the staff" of the Attorney General in order "to stamp out the 'red' menace." That remark, quoted from one of the Attorney General's aids, was supplemented with an opinion by his detective chief to the effect that "the goal of the Federal agents was the capture of the leaders, the 'intellectuals' of agitation." Other official "leaks" for the use of reporters and the agitation of public feeling were to the effect that "with the sailing of the ' Buford '," to quote further from the Times of the date noted above, "came assurances from the Department of Justice, which brought about the round-up" of November, that "more are to follow. " According to these leaks, "one group of radicals consigned to the next boat," to quote the same paper of the same date, was "reported on its way to New York" and plans were set "to continue deportations until" the Department of Justice should have "considerably lessened in number the 60,000 'reds' who were said by the Attorney General" to be "in the United States." The preliminaries to the spectacular "Buford" expedition were typical (except that the Secretary of Labor refused to risk repetitions of the "Buford" experiment) of the whole crusade of which that expedition was an incident — a crusade for deportations of aliens regardless of personal culpability and by methods repugnant to American ideals.

The details of those repugnant methods were not in all instances as fully and undeniably revealed as they were in one. In this instance a private detective agency of Pittsburgh had employed an alien with a criminal record to procure "submarine" or "undercover" information about the activities of workingmen employed by one of its clients. His work consisted in part of organizing "radicals" for the purpose of causing them to be deported if the company employing the detective agency were to think best. In the course of this work the "undercover" man cultivated the friendship of Russian "radicals," and to facilitate his operations he joined the Socialist Party and then the Communist Party. Of the latter he became financial secretary locally, and for it he organized "locals" and solicited members. He reported daily to the detective agency that employed him, the detective agency forwarded his reports to its client, and the client furnished information at its discretion to representatives of the Department of Justice.

Upon information so obtained some of the raids to be described were made. In one of them this provocative agent was arrested along with his dupes. He did not feel disturbed at first, for he had been assured by Department of Justice agents that although he would be deported to Bussia with the others, "so as to shield him from suspicion," he would be brought back in due time and be given a job in the Department of Justice; but when actually facing deportation a distrustful feeling overcame him and he told his story to the Examining Inspector of the Department of Labor at his "warrant case" hearing.

That this instance of repugnant detective methods was neither a solitary one nor the worst in connection with the "red" crusade, is a fair inference from the following news item which appeared prominently the day after the January raids in the New York Times: "For months Department of Justice men, dropping all other work, had concentrated on the 'reds.' Agents quietly infiltrated into the radical ranks, slipped casually into centers of agitation, and went to work sometimes as cooks in remote mining colonies, sometimes as miners, again as steel workers, and where the opportunity presented itself as agitators of the wildest type.... Several of the agents, undercover men, managed to rise in the radical movement and become, in at least one instance, the recognized leader of a district. "

The November raids having through such methods proved successful for the purposes of the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice, the deportations crusade for which there had been such elaborate preparations and experimenting began in earnest with the raids of January 2, 1920.

Their main objective being the sensational deportation in cargo lots of alien members of proscribed organizations, these raids concentrated upon alien members of the Communist Party of America and of the Communist Labor Party.

Those two parties were organized secessions from the Socialist Party, which had figured prominently in American politics, national and local, for a quarter of a century. The "left wing" of the Socialist Party had assembled a convention at Chicago early in September, 1919, to form a rival organization, and this convention organized the Communist Party of America. But a large minority, representative of a group which in the Socialist Party occupied middle ground between its "left wing" and its "right wing," opposed the Communist Party platform, and, upon its adoption, seceded from the convention. Forming still another organization, these seceders from the secessionists promulgated a distinctive platform for what they named the Communist Labor Party, which thereupon became a rival of the Communist Party in the United States.

The Communist Party having by its platform committed its membership to the revolutionary manifesto of the Third Communist International, Secretary Wilson was of opinion that its alien members were subject to deportation for belonging to an organization which advocated the overthrow of the Government of the United States by force. He therefore authorized warrants of arrest for aliens against whom there was proof of probable cause for adjudging that they were members. This was in accordance with the proscriptions of the supplementary law of 1918.

Overlooking in the exciting circumstances a vital difference between the platform of the Communist Party and that of the Communist Labor Party, Secretary Wilson also authorized warrants of arrest upon probable-cause proof of membership in the latter.

Both authorizations were afterwards formally and vigorously contested at full hearings before the Secretary, and in extended written opinions following the hearings, he decided to revoke his original decision against the Communist Labor Party but to affirm his original decision against the Communist Party.

Meanwhile, however, warrants for the arrest of members of both organizations were in preparation in the Bureau of Immigration upon demands of the Department of Justice for the large batch of which the previous Chapter tells.

In expectation of the issuance and execution of those warrants, an important alteration was made in the deportation rules of the Department of Labor. Under a previous administration of the immigration laws the rules had placed at the discretion, very largely, of immigrant inspectors, in hearings where they acted as examiners, the right of arrested aliens to have counsel for the protection of their legal privileges. Some examining inspectors habitually refused that right until important legal privileges which counsel might have conserved had been ignorantly abandoned.

Even such very substantial privileges as that of cross-examining adverse witnesses were not infrequently lost.

Courts had now and then commented with some severity upon this indefensible practice, and Secretary Wilson, after investigating it, altered the rule so as to allow arrested aliens the benefit of counsel from the moment of their arrest, as is required by American ideals of fairness in all proceedings involving deprivation of personal liberty.

Whether or not the later rule had embarrassed the detective crusaders in their November raids, or whatever may have been the cause, the Bureau of Immigration managed, in preparing for the January raids, to have the later rule displaced by the older one. Advised by the Commissioner General of Immigration, the Solicitor made the substitution as Acting Secretary. The change seemed to him at that time as of little moment. It appeared only to suspend rights to be represented by counsel until the "superior rights of the Government" had been conserved. But as the substituted rule had previously worked in practice — the Solicitor was not familiar with its operation then, he having come into the Department after its alteration — so it worked when restored. It gave to examining inspectors a discretion so wide that most of them, affected by the delirium of the period and cowed by the dictation of Department of Justice detectives, completely ignored the legal rights of aliens thus put at their mercy.

When the actual operation of the resuscitated rule came to the Solicitor's attention, his democratic sense was outraged by its abuses, and he frankly laid the whole matter before Secretary Wilson who at once revoked the oppressive rule and restored the equitable one which it had displaced. This restoration came, however, too late to conserve the rights of many a victim of the reckless "red" crusade.

After completion of preliminaries for the January raids upon meetings of the Communist Party of America and of the Communist Labor Party, and less than a week before the raiding began, the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice issued to its field agents confidential in-, structions for the projected raids. Among other exactions, the field agents, while executing Labor Department warrants of arrest, were required to seize books and records of the two organizations in order to prove memberships, to arrest officers of the organizations if they were aliens, to make a thorough search of his person "upon apprehending an alien, " and, "if found in groups in meeting rooms," to have aliens "lined up against the wall and there searched." The method of gaining access to "meeting places and residences of the members" was left " entirely to" the " discretion" of the field agent with reference to procuring "a search warrant for such premises," and the distinct understanding was to be "that the arrests"

were "being made under the direction and supervision of the Department of Justice." The "tentative date fixed for the arrests" was "Friday evening, January 2, 1920," though it was noted in the instructions that this date might "be changed, due to the fact that all of the immigration warrants" might "not be issued by that time;" but advice "by telegraph as to the exact date and hour" was to be sent. The field agents were further required by these instructions to "arrange with" their "undercover informants," if possible, "to have meetings of the Communist Party and of the Communist Labor Party held on the night set," information having come from "some of the bureau officers" of the Department of Justice "that such arrangements will be made." The agents were also informed through those confidential instructions that "the grounds for deportation in these cases" would be "based solely upon membership in the Communist Party of America," or the Communist Labor Party, for which reason it would "not be necessary for" the instructed agents "to go in detail into the particular activities of the persons apprehended," yet that it would be "desirable that wherever possible" they "should obtain additional evidence upon the individuals, particularly those who are leaders and officers in the local organizations,"

Along with that special letter of confidential instructions from the detective bureau of the Department of Justice there also went general instructions. Among other duties imposed upon the detectives in the field these required that any "person or persons taken into custody " be not "permitted to communicate with any outside person until after examination" and " permission "

given by the Department of Justice; that "if a person claims American citizenship, he must produce documentary evidence of same — if native-born through birth records, if naturalized through producing for" the detective a "copy of naturalization papers,"

The detective preparations having been completed for raiding and deporting alien members of the Communist Party of America and of the Communist Labor Party, merely as members and regardless of their personal innocence of any kind of hostility to the United States or sympathy with its enemies, an aggregation of simultaneous raids was made in several cities from the Atlantic far into the interior, and — though with force much spent and the dramatics negligible — even to the Pacific Coast. Such of the warrants of arrest as had been issued in advance served for little mors than a pretense of regularity. Arrests were made regardless of warrants, irrespective of the jurisdiction of the Department of Labor by which alone warrants of arrest could be issued, and with extraordinary indifference to law.

Now that the delirium has passed its peak and little remains of its aftermath but a collection of lawless precedents which may yet serve more efficiently than reckless mobs to shatter the liberty guarantees of the American Constitution, it seems like the memory of a nightmare. But at the threshold of Nineteen-twenty, the outlook excused at least an entry in my journal for the opening of that delirious year. "At present," so that journalentry reads, "there are signs of an overthrow of our Government as a free government. It is going on under cover of a vigorous ' drive' against 'anarchists/ an 'anarchist' being almost anybody who objects to government of the people by tories and for financial interests."

### Chapter VII: The January Raids — A Nation-wide Glance

Hardly had the year Nineteen-twenty opened when the Department of Justice entered upon the "red" crusade for which its raiding of the preceding November had been a " try-out." Numerously recruited for the occasion from "rough neck"

groups of the "strike-breaking" variety and actively supported by local police authorities, the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice spent the night of the second day in January at raiding lawful assemblages in more than thirty cities and towns of the United States — thirty-three being the number officially reported. Their object was wholesale arrests in furtherance of the plans already outlined for mass deportations of alien members of the Communist and the Communist Labor Parties. The approximate number of arrests officially reported was 2,500.

Those January raids, which resembled the raids of November, were spread over a vastly greater territory and involved the rights of a much larger number of American residents, both aliens and citizens. With more or less vigor — in some places more, in others less — they were carried on, simultaneously by the, calendar and the clock, from Coast to Coast — in Maine, New Hampshire, Massachusetts, Connecticut, New York, New Jersey, Maryland, Pennsylvania, Ohio, Indiana, Missouri, Florida, Michigan, Illinois, Wisconsin, lowa, Nebraska, Colorado, Minnesota, Kansas, California, Oregon and Washington State. Toward public law and private rights, these raids were if possible more defiantly reckless than those of November. Although the raidings in the farther West and in the farther South and North were hardly more than publicity gestures to give a nation-wide appearance to the crusade, those north of the Ohio River and east of the Mississippi, as far north as New Hampshire in the east and Wisconsin in the west, were serious displays.

In the strategic cities and towns of that vast and populous region, they involved lawless invasions of peaceable assemblies — public and private, political, recreational and educational. Meetings wide open to the general public were roughly broken up. All persons present — citizens and aliens alike without discrimination — were arbitrarily taken into custody and searched as if they had been burglars caught in the criminal act. Without warrants of arrest men were carried off to police stations or other temporary prisons, subjected there to secret police-office inquisitions commonly known as the " third degree/' their statements written categorically into mimeographed question blanks, and they required to swear to them regardless of their accuracy. The sole excuse for these outrages was the mere presence of the victims at those open and lawful meetings. That the meetings were lawful is evident from the fact that there were no prosecutions of citizens for attending them. And as with public meetings, so with private gatherings and social entertainments. Even homes did not escape night invasions and lawless searches, nor their law-abiding inmates unwarranted arrest. The crusade seems to have been conducted in accordance with the notion which the Attorney General afterwards adopted by way of apology for such lawlessness as he was obliged to confess, that when you suppose you are " trying to protect the community against moral rats you sometimes get to thinking more of your trap's effectiveness than of its lawful construction. "

The day after the January raiding began, accounts of it from all quarters were of course broadcasted by the press over the country. Local news reports were gathered by local reporters for their respective local papers, but the material for the comprehensive news stories that went out from Washington, principally through the Associated Press, was supplied by the Department of Justice. One of these widely published inspirations, justifying the lawless raids by reference to "the seriousness of the attempt to overthrow this Government,"

told of a discovery by the raiders of four bombs in a Communist Party meeting place. Those bombs were the only ones discovered in all the January raids. Nor was there anything to indicate that even they were bombs except the fact that they were iron balls. Taken to the TJ. S. Attorney's office those iron balls, deposited for safety in a pail of water, were exhibited suggestively to local reporters. Their seizure was reported, of course, to the Department of Justice at Washington whence exciting news of a bomb discovery was sent over \he country. Those four iron balls must have melted in the pail of water, for they were never heard of again.

The Department of Justice detective in charge, who assured the local reporters, and presumably his chief at Washington, that the submerged iron balls were bombs, forgot this highly sensational feature of the raids when he testified before a Senate subcommittee a year afterwards. The Attorney General also must have forgotten that important piece of proof against the Communist Party, for in his official report he said nothing of bombs, but limited his Department's credit for seizures in the January raids to " large quantities of revolutionary documents" and "many firearms." If the documents were dangerous, the term "revolutionary" was not sufficiently descriptive. Americans ought to be told what is meant by "revolutionary" as applied to documents, before being asked to condemn their publishers. Is not the American Declaration of Independence a "revolutionary" document? Documents containing secret instructions in promotion of an underground conspiracy would be revolutionary in a sense that American ideals condemn; but if among the documents seized in the January raids any were secret, they are secret yet— even as to their existence. Regarding firearms, the evidence is convincing that with the exception of a few ordinary pistols taken from the pockets or the trunks or the suitcases of individuals whose possession of them signified nothing lawless, the only firearms seized in the January raids consisted of a stack of guns used for amateur theatricals which were found among the stage " properties" of an amateur dramatic society. A careful examination of all the files in the Department of Labor relating to these raids, disclosed, besides those stage-property guns, only four firearms. Might not as many be found upon a search of any 3,000 persons rounded up at any time and anywhere and for anything or nothing?

At no place in all that nation-wide raiding of January, 1920, were any weapons or explosive materials or destructive mechanisms discovered from which an inference of projected crime, private or political, could be reasonably drawn. Even as to criminal thoughts the proof was flimsy— absurdly so in contrast with the severity of the raiding.

### Chapter VIII: The Raiding in New England

Meeting halls and family homes in twenty New England cities and towns were subjected to that ruthless night-raiding of January, 1920. Fourteen of the twenty were in Massachusetts— Boston, Chelsea, Brockton, Bridgewater, Norwood, Worcester, Springfield, Chicopee Falls, Holyoke, Gardiner, Fitchburg, Lowell, Lawrence and Haverhill; the remaining six were in New Hampshire— Nashua, Manchester, Derry, Portsmouth, Claremont and Lincoln.

" Concentration points" had been established in both States, mostly in police stations, at each of which an immigration inspector was in attendance with batches of warrants of arrest. Arrests were made, however, quite regardless of warrants.

Nor were search warrants used, though homes were invaded, trunks broken open, personal papers seized and personal privacies shamefully disturbed. Captives were forced to stand in line against the walls of the / public-meeting rooms in which they had been caught, and in this position were searched like victims of a predatory holdup.

Blank questionnaires were filled out by the captors as they questioned their captives. At a hall in Worcester the entire audience attending a publicly advertised meeting, about 200 in all, were questioned, individual by individual, the citizens being thrust out and the aliens, about a hundred, taken to a jail from which all but sixteen were released in the course of the night.

So many prisoners were released with the same contempt for lawful process which characterized the arrests, that the aggregate of arrests in New England can only be estimated. Responsible estimates, however, place the New England total at from 800 to 1,200. A large proportion were released, as arbitrarily as they had been arrested, after periods of imprisonment ranging from a few hours to two or three days.

Altogether the New England raids were disgraceful legal travesties, riotous lynchings in defiance of law by officers of the law; and they were so characterized by a Federal judge of honorable distinction who saw and heard the testimony of the witnesses on both sides. After describing these and other outrages, which had been proved both directly and circumstantially at a hearing on habeas corpus writs before him in the United States Court at Boston, this United States Circuit Judge, George W.

Anderson, remarked in the course of a comprehensive, convincing and extremely able argument in support of his decision, that "a mob is a mob, whether made up of Government officials acting under instructions from the Department of Justice, or of criminals and loafers and the vicious classes. " Among the meetings raided in New England was one which had assembled to discuss the organization of a co-operative bakery. It was composed of citizens as well as aliens and in about equal proportions. Yet the entire assemblage of thirty-nine persons was arrested and imprisoned in cells until morning.

A woman and her little daughter, the latter a, mere child, who were arrested at one of the meetings in New England, were ruthlessly separated — the child turned adrift at midnight to find her way home in a remote part of the city alone, the mother held captive for a month.

Another woman, a citizen of the United States. was arrested at night in bed at her home, without warrant, by several men not over-refined in manners, who took her to a police station where she was searched and then released.

In one of those New England towns, thirteen women were arrested at a meeting; seven were released at the police station, but the other six were kept in a cell without a mattress through the night and most of the next day.

Those are instances which illustrate some characteristics of the New England raiding. Taken as they are from Judge Anderson's written decision in the habeas corpus cases which were tried elaborately on both sides before him, they may be regarded as especially well verified and characteristic. This is true likewise of the other circumstances of the raiding which are here referred to and which those instances serve to illustrate.

Worse even than these characteristic instances of that raiding was some of the official conduct that followed it.

Captives to the number of considerably mere than 400— being from half to a third of the whole number arrested in this part of New England— were taken to the immigration station at Boston and thence to the Deer Island detention place in Boston Harbor. Bitter complaints concerning the circumstances of the arrests and detentions came to the Department of Labor, and in the temporary absence of the Solicitor, who still had Departmental charge of immigration work, the complaints were referred to me. I ordered an investigation by two competent and trustworthy immigration officers, who upon returning from Boston reported that conditions at Deer Island had been for a few days utterly unfit, but were now greatly improved. In explanation of the original unfitness, they assured me that the fault did not lie with the immigration officials at Boston, for too little time had been given to prepare accommodations for the four hundred prisoners delivered at that station by the Department of Justice. By way of verifying their count, one of the investigators remarked to me that the number of prisoners was so large that when they were unshackled "the chains made a pile that high "—indicating with his hand a height of about three feet.

"Pile of chains!" I exclaimed.

"Yes, indeed," replied both investigators in one voice. Then one of them, corroborated by the other, explained: "The Department of Justice marched their prisoners through the streets of Boston to the immigrant station in chains. We know it for we saw photographs of the chained prisoners lined up in a group." One of the investigators added, with a queer glance of the eye, that "nothing was lacking in the way of display but a brass band."

Conditions at Deer Island were at that time still chaotic. There was lack of proper heat for the January weather. There was lack of adequate toilet facilities. Incommunicado exactions were rigidly enforced. One of the captives dashed out his brains in a corridor by plunging headlong from the fifth floor above. He did this in the sight of fellow captives who inferred from their own experiences that he had been crazed by his. Another was committed for insanity. Others were on the verge of insanity from the bewildering circumstances of their captivity. It was in such bewildering circumstances, terrifying as well as bewildering, that the victims of the raids were given their hearings in deportation proceedings, pursuant to warrants of arrest issued either before or after the raids — some before and some after. Each hearing was conducted by an immigrant inspector authorized by law to conduct such hearings. Each was as a rule held in the presence of a Department of Justice detective who acted both as prosecutor and witness. The accused alien, who in many instances was ignorant even of our language and was not always fairly served by interpreters, was denied the right to have counsel — the temporary rule giving discretion to the inspector having remained in force from less than a week before the raids till nearly four weeks afterwards. But in spite of circumstances so adverse to the prisoners, the immigrant inspectors were obliged to acknowledge their inability to find cause for deportation against a majority. One of the inspectors is on record as testifying in court that out of from 30 to 35 of the Deer Island "red" cases he had disposed of, he was obliged to recommend cancellation of the warrants of arrest in fully 25, finding even as much as bare technical cause for deportation in less than ten. Let it be remembered in this connection that mere membership in either the Communist Party of America or in the Communist Labor Party, was in nearly all the cases the only question of fact involved. By what stretch of inference, then, could any undelirious mind have imagined that those droves of captives were dangerous "reds"! Judge Anderson's mind, at any rate, was not equal to that task in passing judgment upon the writs of habeas corpus. j "Pains were taken," so he wrote in his decision, "to give spectacular publicity to the raid, and to make it appear that there was great and imminent public danger against which these activities of the Department of Justice were directed. The arrested aliens—in most instances perfectly quiet and harmless working people, many of them not long ago Russian peasants — were handcuffed in pairs, and then for the purposes of transfer on trains and through the streets of Boston, chained together. The northern New Hampshire contingent were first concentrated in jail at Concord and then brought to Boston in a special ear thus handcuffed and chained together. On detraining at the North Station, the handcuffed and chained aliens were exposed to newspaper photographers and again thus exposed at the wharf where they took the boat for Deer Island. The Department of Justice agents in charge of the arrested aliens appear to have taken pains to have them thus exposed to public photographing."

Another abuse which Judge Anderson found from the evidence in these habeas corpus cases to have been prevalent in New England — one which appears, indeed, to have been characteristic of the "red" crusade everywhere in the United States— was the employment of provocative agents, after the Czar fashion in Russia, to coax unsophisticated aliens into their web. To these official propagandists of crime Judge Anderson alluded in quoting one of the instructions of the Department of Justice to its agents in the field. The instruction was in these words: "If possible you should arrange with your undercover informants to have the meetings of the Communist Party and the Communist Labor Party held on the night set. I have been informed by some of the bureau officers that such arrangements will be made."
