This instruction necessarily implies, and Judge Anderson so interpreted it, that, to quote from his decision, "the Department of Justice then had, in these two parties, pretended members having sufficient influence so that they could arrange to have meetings 'held on the night set,' " which "shows that the Government spies were then active and influential in these proscribed parties; they were not mere onlookers/' In other words they were provocative agents— a type of detective long before known in the police systems of autocratic countries as "agents provocateurs."

In those habeas corpus cases the Department of Justice was represented by Lewis Goldberg, Assistant U. S. Attorney at Boston, and the aliens by Morris Katzeff, Esq., of the Boston bar.

Judge Anderson was assisted at the hearing by Lawrence G. Brooks, Esq., of the Boston bar, by Professor Felix Frankfurter and by Professor Zechariah Chafee, of the Harvard Law School, each of whom appeared voluntarily as amicus curiae, a friend of the court, to represent the public interests irrespective of those of the contestants.

The cases did not come to a hearing until nearly five months after the New England raids. The decision was not handed down until nearly a month later. It is introduced here for its bearing, not upon the lawful liability of the aliens to deportation—an > \ appellate court having reversed it in that respect on the ground that the decisions of the Secretary of Labor were final since there was evidence to support them — but for the light it throws upon the lawlessness of the January raids in New England. It clearly presents the Ibest possible proof of the manner in which the raids were made; and in this respect it has not been reversed by any court, nor could it be upon the convincing evidence before Judge Anderson.

### Chapter IX: The Raiding in New York and Vicinity

If the New England raids were grossly defiant of American ideals and law, they nevertheless had no advantage in that respect over those that were made under the same direction on the same night and at about the same hour by the clock in other American regions.

In my summary of the raids in New England I omitted Connecticut because that State is in another Federal-Court Circuit — a difference in judicial jurisdiction which is emphasized for purposes of geographical classification by the fact that it was principally in the vicinity of New York that the Connecticut raids were made. As part, therefore, of the "red" crusade in New York and vicinity, we have now to glance at the raiding in Connecticut. Among nearly a score of raided cities and towns in that State were Ansonia, South Manchester, Meriden, Bridgeport, Waterbury, New Haven, New London and Hartford. Active ip this man-chase were private detectives of industrial corporations whose skill was supplemented by the energies of ambitious local police. Group arrests were made at night in social gatherings and public mass meetings. The captives were held through the rest of the night and far into the morning hours without a shred of lawful authority. Some were then released and others detained, all having first been subjected to an arbitrary and secret inquisition. In this part of the New~Tort^raidihg region, a vigorous agent of the Department of Justice was submissively supported by a bewildered immigration officer. High bail was demanded regardless of Secretary Wilson's orders. Bail actually fixed in warrants of arrest was increased without authority. Friends of prisoners were not allowed to see them. Even counsel retained in their behalf were excluded. And long lapses of time intervened between the unlawful detentions and the lawful hearings.

The center of the raiding in the region we are now passing over, was New York City. An evening meeting here at which about fifty persons were in attendance, eighteen of them women, was in session at the Communist Party headquarters, 207 East Tenth Street, when at the stroke of nine "four Department of Justice men," to quote from the World of January 3, 1920, 'Mashed up the stairs of the three-story building " and "with drawn pistols called ' Hands up!' " All hands went up, and the attendants at the meeting were taken to the rendezvous of the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice in high-up rooms of a Park Row skyscraper. At the headquarters of the Communist Labor Party, No. 208 East Twelfth Street, twenty young men and twelve young women attending a meeting were " rounded up" by eight Department of Justice agents and taken to the Park Row rendezvous.

Other raids in New York City were made upon various district meetings and sociaj functions of one or the other of the two proscribed organizations— the Communist Party and the Communist Labor Party. More than 400 persons were arrested before midnight of January 2, of whom 250 were taken, not to the nearest immigration station as the warrants of arrest required, but to the detective rendezvous in Park Row.

Here the prisoners were coercively photographed and " finger-printed" and subjected to unlawful oral examinations, after which those that appeared to their captors to be deportable as aliens were taken to the immigrant station at Ellis Island where, let it be noted, they would have been taken directly if their arrests had been lawful. The others, most of them at any rate, were released as informally as they had been arrested.

Local newspapers noted the fact that no firearms or explosives were found in the New York City raids. But pictures and maps and other innocuous decorations were stripped from the walls of the raided meeting places and, with quantities of propaganda literature, also seized there, were taken to the Park Row rendezvous.

Among the pictures were portraits of Karl Marx, August Bebel and Eugene V. Debs. With those and other confiscated wall adornments, the official force at the detective rendezvous in Park Row amused one another during the day following the raids. Painting Karl Marx's pictured nose red, one of the detectives used the mutilated portrait as a mask, and thrusting a cigarette through the pictured lips into his own, made this caricature of the founder of Socialism seem to smoke gaily as its wearer paraded about the place— all to the delight of the office force. Similarly mutilated, a confiscated portrait of Debs, the leader of Socialism in the United States, was used as a mask to make fun with. Such behavior would hardly deserve mention in a serious story of those lawless January raids were it not that it reflects in vulgar vaudeville humor the spirit of the delirious crusade to which it was incidental.

A more serious aspect of the New York raiding was sketched for the information of a sub-committee of the Judiciary Committee of the United States Senate, about a year after the raiding, by Charles Recht, Esq., the New York attorney for several defense committees. Summarizing his personal experiences, he testified that on January 2, 1920, he was called over the telephone continually from early in the evening until early the following morning by representatives of different organizations who told him that their places were being raided. "Everybody that was present was arrested," so they told him, "including American citizens; in some places they were holding balls and fairs in a public hall, and everybody at the ball or the fair was arrested and taken to the police station." On the following day 600 out of 3,000 who had been reported to him by defense committees as having been arrested, were taken to Ellis Tsland. These statements are in substantial accord with the facts as they came to me officially several weeks afterwards, except that they are set forth mildly. And the New York City newspapers of the two days immediately following the raids published first-hand reports in comparison with which Mr. Recht's testimony is but barely suggestive. In that part of New Jersey which was within the New York sphere of operations, the raiding was true to form. It was here, however, that the " bombs 7 ' were seized — those four iron balls that seem to have melted harmlessly in the pail of water into which they were placed for exhibition.

Another incident of the New Jersey activities was the raiding of a meeting of "radical" Russians while a committee of Poles representing neighbors of a Pole who had just died happened to be present soliciting funds for the funeral. The members of the funeral committee were seized along with all the other persons present.

The raided cities and towns in this State included Jersey City, Passaic, Newark, Hoboken, West Hoboken, Paterson, Bayonne, Franklin Furnace, Harrison and Trenton. New Brunswick, too, should be included in the list, for in that city a Socialist club was raided and drawings of a phonograph invention were seized as possibly representing "the internal mechanism of various types of bombs."

The aliens captured in New Jersey, outside of the New Jersey suburbs of Philadelphia, numbered about 500. Some of them were sent directly to Ellis Island, but most of them were taken to Newark first. They were arriving at Newark during the whole night, were examined there by the detective in charge of the State of New Jersey for the Department of Justice, and as fast as their examinations were finished were transferred by official boat to Ellis Island —after having been photographed forcibly in groups as they were put aboard. The last boatload left Newark in the middle of the afternoon of January 4, 1920.

Of the 500 captives arrested in New Jersey and massed at Newark, 200 were released (without being sent to Ellis Island) as hopelessly non-deportable; and of the 300 who were sent to Ellis Island it subsequently appeared in the lawful hearings in their cases there that in almost every case the arrest had been made upon no better basis than detective expectations of securing admissions of membership from the captives at " third-degree " inquisitions.

### Chapter X: The Raiding in Philadelphia and Vicinity

From Philadelphia the city of Camden, New Jersey, was raided as a Philadelphia suburb along with its Pennsylvania surburb the city of Chester. According to the Philadelphia Ledger of January 3, 1920, more than one hundred persons "out of hundreds seized and examined," had been held under arrest at an early hour in the morning of January 3. "Virtually every headquarters of known radical organizations in this city, Camden and Chester," so this newspaper reported, "was raided last night," January 2, 1920, "by Department of Justice operatives. ' '

At 259 North Sixth Street the raiding was done by a squad of thirty men. On the first floor, members of the Lithuanian Socialist Chorus were caught grouped around a man playing a violin. The music was stopped by the raiders, who put the whole group under guard while they raided the upper floors. On the second floor they found a Technical School to Aid Soviet Russia in session, and, breaking up the session, they arrested those in attendance. The purpose of this school, according to one of its leaders, was the rehabilitation of Russia after order had been established in that country. Proceeding next to the third floor, the raiders found in session a meeting of Russians who had abandoned the Socialist Labor Party and were organizing another party under the name of the Russian Labor Communist Party. This meeting also was broken up and its attendants arrested. The number of arrests on the three floors of that building aggregated one hundred. Those, however, were not by any means all the arrests in the Philadelphia region. Wagon after wagon of the city police department backed up to the Federal building in the early morning of January 3, and unloaded captives who, arrested in different parts of the city and suburbs, had passed the night in cells. The details of these arrests are not unlike those of the worst raiding in other regions of the United States —wholesale arrests regardless of Department of Labor warrants or American citizenship, coercive photographing in groups, private police-examinations of the prisoners by search of person, " third-degree " questioning, arbitrary release of some and arbitrary detention of others.

From Philadelphia, however, there came the most explicit accusations of " third-degree" treatment in the worst physical-force sense of that inquisitional term.

A concise definition of the police "third degree" appeared in the " Questions Answered" column of the Daily News of Washington, D. C, April 27, 1922, which I quote in full and adopt. It was in these words: " A term applied to a very searching examination of a suspected person by police or detectives. Every effort is made in some of these examinations to entangle and trap the accused into a confession of guilt. It has been more than hinted that some of these exui ainations have ended with both physical and mental torture, and that they have been prolonged until utter fatigue and nervous strain has produced a confession utterly at variance with the facts of the case."

In a later issue of the same publication this truly American comment upon the vicious "third degree " practice appeared: "In St. Louis the other day a jury refused to convict a man on trial for murder. The reason was that the defense claimed the police obtained the confession by beating him. If criminals are escaping justice through pleas of police abuse in getting confessions, it is the fault of the police themselves. The whole ' third degree' business is illegal. It is all a form of torture. Our laws forbid it. The man arrested for crime is as sacred in his person as anyone else. The police officer who lays a hand on him or tries in any way to force from him a confession is himself a criminal. "

Inasmuch as the accusations of Department of Justice captives at Philadelphia that they were subjected to "third degree" pressure were denied by the captors a positive judgment is quite impossible. There was a circumstantial probability of the truth of the accusations which the denials lacked, but positive testimony was limited to the statements on either side of interested witnesses, and no serious investigation was made. To anyone, however, who is aware of the propensity of detectives to extort " confessions " rather than "run down" evidence, the accusations of those Philadelphia captives make a powerful common-sense appeal.

The "red" crusade in Philadelphia provoked the resignation of the chief local official of the Department of Justice— the United States Attorney for the Eastern District of Pennsylvania, Francis Fisher Kane — a lawyer of high standing and a citizen of patriotic motives and balanced judgment. Feeling that United States Attorneys were involved, even though the crusade was operated not by them but by professional detectives, Mr. Kane had protested against the raids in his jurisdiction before they were made; and concurrently with his subsequent resignation he outlined his reasons to the Attorney Gteneral in a letter which is vital with the spirit of genuine Americanism and in itself convincing proof of its writer's profound professional qualifications. In the course of his letter of resignation Mr. Kane referred to a case that had come to his attention and which as he rightly inferred was a fair sample of many others. It was the case of a Russian-Jew barber whose wife was an American-born Christian— a Methodist. They had six children, all under eleven years of age, the youngest being a baby of six months. This man's employer spoke highly of his character and industry, and Mr. Kane, who could "not regard him as a menace to society just because he was a member of the Communist Party," thought it "not only foolish but an outrage to take him from his wife and children."

A "red" crusade devotee might respond thoughtlessly that Mr. Kane's dilemma with reference to this case must be faced by every judge when dealing with convicted criminals. But the difference is extrerne. Criminals are taken from their families onlyafter a judicial trial, which determines their guilt and the necessity for their punishment. Not so with the alien to whose case Mr. Kane referred. He was taken from his family for no crime, after no judicial trial, for mere membership in an organization of which his citizen friends could be members, which aliens were at the most but barely forbidden to join, and which a distinguished and able Federal judge who considered the point carefully held to be an organization that aliens might join without thereby subjecting themselves to deportation. If other Federal judges have decided otherwise the significant fact nevertheless remains that the alien was expected to determine the lawfulness of an organization about the lawfulness of which American judges disagree.

### Chapter XI: The Raiding About Pittsburgh and Buffalo

Farther to the west, Pittsburgh and Buffalo were central points in the January raiding.

For the Pittsburgh immigration district, which extends over western Pennsylvania and West Virginia, twenty warrants of arrest had been sent by the Department of Labor to the Immigration Inspector in charge at Pittsburgh. Of those warrants, nine were executed under his direction by agents of the Department of Justice. They did not succeed in executing the remaining eleven, but with characteristic recklessness they arrested other aliens without warrants and thrust them into county jails. No accusations even were lodged, in many instances, against the lawlessly arrested prisoners.

The number arrested was about 30 in Greensburg (a suburb of Pittsburgh) and about 85 in West Virginia. A man who had been missed by his friends for a month was finally found in jail— one of those arrested without warrant by the detectives. Later the Inspector in Charge, a competent and undelirious official, procured Department of Labor warrants for about 40 of the West Virginia prisoners and caused the others, about 45, to be released. In the city of Pittsburgh itself there were no lawless arrests —apparently because the prison warden would not receive anyone without a lawful commitment. The 30 captives in the Greensburg suburb were disposed of by the Immigrant Inspector in Charge as he disposed of those in West Virginia. At Buffalo and near-by towns, including Lackawanna and North Tonawanda, about 150 persons were "rounded up." The arrests in the raiding and within a short time thereafter numbered approximately 153 as reported by the detective in charge at Buffalo for the Department of Justice. Of those captives 31 were released on account of mistaken identity; 9 were turned over to the State authorities on charges of "criminal anarchy" under the New York statutes (they being American citizens and therefore not deportable); 98 were taken over by the local immigration officials under outstanding Department of Labor warrants; and 15 who had been arrested without warrant were held by the Department of Justice until warrants could be procured from the Department of Labor. The charges against these fifteen were that they were members of one or the other of the two Communist parties.

Immigration hearings for the Buffalo region did not begin until January 7. They continued until February 23. A detective from the Department of Justice and an interpreter from the same Department participated in the hearings and virtually conducted them.

Behold the outcome. Out of the 9 citizens who were turned over to the State authorities, 4 were indicted. Out of the alien captives, 1 died in custody, 1 was released at the immigration headquarters in Buffalo, and deportation was proposed by local immigration officials for 111. That proposal had no more legal value than if it had been made by an office-boy. The function of the local immigration officials was to take evidence and transmit it to the Secretary of Labor. Its authority went no farther. When those 111 cases came to me as Acting Secretary of Labor several weeks later I ordered deportation in 24, deferred action in 2, and "cancelled the warrants/'— that is, decided that upon the evidence transmitted to the Department of Labor no lawful ground for deportation was disclosed— in the remaining 85.

Alluding to these 85 "cancellations," the Attorney General, while testifying in his own behalf before a sub-committee of the Senate, remarked that "the trial court" before which the evidence was presented, "found with the Government in 111 out of 112 cases," but "the appellate court...

made a different disposition." The Attorney General should have known, though he evidently did not unless his remark was jocosely made, that the immigrant inspectors at Buffalo before whom the hearings in these cases were had and who "found with the Government," were not in any degree nor in any sense a "trial court." They were at most merely referees with functions limited to making and transmitting to the Secretary of Labor a record of the testimony in each case. He should have known also, if professionally fit for his official station, that the Assistant Secretary of Labor, to whom he alluded as "the appellate court," was not an appellate court even by analogy, but, subject only to instructions from the Secretary of Labor and to habeas corpus proceedings in the Federal courts, was the court of first instance as well as the court of last resort— the only " trial court" that had any jurisdiction in those cases, the only official except the Secretary of Labor who was vested by law with any authority at all to decide them.

But the Attorney General may be pardoned his blunder, if it was not a joke, when it is considered that he probably depended upon an adviser in the "Intelligence Division " of his Department who in his own testimony before the same sub-committee of the Senate implied that the Commissioner General of Immigration gave official weight to the advice of the immigration inspectors by concurring in their findings. The Commissioner General of Immigration had no other authority in deportation cases, as the immigration laws plainly show, than to execute the commands of the Secretary of Labor or his official representative.

The raiding experiences in Buffalo were responsibly summarized by John Lord o 'Brian, a leading lawyer of tha£ city, when in a Bar Association address he said: "The experience of my own city is undoubtedly typical of procedure elsewhere in the country. Early in January there were arrested in Buffalo in several so-called raids conducted by Government and local officials, aided by groups of business men, about 150 aliens, who were taken into custody upon telegraphic warrants issued by authority of the Secretary of- Labor. Those warrants at first fixed universally ten thousand dollars as bail (elsewhere it was fixed at from ten thousand to twenty-five thousand). A few days later this bail was reduced to one thousand dollars. Those prisoners most actively connected with Communist or anarchist societies secured release within a comparatively short time through the aid of their organizations, but the remaining prisoners, who had little or no connection with these societies, being poor wage-earners, found greater difficulty in obtaining bail and thus remained the longer time in prison. A private hearing was given to each prisoner by an immigration inspector who acted as detective, prosecutor and judge, and in some cases acted as interpreter also... Out of these 150 cases of aliens seized and jailed at Buffalo on administrative warrants, 75 per cent of the warrants were subsequently cancelled."

### Chapter XII: The Raiding at Cleveland and Southwestward to the Coast

Passing into the farther West by way of Cleveland, we shall find that the raids in that city were similar to those in the East, unless it may be that they were more systematically made and less spectacularly exhibited.

Under the immediate direction of an agent of the Department of Justice, the Cleveland raiding was done by local police. Twenty-five groups of policemen scoured twenty-five foreign sections of the city simultaneously. Invading meeting halls, coffee houses and cafes, they arrested without discrimination except as to sex. But for the fact that women were not gathered in, these Cleveland raids were very like old-time police raids in the "tenderloin " district of New York City.

Men caught in attendance at the raided meetings, and in the coffee houses and cafes, were hustled into automobiles and rushed to the Federal building, where they were subjected to police inquisitions—without counsel, without friends, and at the mercy of inquisitors trained to be merciless. Along with the men, records also were seized. The " faked" news reports about seizures in New Jersey of Communistic bombs lent a sinister air to the reported activities of the Cleveland captives; but nothing more sinister than club records was found, and the raiding received only scant attention from the local press.

Among the Ohio cities other than Cleveland over which the shadow of the deportations delirium hovered, were Youngstown and Toledo; but nowhere in the whole great region roundabout does the raiding appear to have justified the reports that came out of Washington while it was in progress. Detroit might be cited as an exception, but the circumstances there were extraordinary enough to be reserved for a climax. Meanwhile the material for our story drifts southward through the St. Louis region, and then westward to the Pacific Coast, coming back to Detroit by way of Chicago.

In East St. Louis, Illinois — across the Mississippi from the St. Louis of Missouri— a squad of Department of Justice agents accompanied by immigrant officials, raiding the foreign section at night in automobiles arbitrarily arrested alien residents in their homes and workshops. The circumstances were disclosed by Charles A. Karch, a lawyer of East St. Louis who had recently been the United States Attorney for Southern Illinois.

Mr. Karch testified with reference to a characteristic instance, that the raiding party went at about ten o 'clock at night to the home of a Croatian tailor who owned the property and was an applicant for American citizenship. He had not yet gone to bed. They arrested him without a warrant, together with two men who were visiting him. Not only did the raiding party make those arrests without warrants, but they did it "with considerable gun-play." Having then carried the tailor to their automobile, they took him to the city police-station where they subjected him to an examination. After the examination they took him back to his home, compelled him to open his safe, ransacked it and took from it $150 which belonged to a local organization—the proceeds of a dance that this organization had recently given. Having despoiled the tailor's safe, they then took him to the home of the secretary of the local organization, where, without search warrant and notwithstanding that the secretary was a citizen of the United States, they searched his place and seized books, records and papers —private books and papers, as well as those belonging to his organization. After that they went on with their raiding, from house to house. Most of the inmates of the raided houses were in bed, but the agents forced their way in, aroused the men, ordered them to dress, and driving them into automobiles took them to the city police station. There they examined the secretary of the local organization, who displayed a certificate of American citizenship and protested that they were invading his rights as a citizen. But they forced him to go along with the rest of the captives across the river to the city of St. Louis, and directly to the office of the division chief of the Department of Justice, where examinations of all the prisoners were conducted during the remainder of the night. The examinations were conducted without advising the prisoners of any of the rights to which they were entitled under the law. They were not even informed of the nature of the charges against them. All these facts, derived by Mr. Karch from statements to him by the captives, were admitted to him by the agent of the Department of Justice to be true. When asked by the chairman of the Senate subcommittee examining him whether the captives were discharged, Mr. Karch explained that the local immigrant inspector recommended their deportation, but the Assistant Secretary of Labor discharged them. "Were they shown to be members of the Communist Party?" asked the chairman. "Theoretically, yes," Mr. Karch replied, adding, however, that "they were originally members of a JugoSlovak international Socialist society," and were not shown to have become members of the Communist Party except by action of the larger organization at Chicago, which, through resolution, transferred its locals over to that party. It was not shown that any action had been taken by the unit at East St. Louis. A form letter from Communist Party headquarters had reached the secretary and was brought to the attention of four or five members at a smoker or a luncheon in the club room, where it was read and ordered to be filed. In due time a charter arrived, which, without any resolution, some of the members hung upon the wall.

"That was all the evidence in the record, as I recall it," Mr. Karch added, "which identified any members of this Socialistic organization with the Communist Party." Asked what declaration of principles was set out in the charter, Mr. Karch replied that "the charter did not at all purport to set forth the principles of the party. " In concluding his testimony he stated that "these men were all employed (except the tailor) at the packing industries there, and until Secretary Post discharged them they were out of employment/'

Of the raiding across the river in St. Louis, Missouri, no descriptive testimony is available and the local newspapers did not feature it.

From St. Louis westward to the Pacific Coast the arrests were few in number and relatively inconsequential. The 33 cities of the United States in which the January raids were reported from the Department of Justice at Washington to have been made, included Kansas City, Denver, Los Angeles, San Francisco, Portland (Ore.), Spokane, Omaha, Des Moines and St. Paul. But in San Francisco, Spokane and Omaha there do not appear to have been any authentic reports of raidings; and the reported figures for arrests, in Kansas City 100, in Los Angeles 1, in Portland 20, in Denver 8, in Des Moines 16, and in St. Paul (including Minneapolis) 13, are of dubious statistical value.

The arrests in Portland, according to local reports, numbered 22, but only 7 of the prisoners were aliens. The raiding had been upon the Portland headquarters of the Communist Labor Party, an organization subsequently held by Secretary Wilson to be lawful under the immigration statutes. As no charge but membership in this lawful organization was made against the 7 aliens caught in the deportations raiding at Portland, none were deported.

In the other places westward from St. Louis the raiding does not appear to have been even as important, either in magnitude or lawlessness, as that at Portland. The nation-wide characteristics of the January raids seem to have evaporated in the westward sweep from the Mississippi. In the region of Chicago, however, they continued to challenge national interest.

### Chapter XIII: The Raiding in the Region of Chicago

In this region there was much official confusion, especially in Chicago itself. It seemed to be due to a misapprehension by the States Attorney of Cook County, of which Chicago is the county seat. Overlooking the possible effect upon Attorney General Palmer of that nerve-racking explosion in front of his home and attributing the nation-wide raidiugspectacle to his Democratic ambitions on the eve of a Presidential election, the States Attorney at Chicago, a Republican, marred the dramatics of the national raiding in that city, which had been set as elsewhere for January 2, by " pulling off" a raid of his own the night before.

Originally he had arranged to make the local and the national raiding in Chicago jointly with the Department of Justice on the 2d of January; and for this purpose local as well as national detectives had been gathering what they were pleased to call evidence. But at almost the last moment, political suspicions of the States Attorney determined him to break away from that mutual arrangement and to do his own raiding a day in advance so as to spoil some of the political effect of the Attorney General's "red" crusade. Although his premature proceeding seriously disturbed the Chicago plans of the Department of Justice, especially the publicity plans, its agents nevertheless went through the anti-climacteric performance of making Chicago raids according to the nation-wide schedule.

One of the many places they raided there was a school of which Jane Addams says, in her book entitled "Peace and Bread," that it was one of many that were founded by resident Russians for preparing themselves as skilled mechanics for usefulness in the new Russian state (to which they expected to go), by teaching applied mathematics, mechanical drawing, pattern-work and automobiling, and " where they were so cautious that they did not teach any sort of history or economics." Continuing, Miss Addams makes this description of that raid of 1920 in Chicago: "A general raid under the direction of the Federal Department of Justice 'ran in' numbers of Chicago suspects on the second of January, but an enterprising States Attorney in Chicago, doubtless craving the political prestige to be thus gained, anticipated the Federal action by twenty-four hours and conducted raids on his own account. The immigrants arrested without warrants were thrust into crowded police stations and all other available places of detention.

The automobile school was carried off bodily, the teachers, the sixty-four pupils, the books and papers; the latter were considered valuable because the algebraic formulas appeared so incriminating." Among those arrested was a Russian whom Miss Addams had personally known many years, a Tolstoyan and member of a society "composed of Russians committed to the theory of non-resistance"— a group than which she knew of none in Chicago whose members she would have considered less dangerous. "This man, with twenty-three other prisoners, was thrust into a cell built for eight men; there was no room to sit, even upon the floor; they could only stand closely together, take turns in lying on the benches and in standing by the door where they might exercise by stretching their hands to the top bars. Because they were Federal prisoners the police refused to feed them, but by the second day coffee and sandwiches were brought to them by Federal officials. But the half-starved Tolstoyan even then would not eat meat nor drink coffee, but waited patiently until his wife found him and could feed him cereals and milk,"

The more general circumstances of the Chicago raiding have been conservatively described by Benjamin C. Bachrach, a Chicago trial lawyer of general practice and a Republican in politics, who represented nearly a hundred aliens arrested in Chicago for deportation. When testifying he described himself as "a conservative" to the extent of wanting "all the sections of the Constitution enforced all the time."

According to Mr. Bachrach 's testimony, Department of Labor warrants were issued to Chicago before the raiding. But those warrants do not seem to have guided the arrests, for the agents of the Department of Justice busied themselves after the raiding in singling out arrested citizens from aliens, and aliens who did not belong to any of the proscribed organizations from aliens who admitted such memberships. The aliens who denied membership were released after detention for two, three, four, five or six days and even longer. All these had been released at the end of about two or three weeks— the citizens more promptly than the aliens as a rule, probably within from 24 to 48 hours.

Although the raids were professedly made by authority of Department of Labor warrants, a great number of captives were held without warrants for several days and even for weeks after the raiding. The warrants then obtained were issued upon "information-and-belief" affidavits made by Department of Justice agents, charging the aliens with membership in the Communist or the Communist Labor Party. The fact should be emphasized that their information, derived from " third degree M examinations of the respective prisoners by the agents, was obtained after the raids, before application for the warrants, and while the aliens for whom the warrants were got were in the unlawful and incommunicado custody of their inquisitors. Of 80 Chicago prisoners whom Mr. Bachrach represented, only about seven were found by responsible authority to be deportable. Bail had been put as high in some cases as $10,000, often $5,000; but in most of the cases it was ultimately reduced to $1,000. One alien, a mechanic with a family, was imprisoned from January until some time in March (as a mere member of the Communist Party) because he could not give the $5,000 bail that was exacted. He was finally released for lack of proof of any deportable offense.

In one group of nine prisoners—all hard-working, simple people, with no feeling at all against the United States — who were charged with membership in the Communist Party, not one had any notion whatever of what the principles of the Communist Party were until its manifestoes were interpreted to them at their hearings before the immigration officials. They had joined the organization because they thought it might influence President Wilson to withhold American troops from Russia, so that their relatives might not be killed. They had not even joined the Communist Party consciously, but were automatically transferred to it by the Socialist organization to which they belonged.

Another group arrested in the Chicago raid was composed of thirteen Italians who, all but the organizer, had joined the group so as to get an education in English and music. Although they were ultimately released, their release did not come until after their friends had given bail for them, and then only from time to time— one now and another four or five weeks later. The person who organized the group was held for deportation.

With reference to this group, Mr. Bachrach suggested that the Department of Justice had not been as careful of the liberties of persons as it might have been, for the facts about those innocent men could have been learned before their arrest instead of days and weeks later.

As in most other places over the country, the local immigration officials at Chicago were by no means enthusiastic in their sympathy with the raiding. Arbitrary though the regular procedure in deportation cases is, and trained to the execution of its autocratic methods though those officials were, they did not comfortably stomach that reversion to old-time prussianistic procedure which the Department of Justice had deftly managed to impose upon them. But they were deep in the darlf in consequence of what the Department of Justice called "co-operation" between its detective force and the Bureau of Immigration at Washington.

The raids at Milwaukee, Racine, Rockford and other outlying places in the Chicago region, were like those in the larger places though of course on a smaller scale.

One fact in connection with the arrests at Rockford is, however, worthy of special mention. The examining inspector in the immigration service there was as scrupulous about conserving the rights of the arrested aliens he examined as under the circumstances it was possible to be. Although he could not allow them the benefit of counsel while the surreptitiously promulgated rule forbidding counsel remained in force, he nevertheless invariably warned the aliens he examined that they were not bound to answer any questions, and that if they did answer their answers might be used agains£ them. This was a recognition of one of the traditional rights of every accused prisoner under American law— a right which was almost invariably ignored in the "red" crusade, and which some judges, though hardly bold enough to throw it into the trash barrel, only indifferently safe-guarded.

### Chapter XIV: The Raiding at Detroit

In Detroit the January raids were as abhorrent to an American conscience as were those of New England. They lacked none of the vicious features of the New England raids except manacles and chains, and were as spectacular and if possible more shameless in other respects. There were, however, no court proceedings in Detroit as there were in New England, to put the facts clearly and firmly upon record. Yet the facts have been reported by competent and trustworthy witnesses.

Two characteristics seem especially to have distinguished the Detroit raiding. As to treatment of prisoners, it was more persistently barbarous than that of New England, and it produced a reaction in local sentiment which takes this city out of the delirious class in the history of the delirium of which my story tells. The patriotic lawyers of Boston who volunteered as non-partisan advisers of the court, to stand against an official mob in vindication of American law, had their counterpart at Detroit in a voluntary committee of citizens— lawyers, business men and a bishop of the Protestant Episcopal Church — who undertook to relieve Detroit of the stigma which Department of Justice detectives were casting upon her orderly citizenship. Like all the other January raids, those in Detroit began at about nine o'clock in the evening, local time, on the 2d day of the year nineteentwenty. Some of the typical circumstances are on record in the testimony of W. C. Grarred, the assistant custodian of the Federal building at Detroit where the prisoners were corralled as the raiding went on.

Late in the afternoon of the nation-wide raiding day an agent of the Department of Justice asked the assistant custodian of the Federal building for the use of the fifth-floor corridor as a detention place for "radicals" he intended to arrest that night. The assistant custodian assented, expecting the prisoners to number 25 or 30. But there were 400 prisoners in the corridor when he came to the building on the morning of the 3d of January.

This corridor had no outside windows. It was lighted only through an open court in the center of the building, over which there was a skylight. There was but one toilet. After about the third day the number of prisoners had increased from approximately 400 to approximately 800. It then decreased as the prisoners were given their examinations before an immigrant official.

While they were there, so the assistant custodian testified before a sub-committee of the Senate, the prisoners "had hot and cold water over a slopsink adjacent to" the toilet. As to food, "the friends of these men brought in sandwiches and apples and milk, etc., in suitcases and boxes and bags, and they got food." As to "some of those who had no friends," the witness did "not know what they got," but guessed that "probably they divided up." He "went through this corridor two or three times— walked through to see conditions," though probably not after five o'clock at night, and found that the prisoners "had no bedding of any kind except newspapers and overcoats and clothing and 110 awnings" that were in the corridor.

As to air, "there were no outside windows" and "the ventilation was poor because this was the top floor of the building and all the impure air from the other corridors came up there;" the skylight space being open from the second floor up, "of course all the warm air and heat would go to the ceiling, and it was quite hot up there and rather close." Those conditions continued from the night of January 2 to the forenoon of January 8.

The testimony of the assistant custodian of the Federal building was corroborated and amplified by Frederick K. Barkley, a newspaper man of long experience who afterwards became secretary of the City Club of Washington, D. C. On the 3d of January he had been sent by his paper, the Detroit News, to the Federal building to report the conditions there. Upon arriving at this building he found such confusion among the officials that it was difficult to get any information from them. So he investigated for himself. Climbing one of the stairways to the fifth floor he encountered an iron-bound gate which shut the prisoners within the corridor. Like the lower corridors, the fifth faced a great central court open from the first floor to the roof. Only two rooms opened out of it. One was a little examination room half way between the fifth corridor and the fourth; the other was a toilet room on a level with the fifth. As he came up to the fourth corridor and looked obliquely upward across the open space to the fifth, he saw "men's heads all the way around this whole space, sticking out" through the openings into the spacious court, and heard "a murmur of conversation and shuffling of feet." Going up to the fifth corridor he saw that "the men seemed to be pretty well packed all the way through there." In this corridor he thought there were then about 600, calculating that the number put there originally had been 400 and that the arrests in a couple of supplementary raids had raised the number to 800 by the day following his visit. He noticed, too, that "the heat from the corridors and the rest of the building sort of welled up" into the fifth corridor where the prisoners were. Besides a skylight over the court there were windows in the walls, but so far apart that it was "rather dim" in the corridor. "The heat was sickening, and the stench was almost overpowering"— so revolting that he "could not stand it very long." He saw prisoners in line to the number of 40 or 50 awaiting access to the one toilet in the corridor. The only accessible drinking fountain was by the side of the toilet and in the same little room. The skylight was very dirty and did not seem to admit much light. If there was any way of opening it for ventilation he did not observe it.

Having made his observations of this wretched place of detention, Mr. Barkley investigated the circumstances under which the prisoners had been brought to it. He found at once that no names had been noted nor any attempt made to classify the prisoners as citizens or aliens. He also learned that women had not been arrested. When the raiders caught women at the raided dances and study classes and halls, they let them go. At the time of his investigation the examinations of prisoners had been going on for about hours. They were conducted in the little room between the fourth and the fifth floor corridors. The prisoners under examination were asked only three or four questions. If they said they were citizens they were immediately released, the examiners for the immigration service appearing to have a fear of accountability for arrests of citizens. Apparently the examiners did their best, shorthanded as they were, to expedite the hearings; but some of the released prisoners were confined as long as six days. While some were coming in others were going out, which accounts for 800 as the highest number at one time although 1000 were arrested. Describing the raids as he learned the facts in the course of his investigation, Mr. Barkley testified that the largest number of arrests was made at " Shore Hall," the " House of the Masses, " a large three-story building in which the Socialist Party had its headquarters. On the ground-floor a restaurant was conducted co-operatively by the Workers' Educational Society and the Socialist Party. After the Socialist Party had been swallowed up by the Communist Party the title to this property came into litigation, but meetings were held in the hall and business was done at the restaurant as usual. On the 2d of January a dance was going on at the hall when the raid occurred, and all the persons present there except the women were arrested. Even the professional orchestra which was accustomed to playing "at all high-class entertainments," was reported to have been "run in" with the rest. The raiders also arrested all the men who were eating in the restaurant on the ground floor, which, as well as Mr. Barkley could tell from the outside, resembled "any short-order restaurant." An "intelligent girl, more Americanized than most of those who were arrested,"

and whom he believed, told him while they were together in a lower corridor of the Federal building, that her brother, a prisoner at the time in the fifth corridor, "had belonged to the Socialist Party and when the switch to the Communist Party was made, he did not join the Communist Party because he did not believe in force," but that "he continued to patronize the restaurant" and " while he was eating his dinner there " on the 2d he was captured with the others and had been imprisoned for three days for no other reason than "because he was caught in the restaurant getting his dinner." This girl, who was at the dance in the hall when it was raided, told the reporter further that "they were holding classes in< the ante-rooms and 1 the side-rooms there," and that the raiders "broke up the classes." Several ex-service men were reported to have been arrested at the dance, but these were released as soon as the fact that they were ex-service men was discovered. Even passersby on the street appear to have been swept into the Attorney General's wretchedly constructed "moral rat trap."

Continuing his account of these raids and the subsequent incarcerations, Mr. Barkley testified that after the raids women would come to the Federal Building looking for their husbands, but as the prisoners were transferred to various precinct police stations without a record of their names, some of these women spent five and six days in fruitless searches. In one instance the wife was penniless and had to beg food from neighbors for nearly a week before she found her husband, who had been thrust into one of the numerous detention places.

From Mr. Barkley 's evidently fair and reasonably probable account, it appears that 128 prisoners, taken from the Federal building to the Municipal Court building, were put into the "bull pen"

at the latter place of detention. This "bull pen" was a cellar room, 24 by 30 feet in area, with one window, one little toilet, one door (grated), floored with stone and designed only for very temporary detentions during court sessions.

Within that "bull pen," furnished only with benches without backs, on which perhaps 20 men could sit, as many as 128 victims of the Detroit raids were held for seven days. They were almost literally packed in. The only food they had was one cup of coffee and two biscuits twice a day, except as their friends brought food to them. "The conditions were such," said Mr. Barkley, "that Mayor Couzens"— afterwards Senator from Michigan—"informed the City Council that they were intolerable in a civilized city," and "the health commissioner reported the situation" as not only intolerable but "a menace to the health of the city," Describing the prisoners themselves, Mr. Barkley testified that "they seemed to be simple men, not perhaps very highly educated," but "clean" and most of them "fairly well dressed for workingme — not dirty looking fellows," not like "what we have been led to believe bolshevists look like — that is, when they were taken in there," but "after four or five days, of course," they showed the effects of not being permitted to shave and of sleeping in their clothes.

That those abuses aroused the indignation of the civic-minded citizens of Detroit is not remarkable. The remarkable fact is that similar abuses aroused no general indignation in any of the other cities in which they occurred. Detroit alone organized and gave moral support to a responsible citizens' committee charged with conserving American law in connection with the victims of those raids. For decently recognizing and fairly considering the recommendations of that committee, officially as Assistant Secretary of Labor, I was made a target for much abuse shot out from the Department of Justice to the public. Even from high places in that Department it was falsely as well as loosely if not maliciously asserted that I had organized a committee of my friends in Detroit to decide cases for me. The truth was that publicspirited citizens of Detroit, irritated by the lawless raiding there and outraged by the consequent fact that more than 100 aliens were in close custody awaiting deportations that could not be made, their families suffering meanwhile from lack of support, organized on their own initiative a local committee to relieve the situation in the interest of the good citizenship of Detroit.

That committee consisted of Frederick F. Ingram, a manufacturing chemist and an old resident, a prominent citizen of the State as well as the city, as chairman; S. S. Kresge, the head of a large chain of five and ten cent stores; F. E. Brown, an ice dealer at the head of The Public Necessities Corporation; F. E. Steelwagen, a conservative lawyer; Fred M. Butzel, also a conservative lawyer; and the late Charles D. Williams, Bishop of the Protestant Episcopal Diocese of Eastern Michigan.

The service which this committee undertook in behalf of the law-abiding elements of the city of Detroit was to ascertain the treatment and the culpability of the prisoners captured in the Detroit raids and still in custody, and to inform the Department of Labor at Washington of the facts and of its judgment regarding them. The Department of Labor did not create, select nor organize that committee. Only two of its members were known to me — Mr. Ingram and Bishop Williams. Nor did the. Department assume to invest the committee with any authority. The Secretary's authority could no more be delegated to a civic committee than to a Commissioner General of Immigration or a group of "under-cover" detectives. But upon being informed by its chairman of the purposes of the committee and the local standing and responsibility of its members, the Department did welcome its assistance in relieving Detroit of the scandalous situation which the Department of Justice had produced but for which the Department of Labor was legally responsible.

The problem was baffling. Owing to the raiding and to the lack of communication with Russia, the Department of Labor was compelled to detain in custody, indefinitely, large numbers of aliens who had been convicted of no crime yet had been administratively ordered to be deported as undesirable residents and who were unable to give bonds to assure their appearance when deportation to their own country could be effected. The only recourse of the Department was to keep them in prison or to parole them. The embarrassment was increased by the fact that in all probability many of these prisoners were in reality not undesirable residents even within the proscriptions of a drastic law. Bald perjury at some of the hearings was reasonably suspected. Under those circumstances the public spirit of the Detroit committee was a welcome manifestation. Consequently I gave it access to the imprisoned deportees at Detroit and to the appropriate records, notifying its chairman that its recommendations in individual cases would be gratefully received and fairly considered.

Accordingly the committee made a thorough and conscientious investigation of the Detroit cases, and as Assistant Secretary of Labor I acted favorably upon its recommendations as far as the law allowed. In cases in which deportation was finally found to be necessary under the law for technical resons only, and the committee was willing to accept moral responsibility for paroles pending resumption of communication with Russia, I allowed the paroles. The reasonableness of this course may be inferred from the impression which his work in the committee made upon Bishop Williams.

In the course of a sermon at the Cathedral of St. John the Divine in New York City about a year afterwards, he said, as reported by the New York Globe of April 11, 1921: "Business men are seeing 'red.' They commenced seeing 'red' with their drive on radicalism. They branded every one who had a progressive thought as a 'parlor bolshevist,' and persons have been secretly arrested by paid spies on manufactured information and often deported without cause. I investigated several of these cases in Detroit, and found persons supposed to be dangerous radicals to be but simple, ignorant foreigners, unaware of what was being done to them. It is the foulest page in American history. The very principles of Americanism have been undermined by this hysteria and panic. "

Another member of that Detroit committee testified before a subcommittee of the Senate to the American spirit that prevailed in Detroit both before and after the raiding, and which sustained the Detroit committee in its patriotic task. This member was Fred M. Butzel, who told that Senate subcommittee that the people and the local authorities of Detroit were proud of their course with reference to "radical" meetings there and the treatment of "radicals" at their meetings. "We gave the radicals," he testified, "full and free permission to shoot off their mouths as much as they pleased, and to hold as many meetings as they pleased, so long as they interfered with nobody else."

Before the formation of the citizens' committee, the City Council had requested the Mayor to demand of the Federal authorities immediate removal of the 128 prisoners in the "bull pen" of the Municipal Court building, and toward the middle of January the Immigration Commissioner at Detroit, Percy L. Prentis, succeeded in having them transferred to Fort Wayne barracks. This was after they had been a week in the top floor corridor of the Federal Building and possibly a little more than a week in other places.

Fort Wayne thenceforth became the place of detention for 181 prisoners in addition to the 128— a total of 309. The remainder of the 1,000 arrested had been either discharged or admitted to bail. Hundreds had been discharged because found to be citizens of the United States, the others because they were not proved to be members of the Communist Party, the only accusation against them.

So unique was the attitude of Detroit during the period of this story that the testimony of Chairman Ingram of the citizens' committee of which Bishop Williams and Mr. Butzel were members, may with advantage be quoted from liberally. Describing the sane behavior of the local authorities and the sane sentiment of influential local people at a time when the deportations delirium was making headway everywhere else, Mr. Ingram confirmed the testimony of Mr. Butzel, quoted from above, by saying that Detroit had a policy of "not destroying the so-called radical meetings or permitting the radicals to disturb the conservative meetings. " While "seditious utterances and inflammatory utterances were prohibited" the agitators "were given plenty of leeway to talk" — the effect of their talking being offset by counter meetings. "During the War anything that would discourage the progress of the Federal government in any way was considered" as seditious and was prohibited; and advocacy of the overthrow of the Government by force or violence "was banned completely.

People who made those utterances were not permitted to talk, but we got away with that without any disturbances. They could talk about radicalism, injustice, and all that." And Detroit had no trouble apart from the attempt to interrupt a Breshkovsky meeting with outcries. This disturbance was easily quieted, and the meeting went on. Nor were there any labor troubles. "Then came the raids," Mr. Ingram continued, "and in the raids there were citizens as well as aliens taken and imprisoned, and it caused a tremendous amount of fermentation among those young men, both aliens and Americans. "

Over 1,000 arrests were made in the raids, according to this well-informed witness, who corroborated other witnesses in that particular, and who went on to state that only 595 warrants of arrest were issued, and that the number of prisoners turned over to the Department of Labor was only 527— less by 68 than the number of warrants issued, and less by about 473 than the number of arrests.

One of the persons arrested, a law-abiding citizen, an educated man, a skilled mechanic working for Mr. Ford at the time but afterwards in business for himself, "was attending a lecture, not a forbidden lecture by any means, " to continue quoting Mr. Ingram 's testimony before the Senate subcommittee, when "the hall was surrounded and every one in the hall was taken and imprisoned— amongst them himself. " His wife did not find him until daylight the next morning. "She was permitted to talk to him through the bars," and he, being a citizen, was then promptly released. The place in which he had been arrested was a public hall where an audience had assembled to listen to a public address. It was raided for the purpose of arresting aliens supposed to be members of the Communist Labor Party.

Another man about whom Mr. Ingram testified "was taken out of his apartment house, his suite of rooms, between 2 and 3 o'clock in the morning," without a warrant, and hustled to jail, where he was released about 9 o'clock in the morning upon the fact appearing that he was a citizen. He was "a good citizen, " Mr. Ingram testified, "without any suspicion against him," living "in one of the best parts of the city— as inoffensive a man as anybody could be."

The lawlessness and the cruelty of the January raiding in Detroit differed only in magnitude and unimportant detail from the simultaneous raiding of the "red" crusade in every part of our country in which the detectives of the Department of Justice gave full play on that January night to their deceptive purposes and their repugnant European methods. From Detroit westward and southward to the Mississippi and the Ohio, and eastward through Pennsylvania and New York into Philadelphia, New Jersey, New York City and New England, that "red" crusade was conducted with so little respect for law and order as to discredit the patriotic boast that ours is "a government of law and not of men." The popular approval it received at the time lies beyond the range of any reasonable explanation other than that the public mind was under the influence of what must always be regarded as a monstrous social delirium. Such deliriums cannot continue even in milder forms, nor often recur, without disastrous social consequences.

### Chapter XV: The Assistant Secretary of Labor Takes Charge

Two months almost to a day after the January raids, the Solicitor for the Department of Labor, John W. Abercrombie, gave me formal notice of his intention to absent himself from the Department within twenty-four hours for an extended leave. This decision of his brought me immediately into responsible official connection with the results of the raids.

Until then the Solicitor had been for considerably more than a year in Departmental charge of Immigration subjects. The work was extremely disturbing to him in consequence especially of complications in the Bureau of Immigration, and several times he had assured me of his wish to resign, but I urged him to wait. Circumstances solved Mr. Abercrombie *s problem for him. He told me informally on the Ist of March that in consequence of the death a few hoursTbefore of the Senator from his State, Alabama, he was contemplating a campaign for the vacant seat in the Senate pursuant to a promise he had made his people at the close of a former statewide candidacy of his; and on the sth he waited upon me officially, accompanied by the Commissioner General of Immigration, and, explaining that he intended to be absent for an indefinite time, formally turned all immigration responsibilities over to me.

As Secretary Wilson was at this time absent from the Department, remaining away for several weeks on account of family illnesses and a tedious illness of his own which necessitated his avoidance of any but Cabinet duties when well enough to work at all, I was during that entire period of approximately two months, Secretary of Labor by operation of law for all administrative functioning; and as the Solicitor did not return until after the Secretary's return, I was all the time without an official substitute for any purpose. I had often administered the immigration duties of the Department, and had thus far in the course of the "red 11 crusade been required from time to time to relieve the Solicitor during his brief absences, but now I was plunged into responsibility for the aftermath of this crusade to the full.

A conscientious performance of the duties of the office with reference to that crusade, and in the spirit of the foundation principles of the American Declaration of Independence and in harmony with the American Constitution and the concordant policies of Secretary Wilson as head of the Department of Labor, soon involved me in a hurricane of Congressional politics and newspaper vituperation.

With that rough and tumble experience, my story must in due course deal. For the present, however, it is chiefly concerned with the complex official responsibilities I faced when the Solicitor relinquished administration of the immigration laws.

### Chapter XVI: Communist Party Members

When I took charge of the immigration service the cases of thousands of aliens who had been arrested by the Department of Justice on Department of Labor warrants were pending, and nearly all turned upon questions of membership in the Communist Party of America.

Secretary Wilson had, as I have already explained, authorized warrants of arrest for membership in that organization, and subsequent to the arrests he formally decided the essential membership question after hearing the Department of Justice and the Communist Party in a joint discussion before him. Immediately after the close of the arguments he called the Solicitor and myself into conference over the decision to be rendered. We were all agreed in opinion that upon the case presented he must order deportation. Incidentally I urged that he make it clear on the record that he was deciding only a question of law, so that the courts would be under the necessity in habeas corpus cases of passing upon the lawfulness of the Communist Party with reference to alien membership. Mr.

Wilson needed no urging. It was his own desire that any court decisions in the matter should determine judicially, as he in the first instance must determine administratively, whether or not the Communist Party had brought itself within the proscription of the alien deportation laws. So he decided explicitly, upon the conceded fact of the alien's membership in the Communist Party, that he was therefore and for that reason alone subject to deportation.

The alien in whose case Secretary Wilson made that decision bore the name of Engelbert Preis. The decision was made nearly six weeks before the Solicitor's departure placed upon me the duty of deciding the merits of each individual case in the accumulated mass of cases depending upon membership in the Communist Party. Preis admitted freely that he was an intentional member. So the issue was simple. If the Communist Party belonged to the class of organizations that Congress had prosecribed, then Preis, being an alien and having become a member of that organization under circumstances which charged him with knowledge of its organic declaration, was by the law subject mandatorily to deportation. And that the Communist Party did belong to the proscribed class of organizations was evident from its declaration of a purpose to conquer and destroy the govern ment of the United States, not by parliamentary processes, but by direct conflict with our governmental machinery in open combat.

It was several months later, and after the duty of administering the "red" deportation laws had fallen upon me, that the question upon which the Preis case turned came, on writs of habeas corpus, before the Federal court at Boston, Circuit Judge Anderson sitting. This court decided, June 23, 1920, that the deportation laws did not give the Secretary of Labor jurisdiction to deport aliens for mere membership in the Communist Party. It was afterwards overruled by the next highest court, but the question has never gone to the Supreme Court of the United States. Before Judge Anderson's decision, Judge Westenhaver, a Federal Judge at Cleveland, had sustained Secretary Wilson's view; / and about four months later, Judge Knox of the Federal Court at New York City disagreed with Judge Anderson and sustained the judgment of Secretary Wilson.

To decide Communist Party cases, in accordance of course with the principle of Secretary Wilson? s decision in the Preis case, became part of my duty when, on the 6th of March, 1920, 1 took charge of the immigration work of the Department of Labor. Since I regarded that decision as a correct application of the law, it was not to me a burdensome precedent. I did, however, find myself burdened in my efforts to determine whether each individual alien charged with being a member of the Communist Party was so in fact. If the evidence showed him to be a member, it was my official duty to order his deportation; if the evidence did not show him to be a member, it was just as much my official duty to cancel the warrant of arrest under which he was in custody or bailed— that is, to release him as an alien resident whose rights of residence had not been forfeited. This duty might have been much less burdensome if I had been willing to disregard the evidence, but I felt bound by my obligations of citizenship and by my oath of office to decide each case according to the evidence and in harmony with the law of the land.

### Chapter XVII: Communist Labor Party Members

Besides alleged members of the Communist Party whose eases were before me in large numbers for decision, there i?ere also before me in like manner numerous cases of aliens alleged to be members of the Communist Labor Party.

Though their names were very much alike, these two party organizations were separate bodies, with distinctively different platforms. As already explained in these pages, both were offshoots from the Socialist Party. The left wing of this party of many election contests from 1887 on, had met in convention at Chicago early in September, 1919, and had there organized the Communist Party and promulgated its platform; whereupon another faction of the Socialist Party, one which had held middle ground between the left wing and the right wing, and which had participated in the convention that organized the Communist Party, repudiated the Communist Party platform and promulgating a distinctive platform, formed a third organization which they named the Communist Labor Party.

Against alien members of this third organization, as well as those of the second from which it had seceded, warrants of arrest were applied for by the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice. Consequently members of the Communist Labor Party to the number of about 300 were arrested and their deportation demanded.

When some of those cases were before me for decision, I concluded that the platform of the Communist Labor Party did not bring that party within the proscribed class in which Secretary Wilson had included the Communist Party. But as warrants of arrest had been issued under the general arrangements that preceded my taking charge of immigration matters, I was of opinion that this essential question should be decided by the Secretary in person. So, pending his action, I postponed decisions in all Communist Labor Party cases.

Meanwhile, as the arrested aliens of this class who were financially able to give bail had done so, and as the absence of the Secretary would subject the others to indefinite imprisonment unless they were released during the interval, I directed that pending his decision the imprisoned ones be liborated "on their own recognizance" — that is, on their respective promises, financially unsecured, to appear if and when called upon to do so. From this action of mine the Commissioner General of Immigration took the precaution of appealing to the Secretary, and I transmitted his appeal; but because I refused to continue the imprisonment of these probably undeportable aliens until the appeal could be heard and had been decided, he formally withdrew it.

Consequent upon my reference to the Secretary for decision of the question of deportability for mere membership in the Communist Labor Party, he directed a joint hearing in one of the cases which squarely presented the issue— the case of Carl Miller. This hearing did not come on for several weeks, owing to the Secretary's unavoidable absence. It was discussed at length on the 24th of April, 1920, counsel for the alien and representatives of the Department of Justice contesting the ) point at issue.

At the close of the hearing, at which I sat with Secretary Wilson, as did also the Solicitor and the Commissioner General of Immigration, Mr. Wilson called me into consultation, asking for my impressions of the case. Replying that it might be better if I did not confer with him until he had gone over its merits in his own mind, since I might be too much prejudiced by my previously formed opinion to be a satisfactory initial conferee, I limited my advice to the suggestion that the Communist Labor Party case hinged upon the question of whether or not the organic law of the organization contemplated violence. About a week later the Secretary called me into consultation on the subject again. On this occasion he told me that after carefully reviewing the case in every detail he had come to the conclusion that the Communist Labor Party was not a proscribed organization under the deportation laws.

In his written opinion he explained that as the only question presented by the record was whether the Communist Labor Party came within the purview of the Act of Congress so as to make aliens who were merely members of it liable to deportation, its membership application card and pledge were the first objects of scrutiny. Examining these, he found no intimation that applicants were required to accept the tactics of the Communist International or of the Communist Labor Party itself except in so far as they were expressed in the constitution, the platform or the program of the party. Turning then to those documents and quoting their declarations, he concluded that while they indicated an u extreme radical objective/* there was nothing in them disclosing an intention to attain the objective by force or violence or any other means incompatible with the use of parliamentary machinery. Mr. Wilson closed his memorandum of decision with the truly American sentiment that "if the American people are left free to discuss and decide the questions presenting themselves for consideration from day to day, uninfluenced by the threat of force or violence, they can be relied upon to protect themselves against any false philosophies, wild-eyed revolutions, or dictatorships of any kind."

### Chapter XVIII: A Clutter of "Red" Records

The most perplexing of all my problems in connection with the "red" crusade was the cluttered condition of the "red" records in the Bureau of Immigration, that ministerial agency which the Department of Labor had to use in the administration of all its alien exclusion and expulsion functions. Upon plunging into this clutter, which had embarrassed the Solicitor to the verge of resigning, I was amazed at the facts disclosed. The whole "red" crusade stood revealed as a stupendous and cruel fake. Had the facts as they were then thrust upon my attention been generally known, public condemnation of the Department of Justice and its co-operating agencies would have been sure and swift.

Warrants of arrest to the number of 5,000 or thereabouts were outstanding. They had been issued in routine by the Solicitor after clerical preparation in and reckless recommendations by the immigration bureau, and arrests had been made under more than half of them. Following the arrests, there had been hearings before examining inspectors of immigration at various immigration stations throughout the country, and reports of the hearings had been forwarded more or less promptly to the Bureau of Immigration at Washington. But the reports had "stuck" in the Bureau.

For weeks upon weeks they accumulated, awaiting transmission to the Office of the Secretary for decisions. Very few had been brought to the atten tion of the Solicitor before his departure. Transmissions to me after he had gone were dilatory.

There was little prospect when I took charge that many would reach me short of a distant future. Meanwhile the arrested aliens — all except those who had been able to give the bail exacted— were in close confinement, closer and less humane in some instances than if they had been serving prison sentences for crimes. And agents of the Department of Justice, contemptuous of the exclusive authority of the Department of Labor and using the machinery of the Bureau of Immigration pretty much at their own will and for their own ends, were dealing dietatorially with the whole subject as if all authority had through some subterranean channel been passed along to them.

A revolting instance which came to my attention on the day I took charge, illustrates more than one aspect of these disorderly conditions. An alien against whom no evidence of any culpability appeared in the record had been arrested by Department of Justice agents in one of the January raids. He was imprisoned ten days without a warrant of arrest. For nearly a week longer he was in prison under a warrant of arrest before he was given the hearing the law required. After the hearing, which dragged on for a week without developing any evidence of culpability, his imprisonment was continued five weeks further while the worthless record of hearing awaited transmission to Washington. When after all this delay the record did reach Washington, the Bureau of Immigration, noting the utter absence of inculpating proof, recommended that the Department of Labor withhold its decision indefinitely for the purpose of affording opportunity for the Department of Justice to hunt for proof. That the bureau also recommended release of the prisoner during this indefinite postponement is true, but upon his own recognizance, a condition which would not only have required him to report periodically to the immigration authorities, but would have kept him under a cloud of suspicion in his community while tbe__detectives were huntLog^Jox^tiie proof without which their arreßt-tJfliim in the firsfplace was defiant of law. Such trifling with personal liberty seemed to me inexcusable, and I cancelled the warrant of arrest.

Not only in that case, but with reference to the whole situation which the Department of Justice and the Bureau of Immigration had between them brought about, my official duty was plain.

At the outset I took measures for clearing up the clutter of deportation records. This was not as easy as one might infer from the absolute authority which the law gave me over the Bureau of Immigration. Bureaucratic habits made stubborn resistance.

Among them was one that afforded a reason qnite distinct from either inefficiency ojr collusion for the cluttered state of the records in the a reason without which neither inefficiency nor collusion could have entered in.
