It lay in the lap of an old practice which the Department of Labor inherited when it inherited the Bureau of Immigration from the Department of Commerce and Labor. I had long regarded the practice as both obstructive and unwarranted by law. So had Secretary Wilson. But there had been no opportunity to abolish it without making matters worse. Deeply rooted in the habits of the immigration service, it could not be blown away on the breath of any magical "presto change. "

Pursuing that practice the Bureau of Immigration made primary decisions in both exclusion and expulsion cases, leaving to the Secretary of Labor or his representative the function of formally approving them. Until recently these decisions had been in the form of recommendations. They were set out in each case in an official memorandum which summarized the facts from the record of hearing, drew conclusions from the facts so summarized, and made recommendations to the Secretary of Labor or his representative, who was expected to turn each recommendation into a decision by "signing on the dotted line" below the word " approved. "

This extra-legal practice would not have been half bad if the recommendations thus prepared for the Secretary's approval could have been depended upon as genuinely those of the efficient subordinates in the bureau who examined the records of hearings and summarized the facts; but very often, much oftener perhaps than was known in the Office of the Secretary, the recommendations were written under arbitrary orders. In the actual circumstances, the Secretary of Labor was in these cases assigned by the Bureau to the role to which an experienced Departmental messenger once upon a time assigned a new Secretary of the Treasury who hesitated to place his signature upon a document before him, muttering to himself, "Let me think; let me think." "Mr. Secretary," said the messenger, "you don't have to think; all you got to do is to sign you' name right there on that line."

As the "red" cases began passing through the Bureau, the Bureau memorandums were altered so as not only to assume powers of primary decision as in the past, but explicitly to assert such powers. This innovation gave to the memorandums of the Bureau of Immigration the specific character of primary decisions. Although the assumption of adjudicating power had no recognition in the law, it was one more step in the creation of a Bureau habit of posing as primary authority ("the trial court," as the Attorney General approvingly characterized it afterwards) which the Secretary of Labor in the role of the "appellate court"— to quote the Attorney General again — might overrule, but at the peril of ignorant criticism for interfering with a rigid administration of the immigration laws. This practice alone may have accounted for the clutter of "red" records I found in the Bureau of Immigration when I took charge of Departmental activities in connection with our immigration laws. For the work of making, reviewing, considering and.\ signing those comprehensive memorandums was ofj course very great. The Commissioner General of Immigration did so account for it. He explained that it was impossible for him to dispose of the records more expeditiously if he gave the requisite care to making decisions. Had there been any shadow of legal authority for his making decisions, that explanation would have been impressive; but apart from the Bureau habit, there was not even an excuse for it.

This habit, however, had continued in immigration cases until the Bureau of Immigration came to regard its chief, and he to regard himself, as a species of immigration court of original jurisdiction. What is more remarkable, there were those among the temporary legal advisers of the Department of Justice in its "red" crusade who also thought of the Commissioner General of Immigration as a court of first instance, or as a "trial court," as the Attorney General characterized it; so did some of the members of the immigration committee of the lower house of Congress. According to them, the Secretary of Labor— and therefore his official representatives, the Assistant Secretary and the Solicitor when acting for the Secretary — was a rubber-stamp wielder, who, while he might by arbitrary administrative power overrule the Commissioner General of Immigration, had no legitimate function with reference to the latter 's "decisions" in deportation cases other than the mechanical one of approving them without thought by signing his name "right there on that line."

Knowing that the then prevailing procedure with reference to deportation cases had no legal excuse for resting upon Bureau "decisions" or recommendations—and therefore that the delays in transmitting expulsion records (delays that were ascribed by the Commissioner General to his inability to give the records the attention necessary for passing judgment upon them any more rapidly than he was doing) were indefensible, I-decidedJLojput_an end to this procedure, andJl did. In consequence the accumulation of records had been greatly reduced by April 6, and by the 9th it was negligible. On the 3d of June the remnants of the accumulation, a scattered few of the delayed records which had been overlooked in the general clean-up, were disposed of. On the last of those dates the warrant-case work at Washington, which had been virtually current for several weeks, became literally so.

In this harassing clear-up work I had the assistance, fortunately, of a loyal, vigilant, acute, able and conscientious official private secretary, Hugh Reid, a man with that "saving sense of humor"

which leads through wit to wisdom. He was aided by my official confidential clerk, Lillian Conrad, whose natural abilities, together with her industry and a training for high grade secretarial worir,-qualified her especially for helping in the X^inergency which our office faced. Nor can I forget the ttoughtful service of my official messenger, Carlos R. Blackwell, a man of exceptional competency, shrewdness, honesty and devotion to duty. With their co-operation I was able to stimulate the transmission of immigration files until the accumulated cases, together with the inflow of new cases, had been decided one by one in accordance with my judgment of the evidence and of the law applicable to each. While the work of clearing up the clutter of records went on, the work of making decisions on the merits of the cases went on with it. I decided more than 1,600 cases before the middle of April, a task which no one could have accomplished without the efficient help I had from my messenger, my confidential clerk and my private secretary. Some days the number of cases to decide ran so high in the scores that they touched the five-score mark. How so huge and exacting a task was performed in so short a time and with so little error that only a few cases have been criticized by even the least tolerant of critics and after meticulous scrutiny, is a story by itself.

### Chapter XIX: Decisions in the "Red" Cases

In any account of the decisions I made in "red" cases after taking Departmental charge of the immigration service in behalf of the Secretary of Labor, it would be impossible to enter into detail without a tedious survey of immigration files running into thousands. Nor could minute detail be of much if any value. In so far as statistics may be important, round figures tell the story minutely enough; and as to the facts involved in individual cases, nothing more is required for a just understanding of the whole experience than a fair explanation of such cases as were either distinctive or typical.

Statistics purporting to be exact with reference to all proceedings against aliens under the "anarchistic provisions of the immigration law" for the period from July 1, 1919, to January 1, 1921, were formulated early in 1921 by the Bureau of Immigration. According to this statement 6,328 warrants of arrest were issued, and of those 2,919 were cancelled and 1,11.9 were sustained and deportations under them ordered. That period began, however, several months before the responsibility for decisions fell upon me and continued several months after the deportations delirium had begun to subside. But the annual report of the Commissioner General of Immigration for the fiscal year from July, 1919, to June, 1920, inclusive, gives data for an intermediate period that coincides pretty closely with the period when I was in charge of warrant-case work. It begins two months earlier and ends when most of the "red" cases had been disposed of. According to this report the number of warrants of arrest issued for "reds" from December 29, 1919, to June 30, 1920, was "in the neighborhood" of 5,000, and the number of these that were executed by the making of arrests was "approximately"

3,000, the arrests being followed by hearings which resulted in deportation orders to the number of 556. and in cancellations of warrants of arrest to the number of 2,202.

Using that report as a guide, with due allowance for the confusion of records in the Bureau and for variations in the period of time covered, and taking my own impressions and rough notations into account, I should think it a probable estimate that somewhat within 6,000 warrants of arrest were issued for alien "reds" by the Department of Labor at the instance of Department of Justice detectives; that somewhat within 4,000 arrests were made, the remainder of those warrants being "thrown into the discard" by the detectives; that about 3,000 wer«» cancelled after hearings, nearly if not quite all by myself; and that out of less than 1,000 deportations ordered after hearings, more than 500 were ordered bv me.

Further statistical light may be shed upon the circumstances by reference to the number and character of the decisions I made in this class of* cases during the first month or so of my activities in connection with the "red" crusade— from March 6 to April 10, 1920. In that period I made about 1,600 decisions, nearly all on charges of membership in the Communist Party; and of those decisions, about 1,140 ordered cancellation of warrants and about 460 ordered deportation.

- My deportation decisions were made because, in my judgment, the evidence in the record proved, with more or less certainty, that in those cases the aliens were within some clause or other of the immigration statutes which demanded deportation. In an overwhelming majority of those cases the necessity for deportation was established only by proof of membership in the Communist Party of America. My cancellation decisions were made because, in my judgment, there was in the records before me no lawful proof to sustain the charges in the warrants of arrest under any provision of the immigration statutes. Of the total of about 3.000 cancelled warrants, it should be explained incidentally that about 300 had been issued on the ground of membership in the Communist Labor Party, and that these were cancelled as matter of course after the Secretary's decision that the Communist Labor Party, unlike the Communist Party, was outside the proscription of the immigration laws.

That my judgment was right in every decision, I could not and would not contend. No government official charged with responsibility for deciding questions of law and fact involving personal rights and the principles of our Government could with any grace insist that every one of nearly 4,000 decisions he had made was a correct decision. The most he could insist upon would be that each decision was right in his own judgment when he made it. Upon this I do insist.

And in all those cases no substantially erroneous decision has yet been specified, except by Judge Anderson of Boston when he decided that mere membership in the Communist Party of America was not a lawful cause for deporting aliens. On this point Judge Anderson's decision was reversed by the appellate court of his Circuit (rather perfunctorily, I am bound to concede), but neither his decision nor its reversal affects any of my cancellation decisions. I had followed the judgment of the Secretary of Labor, as I should have been in duty bound to do even if had disagreed with him, that the Communist Party was a proscribed organization; whereas Judge Anderson decided that the Communist Party was not proscribed. I may therefore repeat that of all the "red" cases I decided, no substantially erroneous decision of mine has yet been specified. Most certainly and without qualification may this be said of my cancellation decisions, and it was for these alone that my official fidelity was clamorously questioned.

Had I been governed in those cancellation decisions by no better motive for making them with extreme caution and the utmost accuracy at my command, my knowledge of the fact that detectives for the Department of Justice, law clerks in the Department of Justice, and politically alert mfembkrs of Congress inspired by special interests, together with coadjutors in the Department of Labor itself, were vigilantly scrutinizing every case in which my decisions were favorable to accused aliens, would have been enough to restrain me from unfaithful wandering. Nobody in my place with even a minimum of discretion would have challenged such a combination by consciously making assailable decisions.

Regardless, however, of any or all of my motives for trying to make unassailable decisions— and I modestly confess to better motives than fear— every attempt to show even one erroneous cancellation decision has utterly failed.

As to one of those decisions, there might be honest, though certainly not intelligent, criticism. It was made in the case of the late Enrique Flores Magon, a Mexican who had lived in the United States for several years and was the father of resident American-born children.

Had I ordered him to be deported to the country whence he came, he would have been promptly shot for Mexican political offenses by the military authorities then in control of Mexico. No Americanminded official who cherishes one of the noblest of his country's traditions, that it is an asylum for the oppressed of all nations, could lightly deport an alien to such a fate even in a period of delirium. Yet Magon would have been subject mandatorily to deportation if at his hearing he had withheld explanations after calling himself an "anarchist."

Replying, however, freely and fully to searching questions by the immigrant inspector who examined him under the Labor Department's warrant of arrest, \he showed himself to be the very opposite of an anarchist of any kind— whether violent or peaceable. A thorough-going believer in government, he lacked the distinctive characteristic of every type of anarchism; for anarchism means government by none, even as monarchism means government by one. 1 Although the kind of government Magon believed in might not appeal favorably to a Hainiltonian, nor for that matter to a Jeffersoniptf, it came nevertheless within the category of^overnmentalism, and therefore not of anarchism.

There being no evidence either way in Magon 's case except his own statements, the only serious question before me was whether or not our deportation laws required deportation of aliens who label themselves "anarchists," though their unimpeached statements of what they believe show them to be governmentalists. As at the time I decided that question, so am I still strongly of the opinion that governmentalists could not be lawfully deported as "anarchists," no matter how they labeled themselves, unless the label was the only evidence in the record. An alien against whom there was no proof of belief in overthrowing the government of the United States or all forms of law by force, could not be lawfully deported under those laws even if his mental processes did confuse anarchism with 1 See "Anarchism" in "What's What in the Labor Movement: A Dictionary of Labor Affairs and Labor Terminology". Compiled by Waldo R. Browne. Published at New York by B. W. Huebsch, Inc., in 1921.

governmentalism in his choice of terms. So I cancelled the warrant of arrest under which the Department of Labor held Magon in custody.

Another of my cancellation decisions that might be criticized in all good faith by the inconsiderate was made in the case of Paul Bosco, a Socialist who, early in the period of our participation in the World War, had been convicted of distributing literature which fell under the inhibition of one of our temporary War laws. For that War-time offense he was sentenced to imprisonment for ten years, a term reduced afterwards by President Wilson to two. Part of the inconsiderate as well as the malignant criticism to which I was subjected for my administration of this case related to the fact that after the alien had been ordered deported by the Solicitor as Acting Secretary before immigration matters came under my charge, and had been sent to New Orleans for deportation to Russia, I ordered that he be brought to New York and given a rehearing. The facts on which those criticisms rested were correct as far as they went, but they were grossly incomplete.

Bosco 's first hearing on deportation charges had been given him in the Atlanta penitentiary while he was still serving sentence for his War offense. He was hundreds of miles from home, possessed of only thirty dollars, and in an unfriendly and depressing environment. Although told he might have counsel if he wished, his formal waiver of counsel in those circumstances seemed to me to have been at the best only barely voluntary. At any rate his freedom to employ counsel was narrow enough to justify a re-hearing at which counsel provided by friends might protect him. Nor could the Government by any possibility be bothered or embarrassed by a second hearing. As to the criticism that I brought him to New York from New Orleans where the immigration bureau had taken him for deportation upon the expiration of his sentence at Atlanta, the transfer to New York had been already ordered by the Solicitor as Acting Secretary; and rightly ordered, for New York was the only port from which Bosco could be deported to the country from which he had come. The Solicitor's order for removal to New York having been suspended at the behest of the Bureau of Immigration, I terminated the suspension because, whatever its purpose, the only effect of its continuance would have been an indefinite, cruel and useless imprisonment of Bosco hundreds of miles away from his friends.

But the important consideration regarding the Bosco case was my decision on its merits after the re-hearing at which Bosco was represented by Harry Weinberger, Esq., of the New York bar. For deportation purposes the only accusation against Mr.

Weinberger's client related to a verbal outburst of emotion of which he was guilty immediately after the sentence for his War-time offense had been pronounced. Angered by the imposition of a prison term of ten years for distributing what he had supposed to be lawful circulars, he exclaimed: " When I come out of prison, I hope to see the red flag flying the stars and stripes. " This exclamation marked the alien as a "red," but there was nothing in the record to show a hostile purpose or opinion, and much to show that there was no other impulse for his exclamation than anger. His verbal outburst, the only evidence against him in the deportation proceedings, was no better proof of belief in overthrowing our Government by force and violence, the only ground on which he could have been deported lawfully, than angry profanity is proof of atheism. As most men's swearing is as meaningless as some men's praying, so this man's " treasonable" exclamation was as meaningless as -some men's patriotic eloquence.

Concluding that Bosco was not subject to deportation on any facts disclosed by the record in his case, I decided to cancel the warrant for his arrest. This decision has been criticized by some persons who, though they reason and reflect, have not seen the record of the case and are not familiar with the deportation laws. It has been denounced by persons less disposed or qualified to reflect reasonably than to censure hysterically or to stigmatize maliciously. But I have yet to be apprised by anyone or in any way of anything in the law or the evidence affecting that case which would have warranted a contrary decision. There have, indeed, been appeals to those mushy "general principles" with which apologists for lynching flippantly defend the lawless mobs they sympathize with. But "general principles" of this savage sort, while convenient enough for excusing mob outrages to the mob-minded, afford no basis for administrative decisions by sworn officers of a government by law and not by lynchers.

A few more of my cancellation decisions in the "red" cases would possibly offer to thoughtful criticism almost as firm a footing as those in the Magon and the Bosco cases. Among them might be counted cases in which I decided that aliens who had renounced beliefs and withdrawn from memberships that would have subjected them to deportation were not deportable under laws enacted after their renunciation and withdrawal. But I am confident that none of my hundreds upon hundreds of cancellations offers any better grounds for criticism than the Magon and the Bosco decisions do. This seems, too, to have been the impression of my most industrious and even of my most malignant critics; for the few other cases cited in support of their criticisms were weaker than these.

Of all my decisions in the "red" cases, the vast majority— a proportion so large as to warrant the statement that there were hardly any others— were in cases involving nothing but membership in the Communist Party of America. There were also about 300 decisions in cases involving nothing but membership in the Communist Labor Party; but I pass the latter by, because the warrants for membership in this organization were cancelled by the Secretary of Labor himself, through his final and unassailable decision that an alien's membership in the Communist Labor Party did not subject him to deportation. Confining, then, my present explanations to decisions on questions of membership in the Communist Party of America, I recall many in which I had to order deportations of aliens whom not even a lynching mob with the least remnant of righteous spirit would have deported from a frontier town.

In those cases also I acted as a responsible, and to the extent of my moral sense as a conscientious, official for a Government of law. The Congressional statutes required me to order deportations in certain specified classes of cases, and I did so regardless either of personal sympathy for the alien or his family or of private judgment of what the law ought to have been. Being in my official capacity a law administrator, not a law maker, I could, in civic conscience and with due regard for my oath of office, no more yield to the importunities of kindly Americans in such cases, than in cases of the opposite type I could yield to the denunciations of embittered provincial politicians or embittering publicity agencies. Two or three of my decisions in cases of harmless yet technically culpable victims of the "red" drive will sufficiently illustrate.

An attorney having made the objection in behalf of two clients that mere membership in the Communist Party was not sufficient ground for deportation, I filed a memorandum in explanation of my decision to deport, in which I wrote: " Alien's attorney is in error in supposing that mere membership in the Communist Party is not sufficient ground for deportation. On the contrary, mere membership is a mandatory reason for deportation. Neither activity in the organization nor actual knowledge of the constitution of the organization is necessary to make deportation mandatory if the alien is knowingly a member. Members are presumed to know the organic laws of the organizations they belong to. In each of these two cases the alien was knowingly a member of the Communist Party. " Another phase of the same question was presented in the case of an alien whose deportation I ordered, explaining my decision in the following memorandum: "Upon being asked at his regular examination before the immigrant inspector when he had joined the Communist Party of America, the alien replied, October 28, 1919. Inasmuch as the unlawful Communist Party was formed in September, 1919, the date of joining indicates a purpose to become a member of the unlawful organization. The case is in a different category from those in which members of the lawful Socialist Party were transferred in groups, unconfirmed by any individual action. In the latter cases the alien would be what in several of the files is described as ' automatically' a member of the unlawful organization.

Mere automatic membership is not within the purview of the Act of October 16, 1918. It is purely technical, and the alien ought not to be deported for it without evidence of his own assent. The same principle applies in the other direction. If conscious application by an individual has been made for membership in the proscribed organization, and the membership is complete substantially, he is deportable even though all technicalities have not been complied with. In such a case the alien is affiliated with, even if he has not yet technically become a member of, the organization. "

Cases like those presented no peculiar difficulty. The fact of conscious membership in the proscribed organization had been proved, and under the law innocency of purpose personally was irrelevant. But in the great majority of cases I had to decide, actual membership was unproved; or else the member's consciousness of being a member was disproved. There were, for instance, a large number of what the Department of Justice detectives called "automatic members," a term that needs explanation. After the Communist Party was organized at Chicago in September, 1919, many local branches of the Socialist Party, from which the Communist Party had seceded, voted to transfer their allegiance from the Socialist Party to the Communist Party. Sometimes the vote of those present was nearly if not quite unanimous; sometimes there was only a majority vote of the members attending the meeting at which the vote was taken; but never was the action indicative of acquiescence by any members but those voting in the affirmative. Often individual members of the lawful Socialist Party were thus transferred to the unlawful Communist Party without the slightest knowledge on their part that the transfer had been made. Many continued paying dues to their respective branches of the lawful Socialist Party, as they supposed, but— owing to their technical transfer— actually into the treasury of the Communist Party. Socialists thus automatically transferred from a lawful to an unlawful organization were denounced by the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice as " automatic members " of the unlawful Communist Party, and, regardless of their knowledge or understanding of the Communist Party, and even of their antipathy to it, their deportation was demanded if they were aliens.

To illustrate my official experience with this phase of the "red" crusade, I quote two of my memoranda of decision, one for cancellation of the warrant of arrest, the other for the alien's deportation. In the cancellation case I officially explained my decision in favor of the alien in these terms: "It is essential to membership in the Communist Party that applicants for such membership shall sign an application card in which they state that they have 'read the constitution and program of the Communist Party,' that they declare their i adherence to the principles and tactics of the Party and the Communist International/ and that they i agree to submit to the discipline of the Party as stated in its constitution,' pledging themselves 'to engage actively in its work.' This document is quoted in such manner by the Secretary of Labor in his Communist Party decision (Preis case, January 24, 1920) as to indicate that the alien's signature to the same is to be regarded as important with reference to the fact of membership. The fair inference is that in his decision the Secretary did not intend to include transmitted membership, unconfirmed by individual assent, as being within the scope of the Act of Congress of 1918. Of course, it is not necessary to prove the signing of the aforesaid application card by actually producing the same. Its having been signed may be inferred from proved circumstances. But when membership in the unlawful Communist Party is to be inferred from transfer of memberships in a group from the lawful Socialist Party, facts must appear which will reasonably justify an inference that the alien has signed the application which the Communist Party exacts as a condition of membership. Such an inference cannot be reasonably justified from the evidence on file in this case."

The essential difference between that case of "automatic membership" and cases of membership transfer in which I decided against the aliens by ordering deportation, is explained in the following memorandum of decision in one of the latter class of cases: "The alien appears to have gone over to the unlawful Communist Party from the lawful Socialist Party knowingly. That is to say, he appears to have known the facts which make the Communist Party an unlawful organization and those which make him a member of it. Whether or not he understood that the facts of which he had knowledge made this organization unlawful is immaterial. Th.e question is one of the difference between error as to fact and error as to law. Violent as the presumption often is, everyone is nevertheless presumed to know the law. But knowledge of essential facts must be proved. In this case the record shows that the alien knew the facts which make the Communist Party unlawful, and also the facts which make him a member of it."

Still another class of perplexing cases were those in which the alien, having been a technical member of the Communist Party, had withdrawn. In such cases it seemed to me that the decisive consideration was the intention in joining, coupled with the motive for withdrawing. It was explained in a memorandum filed with one of my decisions, in which I wrote: ' * This alien did join the Communist Party, but upon being informed later by a friend that it was 'no good' he quit the Party and tore up his membership card. His withdrawal appears to have been under circumstances indicating good faith. The question which therefore arises is whether or not, under the Act of October 16, 1918, the peculiar doctrine of 'once a member always a member* applies. By the language of the -Statute,- if construed with wooden literalness, any alien who had once become a member of an organization such as the Department holds the Communist Party to be, would forever thereafter be subject to deportation for that reason— even if he abandoned the organization before the next day's sun had risen on his membership. Such a legislative intention is unthinkable in the absence of language in the statute so definite as to leave no room for interpretation. A member who in fact withdraws from a proscribed organization in good faith—that is, not for the purpose of serving it secretly in other ways or merely of escaping: deportation upon detection, but because he honestly renounces its objects or discovers the organization to be unlawful, whereas he had been deceived into supposing it to be lawful— comes no longer within the purview of the statute. In support of this interpretation, if support of an interpretation so obvious be needed, it may be observed that the proscribed membership is not criminal, a doctrine with reference to expulsions under the immigration laws which has been too well established by court decisions to admit of question. ) It follows that deportation is not a punishment fo'fa past offense, as the penalties of criminal statutes are; its only purpose is to expel aliens whose societary affiliations, etc., make them obnoxious. \ Like a resident alien who has recovered from a-centagious disease, an alien member of the Communist Party who quits it under circumstances indicating good faith cannot be deported for having theretofore brought himself within the plirview of the law by joining that party. "

### Chapter XX: Principles of Decision

The "red" cases were not decided upon the spur of a moment. All were put to the test of carefully prepared principles of decision. I prepared an outline of the principles to guide me, doing so early in the period during which official responsibility rested upon me for administering the immigration laws, and throughout that period those principles were strictly applied to each case.

To begin with I placed the Communist Party of America within the proscription of the supplementary Act of Congress of 1918, which made membership in organizations of its character a mandatory cause for deportation.

Accordingly I provided in the principles of decision that aliens who were proved to be members of the Communist Party consciously should be deported. ( As proof of membership I required, in harmony Srith the Secretary's decision in the Communist Party test case, that as a principle of decision there must be evidence in each case of personal signing of the application for membership which the Communist Party exacted, such signing to be supplemented by facts or circumstances indicating membership subsequent to the creation of the Party.

But signing by another, if with the authority of the" 1 alleged member, was, of course, to have the same effect as personal signing, provided authority to sign and understanding of the purpose of such authority were proved.

Another of my principles of decision was to the effect that applications for membership not confirmed by proof of acceptance were of no value as proof of membership unless membership were indicated by proof of membership behavior. And inasmuch as many applications for membership in the Communist Party had been signed before its organization, and therefore before it could have brought its members, merely as members, within the proscription of the deportation laws, I formulated in this connection the additional principle that signed applications antedating the formation of the Communist Party would not be regarded as in themselves constituting proof of membership nor of application for membership in the Communist Party, unless unlawful by their own terms or in their legitimate implications. The alien's name in a membership list was not in itself to be regarded as satisfactory proof of membership for deportation purposes. There must be facts or circumstances tending to show that it was there by his authorization. As to "automatic membership "— transfer of members of a legitimate organization to an illegitimate one by the vote of a branch of the former transferring its whole membership — my principles of decision required that this be not considered as subjecting to deportation any alien so transferred without proof of his own activities or declarations indieating knowledge of the transfer and of the illegitimate character of the organization to which his branch of the legitimate organization had voted to transfer him.

Regarding withdrawals of membership, I adopted the principle that when a member of a proscribed organization had withdrawn under circumstances establishing good faith, he was not subject to deportation for mere membership.

Two other principles were embodied in my formulation. They bore upon all varieties of deportation cases, not alone upon questions of mere membership in a proscribed organization, and had reference especially to lawless conduct by Department of Justice detectives in making arrests. One of them was to the effect that statements by the accused alien, whether oral or in writing, made while he was in custody, without opportunity fairly afforded him from the beginning to be protected in his lawful rights by counsel, and without clear warning that his statements might be used against him, would be disregarded by me in deciding his case. This^principle rested specifically upon a Federal court decision in Montana, made by Judge Bourquin in a deportation case, and a tTnitecTStates Court decision made in connection with a criminal case. The second of those two principles was to the effect that exhibits seized upon the premises or the person of the arrested alien without lawful process, would also be disregarded. It rested upon the same court decisions as the first.

Before passing on to the last of my formulated principles of decision, I must state certain special circumstances. /Among the aliens proved to be members of the Communist Party were many whose deportation would have imposed cruelties that no consistent American could contemplate with satisfaction, and that most Americans would abhor. Nor did their cases involve criminal or otherwise intolerable conduct on their part. These victims of the cruelties incident to deportation were not bombthrowers, nor associates of such, nor sympathizers with them. They were as a rule industrious men, of worth in the community, who were accustomed to working their passage honestly and usefully through life. It was with no improper motives that they had become members of the Communist Party. They thought of it as a social club, or as an organization offering educational facilities to adult aliens, or as a political party of the toy variety, or as a body to which their friends belonged. So they joined, and paid their dues, and performed the ordinary functions of society members, and participated in festivals and musical entertainments and amateur theatricals; or, not taking their membership seriously, they staid at home when not at their places of work. Suddenly such men found themselves hustled off in groups to jail, "third-degreed" by detectives, held incommunicado by the police, tried before immigrant officers for an offense they could not understand as an offense, and condemned to banishment from the United States— to be deported to the country whence they had long ago come, back to places once familiar but now grown strange— condemned to separation, probably forever, from wife and from children of American birth and citizenship, L bad »o^ alternative under the law but to order their deportation if proof of membership m the Communist Party were clear, however innocent the individual might have been. JBiOLdfiteimii^ in sudLcases the proof of membership must be as clear as crystal before ""{Rose accidentaT victims of a silly delirium were deported by an authorization of mine. S6*T"added"TEs final clause to my outline of principles of decision: "In casein which the alien is the father of children born in the United Stafevaad therefore Constitutionally American citizens^^and who are dependent upon and receive from him parental support, eve^Taxr^3oubFregarding membership within the purview.oLthe_statnte will be accorded. "

Following that outline of principles of decision, I decided as consistently as I could, the 3,700 to 4,000 "red" cases which it became my official duty to decide, ordering about 700 aliens to be deported and cancelling (in addition to the 300 Communist Labor Party warrants that were cancelled by instructions from Secretary Wilson), about 2,700 of the warrants under which the regular hearings in the "red" cases had been held.

### Chapter XXI: Routine Methods

The task of making decisions in these cases was so great as a whole that I could not possibly have accomplished it but for the help of Hugh Reid, the official private secretary to the Assistant Secretary of Labor. In an official association of more than two years I had found that I could rely upon Mr. Reid implicitly, both as to fidelity and as to judgment. A law student of rare legal sense combined with rarer common sense, and an organizer and administrator of exceptional skill, he had served in the Office of the Secretary with high efficiency during much of the stressful period of the World War and in circumstances that demanded extraordinary qualifications and devotion to official duty. His service during the period of the "red" deportations decisions ranked especially high.

When I had outlined the principles upon which to make those decisions, I directed Mr. Reid to examine the records of hearings and to advise me with reference to individual cases, distinguishing the doubtful ones from those regarding which there was no room for doubt. In some he advised cancellation of the warrants of arrest; in some he advised deportation of the arrested aliens; some he reported upon as demanding my personal examination. At first I scrutinized all the records personally, T>ut finding that Mr. Reid's advice in cases he did not regard as doubtful was in harmony with the principles of decision which I had outlined, or "on all fours" with decisions I had made, I thereafter took the responsibility, without scrutinizing the records myself, of deciding in all that class of cases as he advised. Those, however, that he reported as doubtful, I scrutinized myself. This method was quite as lawful and very much more dependable than the old Bureau routine.

Besides scrutinizing the cases that Mr. Reid reported upon as doubtful, I personally examined enough more of the records to make an aggregate of perhaps a third of all. These did not come under his inspection at all. He could not examine every record any more than I could. But he examined a daily quota aggregating about twothirds, and I the rest. By that division of labor, both of us working at night as well as in the daytime, on Sundays as well as on other days, with the assistance also of my confidential clerk, Miss Conrad, and my messenger, Mr. Blackwell, I was able —in addition to directing all the other administrative work of the Department of Labor during the absence of the Secretary and the Solicitor, in so far as it required Departmental direction — to make decisions in "red" warrant cases at the rate of from 25 to 100 a day.

This routine continued until the obstructive accumulation of records of deportation hearings in the Bureau of Immigration had been disposed of and the inflowing tide was no longer dammed up in its passage to the Office of the Secretary of Labor. Consequently captives unable to give bail were promptly freed from imprisonment if innocent of the deportation charges against them, or ordered deported if the charges were lawfully proved.

Though the deportation orders were not in all instances executed promptly, the fault was largely due to lack of transportation to the countries whence the expelled aliens had come. Meanwhile, unless reasonable cause for exacting high bail appeared, the deportees were released temporarily on fair bail. Some were released on their own promises to appear when called for; some on the promises of responsible friends; some, as at Detroit, on the word of citizens of good character interested in relieving the aliens of hardship and the community of dishonor. In the course of our routine work we frequently came across cases of special hardship resulting from reckless arrests or careless detentions. One such case has been already mentioned in the course of my narrative. Among the others was one 3n which the alien had been arrested early in January and detained in default of $2,500 bail, which was $1,500 in excess of the Secretary's requirement. Down to the latter part of February no record of hearing had reached the Office of the Secretary, and the man was then still in jail. In another case the alien had been arrested early in January. His hearing was had soon after his arrest, but as late as the last of March the record of hearing had not been transmitted to the Office of the Secretary. An alien who had been arrested on the 7th of November, 1919, and whose hearing took place ten days later, was still in jail in the latter part of February. Another who had been taken into custody under a Department of Labor warrant soon after the 19th of January, 1920, was still in custody and no record of hearing had been received as late as the middle of March. In this case the warrant of arrest had been issued without any evidence of probable cause, and although it exacted bail in $1,000 the alien was being held in default of $2,500. The record of another case in which there was no proof of probable cause on which to base a warrant of arrest, showed an inadequate substitute for such proof in the following letter: "I am in receipt of a telegram from the Hartford, Conn., office of the Bureau of Investigation requesting warrants for the following aliens." The name of this alien was included in the list and the letter was signed by a Special Assistant to the Attorney General. Although the warrant issued upon that farcical proof of probable cause fixed the bail at $1,000, the alien was in custody for more than a month in default of bail in $5,000.

Those instances are only samples. The number of persons held in custody for long periods in default of exorbitant bail, sometimes without hearings, often in consequence of the clutter of records passing through the Bureau of Immigration, and frequently despite their innocence, was not meager.

Their suffering was incidental to a policy that can be excused only as a symptom of delirium.

### Chapter XXII: Deportations Directed

The number of deportations directed from January 1 to June 30, 1920, is specified in the report of the Bureau of Immigration for the fiscal year 1919-1920 as 556. This figure is probably not far wrong. It may be exactly right, although I should guess the number to be at least 100 more. It differs but slightly, however, from the the number reported incidentally by the Bureau for the period from January 1 to June 15, 1920, which was 562. The incidental report classified the cases according to causes for deportation, making, however, only two classes. From this classification it appears that of those 562 deportation decisions— nearly all of which I made, the Solicitor having made very few before turning the immigration work over to me — the number deported for mere membership in the Communist Party of America was 530. Those deported as " anarchists " and members of the "Russian Workers " are undistinguished in the report but they total only 32. In no instance was it shown that the offending aliens had been connected in any way with bomb-throwing or bomb-placing or bomb-making. No explosives were discovered, nor trace of any, nor any firearms except four pistols personally owned and some guns in the "property room"

of an amateur theatrical group.

One important case illustrates the general character of the Communist Party membership cases in which* I ordered deportation. The alien was John Dudinsky. The warrant of arrest had been issued upon the affidavit of a special agent of the Department of Justice charging, on information and belief, that the alien was a member of the Communist Party. At the immigration hearing he refused to answer crucial questions in the absence of counsel. The only evidence in support of the cause alleged for deportation proceedings was (a) a type-written copy, unsigned and in every other respect unproved, of what purported to be an examination of the alien made by a special agent of the Department of Justice while the alien was in the agent's custody; (b) a translation of a letter purporting to have been signed by the alien but without proof that the translation was correct or that the original signature was the signature of the alien; (c) a typewritten copy of the information-and-belief affidavit on which the warrant of arrest had been issued; (d) Communist Party membership cards in alien's name but without proof connecting him with them, beyond such inferences as might be drawn from their apparent genuineness and their being in his name; (e) a photostat copy of correspondence in alien's name but without proof of its genuineness; (f) typewritten protocols of the Communist Party without proof of their genuineness or alien's responsibility for them. On this record I concluded that notwithstanding the weakness of the proof against the alien there seemed to be sufficient lawful evidence in the record to put him to the defense of his right to continued residence in this country, and therefore I decided to open the case for further hearing. At the rehearing the alien still refused to answer questions, acting under advice of counsel, and I then ordered that he be deported as an alien member of the Communist Party.

A somewhat similar case, the original hearing in which I likewise opened for further proof, was that of Roman K. R. Consega) The warrant of arrest had beenlssued upon the affidavit of a special agent of the Department of Justice— one of the private detectives temporarily employed by that Department—charging the alien with being a member of the Communist Party. It was verified upon information and belief only, and at the hearing the evidence introduced in proof of the alien's alleged membership consisted of nothing but a mimeograph copy of the information-and-belief affidavit on which the warrant had been issued, and of a typewritten copy of what purported to be an examination of the alien by a special agent of the Department of Justic — also a private detective in that Department's temporary employment,— while the alien was in the incommunicado custody of the detective. This copy was unsigned, not sworn to, and unattested in any way. As evidence of anything but the inefficiency or worse of the officials who treated it as proof of the alien's deportability, it was as worthless as a blank sheet of foolscap would have been. If a resident alien could be unlawfully arrested as this one was, be brought unlawfully to a hearing, be there accused of unlawful conduct, and be then deported because he refused to answer questions asked by his law-breaking inquisitors, no one, whether alien or citizen, would long be immune from similar outrages. Deciding, therefore, that the alien was under no obligation to answer the questions he had refused to answer, I ordered a rehearing for prima facie proof of the alleged cause for deportation, and upon the merits after rehearing I cancelled the warrant of arrest.

Among my deportation orders there was one which I felt bound to revoke after deportation had been actually effected. The alien was Julius Maninger, a young man of twenty-four who had come from Austria in 1909 and had lived here ever since. He had been arrested at Fort Wayne, Indiana, on the 2d of January, 1920, in connection with the nation-wide January raids. Four days later the warrant for his arrest issued from the Department of Labor. The charge against him was membership in the Communist Party. At his hearing before an immigration examiner five days after his arrest, the alien testified that he was an upholsterer and owned property worth about $1,275; that he had never been out of this country since his arrival in 1909, and that in 1916 he had made application for citizenship. No evidence against him was produced, but he was asked whether he belonged to the Communist Party and he replied: "No, I don't; but I unconsciously affiliated myself with it— that is, at the time a speaker was there and after the speech they held a vote, took up a vote, and I voted for it. 1 was undecided at the time; I thought I would decide later on, knowing full well I could always back out if I didn't agree with the principles. Since then I have not made any application for membership in that organization."

With that statement on the record, the examiner pursued his inquiry, egged on by a Department of Justice detective, until from sheer weariness due to repeatedly denying conscious membership in the Communist Party in response to monotonous questions, and to explaining again and again the circumstances which proved his innocence, and being denied counsel to protect his rights, the alien finally assented to a formulated statement which the immigrant examiner put to him and which involved a formal admission of membership which was no admission at all when his entire testimony was taken into account.

Deceived by the alien's categorical answers, supposing his to be a case in which a false denial at first had been withdrawn and membership in the Communist Party unqualifiedly admitted, I made what any fair reader of the examination will agree was an erroneous decision, for I ordered that the alien be deported. He was accordingly deported, but several months afterward the Advisory Committe 1 which I had meanwhile organized in the De1 In harmony with my judgment formed long before, that the Secretary of Labor when acting in immigration cases was not a perfunctory reviewing officer, but that he had exclusive responsibility — primary, intermediate and final, — I climaxed my strenuous experience in deciding the "red" cases by securing the creation, toward the close of the fiscal year 1920, of an partment called my attention to the case in a carefully considered memorandum.

The alien had written from Austria asking permission to return to the United States. His letter Advisory Committee in the Office of the Secretary to act upon immigration cases in behalf of the Secretary. This Committee performed the advisory service theretofore performed by the Bureau of Immigration, but under the instructions and immediate supervision of the Secretary of Labor and with no pretense or appearance of authority to make decisions or to transmit recommendations having the color of decisions. Thereafter until the close of Secretary Wilson's administration of the Department of Labor, all quasi judicial authority in both exclusion cases on appeal and expulsion cases under warrants of arrest was exercised by the Secretary of Labor or his lawful representative, with the advice of his own chosen staff, the functions of the Bureau of Immigration being limited in these matters strictly to ministerial duties. A more complete explanation may be found at pages 73-76 of the Annual Report of the Department of Labor for 1920. The personnel of the Advisory Committee thus created was as follows during the remainder of Secretary Wilson's administration: Walter D. Davidge, chairman; Bryce Edwards, expert in contract labor cases; Charles E. Booth, expert in Chinese cases; and George W. Stillson. At the close of Secretary Wilson's -term, his successor, James J. Davis, appointed Edward J. Henning to the office of Assistant Secretary (from which my resignation, conditioned to take effect with the retirement of President Wilson, had been accepted by the President prior to the inauguration of his successor), and Assistant Secretary Henning soon afterwards dispensed with the Advisory Committee and went back to the former practice. But after a few months' experience with that practice he re-established the advisory committee plan under more favorable conditions than those with which I had been obliged to contend. The plan has since been extended by Secretary Davis and Assistant Secretary Henning with the aid of Congressional legislation in what I regard as the right direction, its tendency being to separate ministerial functions from quasi- judicial functions — the sheriff from the judge. Under the new "Congressional iegislation, enacted in 1922, the office of Second Assistant Secretary of Labor was created and its first incumbent, Robert Carl White, was immediately assigned to the chairmanship of the advisory body, then called the Secretary's Board of Review. As originally constituted the personnel of this Board, in addition to the chairman, was as follows: Terence V. Powderly, Albert E.- Reitzel and George W. Bope, with Carlos R. Blackwell as confidential messenger. having been referred to the Advisory Committee for a recommendation, the Committee submitted a memorandum for my official signature from which I^herejji^^i^conclusion: "This little creature testified, confronted by two officers of the Government, before the inspector who framed his answers for him and a suggestive agent of the Department of Justice, the former persistently ignoring the alien's reason for which he said he was a member or believed in the principles, and the latter suggesting that the qualification be striken entirely from the record. Clearly the alien was right when he threw up his hands and explained he might as well go one way as the other. He was destined to walk the plank anyhow. The charges in the warrant are not sustained and cannot be established by such a low, vulgar travesty on justice as this record presents. ' ' The memorandum from which this quotation is taken was prepared and vouched for in behalf of the Advisory Committee by W. D. Davidge, Esq., a member of the Bar of the District of Columbia, who was at the time the chairman of the Committee. Upon considering the recommendation of the Advisory Committee and re-examining the record of the case, I realized the error I had made in ordering the deportation of this alien and decided to rectify it if I could. But there were almost insuperable technical difficulties in the way. Under the expulsion law no one could give the deported alien permission to return to this country, and his returning would have been a crime. I therefore laid the matter before the Acting Solicitor, Herbert B. Collins, for advice. In co-operation with one of his official associates, Albert E. Reitzel, Mr. Collins took the case into careful consideration. He was First Assistant Solicitor, a good lawyer and a cautious adviser; Mr. Reitzel was Second Assistant Solicitor, a man of democratic ideals, and also a well trained lawyer. They prepared and submitted to me a memorandum which I adopted and signed.

Reciting that my original decision had "been based upon a misapprehension of the evidence in the case," and that this "evidence upon re-examination" proved "to be wholly insufficient to justify a decision of deportation," their memorandum closed with the following order to which my signature gave effect: "The case is hereby opened, the evidence reconsidered and the warrant of arrest cancelled. A warrant of deportation having been made on the basis of such warrant of arrest, the said warrant of deportation is for the same reason also cancelled. The alien having been actually deported pursuant to said warrant of deportation, it is hereby directed that he be advised of this decision and that application is hereby granted for alien to apply for admission within a year, and upon such application that he shall be admitted provided he is lawfully admissible in all other respects than such as apply to the fact of his having been deported." Of this decision the alien was notified by Departmental letter dated February 4, 1921.

Not only did the record in this case necessitate reversal of my original decision, but it yery well illustrates the "head hunting" spirit in which most of these cases were managed by detectives of the Department of Justice, and how through their unwarranted but dreaded domination, field officials of the Bureau of Immigration were influenced.

A curious phase of the "red" crusade is presented by another deportatkHMlecision of mine. The alien involved was Robert Elia, arrest was in some mysterious way connected with that of Salsedo, the prisoner who was sensationally killed in a drop from a high building to the street in New York. When I examined the Elia file early in May, 3920, I found nothing in it but a Bureau memorandum, a copy of an immigration officer's telegram for a warrant of arrest, a copy of the warrant dated more than two weeks earlier, and a telegram authorizing Elia's arrest and his delivery to an immigration station. The Elia case was wrapped in mystery. Although the detective division of the Department of Justice professed to regard Elia as a bomb-plotter and important in this connection as a witness, that Department was apparently more anxious, as the subsequent record shows, to get him out of the country than into the dock or the witness c hair of a criminal court. The evidence before me showed Elia to be an " anarchist " of a type not indicated, but a "disbeliever in all organized government" and "opposed to all organized government."

Those were the grounds, the only grounds, on which his deportation was or could have been ordered in accordance with any evidence before the Department of Labor, and it was on those grounds only that I ordered his deportation.

If the records in all the 562 "red" cases in which I ordered deportation were minntely scrutinized it would be difficult, even if it were possible, to select any more culpable ones than those I have described in this Chapter. In nearly every deportation case the record disclosed nothing but some variety of proof of mere technical membership in one or another organization which the Secretary of Labor had held to be within the proscription of the alien deportation laws. And in most of {hose cases it was apparent that the alien had neither suspicion nor cause for suspicion that the organization was unlawful. There were few cases in which any reason appeared in the record for inferring guilty knowledge on the part of the alien, and in most of those in which such an inference would have been reasonable there was no other ground for the inference than that the alien held office in the organizatio — usually the office of secretary for a subordinate unit.

In addition to the preponderating proportion of deportations for membership, there were a few cases in which the alien was proved to hold "anarchistic" opinions. But in all these in which the proof was at all clear, the "anarchistic" opinions were of the extreme pacifist type. A single instance of any anarchistic opinion of the violent revolutionary type was as great a curiosity as a "sport" in a herd of cattle.

Quite regardless, however, of their personal guilt aliens who were proved to be anarchists in any sense of that word, or actual members of the proscribed organizations, were mandatorily subject to deportation. In such cases I had no discretion, however innocuous the alien might be. Consequently, and guided by the principles of decision I have already summarized, I decided to deport in every case in which it appeared that the alien was within the scope of the alien deportation statutes, though he were only technically so.

Those decisions evoked no denunciations from influential "quarters. Naturally not, for the deportations delirium was then at its peak. Or, may be it was respect for law that inspired such tolerance toward the deportation 6f harmless aliens who had offended without knowing it. But when the proof did not bring the alien within the deportation statutes, denunciations whirled toward me like cyclones for deciding against deportation.

### Chapter XXIII: Deportations Denied

Although I tried to test each case in the enormous mass, by the principles of decision which are summarized in a former Chapter, a more complete understanding of my cancellation decisions, those in which I deny deportation, may be obtained by considering a particular case which seemed to me at the time of mj r decision, and seems to me still, to have been iypical of nearly all the cases in which I decided in favor of the arrested aliens.

While the Bureau of Immigration was still gesticulating with "decisions" disguised as "recommendations, ' ' but so thinly disguised that its phrase "therefore the Bureau finds and so decides" emphasized its adjudicating assumption, an attache of the Office of the Secretary who had been detailed to the Bureau at its request for the purpose of expediting transmissions of records of hearings, informed me in official confidence of a significant incident. It had to do with the typical case of which I am about to tell.

When the record of this case reached the Bureau of Immigration on its way to the Office of the Secretary of Labor for decision, it was caught in the clutter of other deportation records that were making the same snail-pace journey. Upon its emergence it was referred first to my informant, the attache who had been detailed to the Bureau from the Office of the Secretary. A trained and experienced lawyer, this gentleman, upon reading the record, was strongly impressed with the innocence of the alien. Being also a man of conscience and fearless he wrote a memorandum recommending cancellation of the warrant. As the record with that memorandum passed from him on its journey to the Office of the Secretary, it was stopped in the Bureau of Immigration and referred to another attache. The stoppage lasted several days, a memorandum adverse to cancellation and directing deportation being meanwhile made.

When those circumstances came to my knowledge, I ordered immediate transmission of the record to the Office of the Secretary. Though this order was complied with promptly enough, the record did not reach me, owing to anterior delays, until early in April—nearly three months after the arrest and about two months after the hearing at the immigration station. By that time I had decided more than a thousand "red" cases in which the arrests were groundless, besides several hundred of those in which there was more or less inculpatory proof; and as this case appeared to be typical of most of those in which I had cancelled the warrants of arrest, I accompanied my decision with an extended explanatory statement, which was published in full in May, 1920, by the National Popular Government League of Washington of which Judson King was then as he still is the executive secretary. The name of the alien in this typical case was Thomas Trus^J In the record of his hearing there was no evidence of any unlawful conduct, declaration or opinion, except membership in the Communist Party. His deportation hinged, therefore, solely upon the question of membership. Was he or was he not a member of the Communist Party within the meaning of the "red" deportation law?

Mr. Truss had authorized a Communist Party organizer to sign an application for membership in his behalf. But this occurred several weeks before the Communist Party adopted the manifesto which outlawed its alien members. The organization itself did not yet exist. Of course no proxysigning nor authority for it could, in those circumstances, have any effect under the deportation law, unless confirmed in some way by the alien after the organization of the Communist Party.

The alien's understanding at the time of authorizing his application for membership was also important. He supposed, according to his own testimony, and this was consistent with the circumstances, that he was about to join an organization for socializing mines, railroads, etc., and thereby lowering prices as the Government postoffice had lowered postage.

Following his application for membership in the prospective Communist Party, Truss associated friends with him in the city of his residence to form a branch of the still non-existent Communist Party. Upon the actual organization of that party, this inchoate branch received a charter from it. But the branch postponed acceptance of the charter pending receipt of a copy of the constitution of the main body or the appearance of an organizer to explain the purpose and methods of the organization. As neither constitution nor organizer came, the branch dissolved and returned the charter— all in complete ignorance of the unlawful declarations of the general organization.

Upon those facts I decided that Mr. Truss was not a member of the Communist Party. This decision was the principal asserted cause for the attempt to impeach me which followed close upon my cancellation of the warrant of arrest against Mr, Truss.

Not alone in technical insufficiency of proof, but in several other respects as well, was the Truss case typical. Mr. Truss was an alien (a Pole by birth) and unnaturalized, slightly over thirty years of age, a resident of the United States for twelve or thirteen years, domiciled in Baltimore with his wife and three American-born children, an elder in the Polish Presbyterian Church of Baltimore, a coat-presser by occupation, a trusted leader in lawful labor organizations of his city, a man of good reputation in his community. Yet he was arrested without warrant and subjected to a police office inquisition by a secret service agent of the Department of Justice, who afterwards preferred charges against him in deportation proceedings. The original arrest had been made by a police trick. Truss was asked politely by two "plain clothes men" to go with them to a police station for the purpose of giving information about some matter regarding which he was supposed to be able to help, and he assented. Told that the errand would detain him but a few minutes, he expected to return directly and therefore left no word for his family. Upon arriving at the police station, he was unceremoniously thrust into a cell where he was detained incommunicado until far into the next day, his family and friends being meanwhile frantic over his mysterious disappearance.

During the second day of his confinement a Department of Justice detective subjected him in his cell to the police inquisition on the basis of which the warrant of arrest in deportation proceedings was issued two days later, he remaining meanwhile unlawfully imprisoned. Being then arrested lawfully lie remained in prison, but not incommunicado, until released on bail somewhat more than a week after his original arrest.

Not in all its details, of course, was this case typical. But it was typical in general outline of a large proportion of all the "red" cases I decided after hearings under Labor Department warrants of arrest issued on^grima facie, proof of probable cause furnished by secret service agents of the Depart of Justice. The alien "reds" had been arrested, most of them in or in connection with the January raids, and forthwith imprisoned incommunicado; while so imprisoned each was subjected to a police-office inquisition, that is, to coercive questionings by their captors after the police customs of mid-European autocracies; on the basis of their reported answers, information-and-belief affidavits of probable cause to justify an arrest were made and forwarded to the Department of Labor; thereupon the Department of Labor issued a warrant of arrest in each case, took over the custody of the alien "red," and proceeded as usual in warrant cases under any of the expulsion clauses of the immigration laws. When the hearings at immigration stations in the cases of alien "reds" were reported by the examining inspector through the Bureau of Immigration to the Secretary of Labor, verbatim (as the rules required the reports to be), T found upon officially examining them that there was no better proof to justify deportation in a large percentage of the cases than there was in the Truss case, which I have outlined above and characterized as typical.

In some the membership status was " automatic, " the alien having been transferred from a lawful organization to the unlawful one by vote of a group or branch of the former and without his knowledge. In some the alien had knowledge of the transfer, but none at all of the character or purpose of the organization to which he had been automatically transferred. In others he had signed an application for membership before the unlawful organization existed and had never confirmed membership by any expression or conscious act. Sometimes an organizer or friend had, without the alien's knowledge, signed the application in his behalf but he had never confirmed it. In the great majority of all the cases, personal culpability even to the extent of knowledge of the transfer in the " automatic" membership instances was whollv lacking The records seldom showed any cause whatever for deportation other than a purely technical one. It seemed to me at the time, and the impression has been deepened by subsequent developments, that if there were any alien conspirators in the United States who were at all dangerous to its institutions, its free institutions, the detectives of the Department of Justice did not "hit their trail."

As a rule the hearings showed the aliens arrested to be workingmen of good character, who had never been arrested before, who were not anarchists nor revolutionists nor obnoxious to the spirit of our laws in any other sense. Many of them were faithful fathers of American-born children. Nearly all had been subjected to arbitrary arrest, to long detention in default of bail beyond the means of hardworking wage-earners to furnish, and for nothing more reprehensible, so far as intent counted, than affiliating with friends of their own race, country and language. Cases in which there was substantial proof of any unlawful act with sinister intent or guilty knowledge were exceptions— very rare exceptions.

### Chapter XXIV: Distinctive Cases

Although most of the cases in which I cancelled the warrants of arrest were as ill-founded as the typical case described in the preceding chapter, there were a few about which genuine differences of opinion might be possible — especially in a period of delirium. Among these was one of a type so different from the possibly doubtful two told of in Chapter XIX that it should be given a place in my narrative..._...■.-.^-'== \ It was the case of Louis Wirth, \ young man of German birth, twenty-two jrears^tfld, who had come to the United States at the age of fourteen. Five years after his arrival he entered the Chicago University as a student, having graduated from a high school in Nebraska. At the University he received his degree with special reference to his qualifications as a student of social science. This was six months before his arrest. During his educational period he earned his living as a teacher and also as a field investigator in the Market Analysis Department of the Chicago Daily News; and for the six months immediately preceding his graduation ho worked in one of the departments of the American Red Cross. Immediately following his graduation he took up work in the Bureau of Personal Service at Chicago in the boys' division. The superintendent of this Service, Minnie F. Low, a Chicago woman of impeccable Americanism, described him as "the personification of obedience to law and order," whose good character and competency had led to his assignment to the Boys ' Court of Chicago, where he u worked in perfect co-operation and harmony with the court and all persons connected with it, and did so in a whole-hearted way." So keen was his insight and his sympathetic understanding of the human problems coming to his notice, as his superintendent further described him, and so unusual his "ability and conscientious devotion to duty," to quote her language further, that "in the short period of six months several promotions were given him," up to the grade of senior in his division. He had intended to become an American citizen, but before reaching the naturalization age he was barred by the War and his German nativity. By the professor of history under whom he had studied at the University, he was described as a man of superior mental qualities whose habits were regular, whose self-discipline and his tolerance of the opinions of others commanded respect, and in whom during three years' association the professor had, as he wrote, "never discovered the least trace of addiction to the methods of violence."

Young Wirth was arrested in the Chicago raiding of January and held in custody at a police station without warrant, and for five full days without even being "booked." Meanwhile his friends were ignorant of his whereabouts, and from neglect he suffered hardships that nearly culminated in his death. A Department of Labor warrant of arrest was issued at last, and under this he was bailed and given the regular examination before an immigrant inspector.

The only charge against Wirth before the inspector was membership in the Communist Party, and to support this charge no evidence was produced except his replies to questions put to him at an unlawful and secret inquisition by his custodian during his lawless incarceration at the police station. At his lawful examination before the immigrant inspector, Wirth was asked whether or not he had ever been a member of the Communist Party. He replied: "One time I thought I was; Ido not know whether I am or not."

Being further questioned, and with special reference to his alleged responses to his custodian's questions at the unlawful and secret police-office inquisition, he explained that he had been at one or two meetings of the Communist Party some time in September or October, 1919, and thought he had paid in as much as fifty cents, but did not know what the payment was for. A friend had paid it for him and he had reimbursed his friend.

Asked if he believed in the principles of the Communist Party, he replied that he "could not say"

that he did. But questioned more specifically and with direct reference to the objectionable Communist documents, he answered: "I do not believe that all means should be used to gain the victory of the workers. I believe that all lawful means should be used to gain victory for the working classes. To obtain that victory by means of force or violence, I would seriously object to that."

Asked if he believed in the principles of the manifesto and program of the Communist Party of America, he replied: "No, sir."

To further questions by the immigration examiner, young Wirth explained his superficial membership in the Communist Party in these words:

"I was a student of social and political science, and I was writing my master thesis in the department of sociology and on some phases of the Communist movement; and I felt, therefore, it was necessary to come in direct contact with the movement. I had carried a good deal of literature on the subject I was studying, and I felt as a young man I was not doing anything wrong in belonging to such a movement. I thought it was a perfectly legitimate movement. I thought it was lawful. I had no idea it could be considered wrong to belong to the Communist Party of America. No one told me about that."

Asked then if he believed in organized government, he said he did; if he believed in the soviet govment of Russia, he said he did not; if he had signed an application card for membership in the Communist Party, he said he had not.

With that examination record before me, a record void of any other proof against the alien, I cancelled the warrant of arrest.

Referring afterwards to this decision while testifying in inchoate impeachment proceedings against me, I characterized the Wirth case as one "on which any jury might well have disagreed. ' ' Having now again read all the testimony in the case, I am obliged to confess that in that remark I was unjust to jurymen. It would be a reflection upon the intelligence of any American juror to imply that he could have stood out for a verdict of guilty on no other evidence than the Wirth-case record presented.

Of the more distinctly frivolous class of cases in which I cancelled warrants of arrest, there were several varieties. In one of them the alien was arrested early in January but the warrant of arrest did not issue for more than a week. It charged membership in the Communist Party. The only item of evidence against the alien, besides an affidavit made merely "on information and belief/'

was a Communist Party membership card of which he denied all knowledge, and regarding which there was no proof connecting him with it other than similarity of name.
