From his testimony, confirmed by some of the circumstances, this alien had been arrested by a man in civilian clothes who did not explain his authority nor show a warrant of arrest but who took the alien to the city hall, where he was subjected to a police inquisition. He was then taken to a neighboring city where he was photographed against his will with about 100 other "red" prisoners. At his lawful hearing there was no evidence except his own testimony from which any inference could be drawn. This testimony was to the effect that he was a member of the lawful Socialist Party, and that if his branch in that party had been transferred to the unlawful Communist Party— as was asserted against him— he didn't know it for he had attended no meetings and had heard of no transfer. Besides thus exonerating himself without contradiction from an unproved charge, this alien testified that he and others arrested in like manner with him were threatened with violence by two men in uniform and some Polish detectives, for refusing to answer police-inquisition questions. "The Polish detectives leveled revolvers at us," he asserted, "saying that if we do not answer those questions we will be killed." The questions were "whether I am a Communist, whether I belong to the Communist Party." Similar sworn assertions were made by other aliens arrested in like manner, but as all were denied by detectives or policemen present at the inquisition, the truth of the matter cannot now be determined. No serious attempt was made at the time, and the only present recourse is an appeal to the probabilities. One of the larger facts upon which such an appeal would rest is the wellknown practice, toward which the courts have for several years been dangerously complacent, of giving the "tted-degre^ ** to Tmseßer&-in tha secret cusiady of detectives. This practice was imported from Prussia in the mid-eighties by Inspector Byrnes of the New York police. Its efficiency in making cases regardless of guilt, popularized it with inefficient detectives. It was probably resorted to by the private detective attaches of the Depart* ment of Justice in connection with the January! raids. Very likely, therefore, this alien told $he truth when he charged the use of police intimidation for the purpose of extorting a " confession " in his case.

In a somewhat similar case, in which also I cancelled the warrant of arrest, the only evidence tending to justify deportation was the alien's statement, made while in the unlawful custody of a special agent of the Department of Justice twelve days before the warrant of arrest issued. In this statement the alien was recorded as admitting membership in the Communist Party. He had signed the statement at four or five o'clock in the morning following the raid, immediately after his delivery at the detective rendezvous and in reply to questions put to him there by the detective who held him in close custody during the questioning. I cancelled the warrant of arrest in this case on the ground that whether or not the maltreatment testified to by the alien, and confirmed by another arrested alien who was present, but denied by the arresting officer, had occurred or not, his "confession" had manifestly been mado under police duress, and without it there was a total failure of proof of any unlawful membership.

Another type of cancellation cases was represented fairly enough by one in which, upon the alien's arrest by Department of Justice detectives without warrant, they questioned him and inserted their own version of his replies in a mimeographed question-blank which they caused him to sign and swear to. According to this document the alien seemed to have admitted categorically that he was a member of the Communist Party, and this apparent admission alone constituted the evidence against him at his lawful hearing. There was nothing else. On the other hand, from his uncoerced testimony at the lawful hearing it appeared that the alien had joined the Communist Party without any knowledge of its program, and upon the understanding that he was joining a school for the study of mechanics, having done this in preference to going to public school because he did not understand English. Not until he was arrested did he discover that the organization he had joined in order to advance his education as a mechanic was unlawful, and upon making that discovery he immediately gave up his membership. In cancelling this warrant I explained on the record that the case against the alien depended upon his voluntary statement at his lawful examination that "he belonged to the Communist Party without knowing its program and only because he wished to join its school to learn the trade of a mechanic/' and that in my judgment it was "not probable that Congress intended by the Act of October 16, 1918, to deport members of an unlawful organization who have become such under these circumstances, and who, upon discovering that the organization is unlawful, have withdrawn from it More distinctively^frivolous^ a s the evidence against an alien who admitted joining the Communist Party prior to March 10, 1919. As the Communist Party did not exist until September, 1919, this admission proved nothing. There was no other evidence.

Still another type of my cancellation decisions was exemplified in the case of an alien who, while a member of the lawful Socialist Party, had been transferred automatically by a vote of his local branch, in which he did not participate and of which he was ignorant, to the unlawful Communist Party, which credited him with payment of dues for three months. His explanation of payment of dues fitted many other instances of automatic transfer. Here it is: "At the time when I joined the Socialist organization I paid four months' dues, and I never knew that it was turned over to the Communist Party."

In a case involving still another distinctive phase of "automatic membership" in the Communist Party, the only evidence against the alien was a copy —not the original, but a copy— of his unlawful examination by a Department of Justice detective. It gave no indications of having been sworn to in the original, or of having been even signed in the original, and its contents Were neither certified to nor sworn to as a copy of an original. No witness had been examined at the lawful hearing, and at that hearing the alien refused to answer questions. In still another case the only evidence of membership was a membership card of the Socialist Party dated three months before the Communist Party existed. In a third, nothing more vicious was proved against the alien than that he was a versifier who might not be regarded as a poet of any classical school. In a fourth, a Russian revolutionist of 1905-1908, who declared his belief in soviet government for the United States if adopted by the people without violence and by means of our legal methods, but opposed overthrowing this Government by force or violence, was on the way to deportation as "a fugitive from justice" from the Russia of the Czars! I cancelled the warrant under which he had been arrested, upon the indisputable ground that no deportation law of ours applied to such a case.

In some of the particularly distinctive cancellation cases, the aliens arrested had refused to answer questions, and for this refusal alone, notwithstanding that the record contained no incriminating proof, not even the record of a police-office inquisition, nor so much as an uncertified copy of the alien's answers at such an inquisition, deportation was demanded. In this class of cases I took the ground, as set out in one of my memorandums of decision, that — "although deportation cases are not criminal cases in the technical sense, and are therefore not subject to the rules of criminal prosecution, they are nevertheless of a penalizing character and should be conducted throughout with a reasonable regard for the safeguards which the American Constitution and the principles of American liberty are designed to throw around every resident of this country (whether alien or citizen) for the protection of his person as well as his property. It is therefore requisite that no warrant of arrest issue in these cases except upon probable cause. Proof of probable cause may be supplied by an affidavit made upon information and belief. But it Is to be assumed that when a complainant makes such an affidavit he in fact has information justifying his asserted belief. If he has not such information, he has sworn falsely. Consequently, when the case comes to a hearing, he should be able either to produce his informant as a voluntary witness or to furnish the examining inspector with the facts necessary to secure compulsory process. At any rate, the resident alien must be confronted with lawful prima facie proof of his culpability under the deportation statutes before any adverse inference can be drawn from his refusal to answer questions. Mere fishing expeditions in these cases are not to be encouraged. The notion that resident aliens are bound to prove affirmatively that they are not unlawfully in the country has no justification in the United States under the Federal Constitution. The impression that they are so bound when arrested on the warrant of the Secretary of Labor is probably due to the conventional form of the warrant of arrest, which directs that the accused alien be arrested and given a hearing 'to enable him to show cause why he should not be deported.'

But this expression means no more than that the alien shall be allowed to show cause, not as a preliminary condition of continuing to reside here, but in defense of his right to remain after he has been confronted with prima facie proaf that he has been found here contrary to law."

The part of my decision which I have just quoted was criticized at the time as being in conflict with a decision of the Supreme Court of the United States. 1 But there was no conflict. The criticism belonged in the class of " case-law " criticisms which are so faithful to precedent that they sometimes overlook essential differences. The essential difference in this instance was noted by that excellent authority, Clement L. Bouve, in these words:

"Since by general intendment the immigration laws impose the burden of proving the fact of the alien's ineligibility thereunder on the Government, the obligation to prove the contrary can only rest on the foreigner seeking admission, where the law clearly provides that such is the intent." 2 As to that Supreme Court decision, one need but read it to realize that it applies only to such of our immigration laws as affect the residential rights of Chinamen. This decision of our highest court was made in the case of a Chinaman claiming residential rights, and it sustained his deportation on the ground that by our laws affecting the residential rights of Chinamen the burden of proving this right is imposed upon the Chinaman claiming it. Congress has not imposed that burden of proof upon alien residents who are not Chinese. That it has the power to do so is, indeed, involved in the principle of the Supreme Court's decision; but until it does so, the 1 Fong Yue Ting versus the United States, 149 U. S. Reports, page 698.

2"A Treatise on the Laws Governing the Exclusion and Expulsion of Aliens in the United States. By Clement L. Bouve, of the District of Columbia Bar, Member of the American Society of International Law. Washington, D. C, John Byrne & Co., 1912." Page 553.

general rule as to burden of proof prevails in alien expulsion cases in which the alien is not a Chinaman. The deportation cases I have so far outlined illustrate every variety of all the "red" expulsion cases which, as Assistant Secretary of Labor, I was charged with deciding. That they do so fairly, I am sure. As to those which directed deportation, there were few protests. Not so with my cancellation decisions. They stimulated hostile activity in private detective centers and publicity bureaus and gave birth to a movement in Congress for my impeachment. The story of this Congressional movement and that of the widespread newspaper villifieation which preceded and accompanied it, together with that of the futile effort of a busy and afterwards discredited clique in the American Legion to induce President Wilson to remove me from office when the impeachment scheme collapsed, must be reserved for coming chapters.

### Chapter XXV: Impeachment Proceedings Initiated

While I was busy with my official work, employees of the Committee on Immigration and Naturalization of the lower house of Congress, of which Albert Johnson of Washington State was chairman, were scrutinizing immigration records in the Department of Labor and reporting sensational discoveries to their committee. This committee, or a branch of it, led by its chairman and reinforced by its sergeant-at-arms, had called upon me for the purpose of requesting permission to make the scrutiny and I had granted its request. My only reservation required that no records be taken away from the Department building, but I directed that ample accommodations and facilities within the building be provided. One outcome of the visitation was a sub-committee report on which proceedings for my impeachment were afterwards based.

A hotch-potch of extracts from immigration records, this report had been compiled by the Committee's sergeant-at-arms, assisted by an official of the immigration service at Seattle who had amusingly distinguished himself by officially putting Henry George's "Land Question," Thomas Paine 's "Age of Reason," William Morris's "Useful Work and Useless Toil," and Schaeffle's "Quintessence of Socialism" in an improvised index expurgatorius as "anarchistic." The report was rich in false leads which might have had a malicions origin but were crude enough to have sprung out of nothing worse than mental djaUness at high tension.

One of its misleading statements— -of much importance, as the sequel showed — implied that the Assistant Secretary of Labor, at whom it aimed its accusations and insinuations, was subordinate in the Department of Labor to the Solicitor. In truth the Solicitor neither had nor professed to have any Departmental authority except in the absence or inability of both the Secretary and the Assistant Secretary, whereas in the absence or inability of the Secretary, or by his direction regardless of his absence or inability, all Departmental authority vested by law in the Assistant Secretary.

Another of the false leads of that committee report indicated that in my decisions cancelling warrants for the arrest of accused alien "reds," I had arbitrarily balked other officials by setting aside decisions of theirs which I ought perfunctorily to have approved. Quite at variance with that estimate of my official obligations was the Act of Congress from which I derived my authority and by which I was bound. This law explicitly vested in the Secretary of Labor every particle of alien-expulsion authority and responsibility. To no one else except his Departmental representative (myself in the instances under consideration) did it give so much as a twilight shadow of authority to make either decisions or recommendations.

All this should have been known to the officials who prepared the report of the immigration committee. But in their zeal they jumped with agility to the conclusion that the Bureau of Immigration had lawfully made decisions which I had unlawfully killed. The fact that what the Bureau called its " decisions" were of no more legal value than crumpled paper in a waste-basket was not to them obtrusive until, to their embarrassment later in their pursuit, they learned that my "cancellations" had no reference to anybody's decisions but my own, that they set at naught nobody's authority (whether primary or secondary) but that they were brief records of decisions by the only official then in the Department of Labor who was invested by law with authority to decide.

Yet the chairman of the committee and its sergeant-at-arms drenched their committee report with their notion that the Commissioner General of Immigration possessed authoritative advisory powers.

So absurdly influential with them was this obsession that they burdened their committee report with the full text of long memorandums by the Commissioner General, ending with stereotyped recommendations and pseudo decisions— all written by subordinates who dared not write otherwise. In the report notations followed each memorandum, showing that I had overruled or ignored the recommendatory decision.

The strongest objection to embodying such memorandums in the committee's report would be the useless printing expense it involved; for otherwise their reproduction in print was harmless. But the obsession had such a hold on the chairman of the committee that while inserting this legally useless collection of memorandums for the purpose of proving that I had made lawless decisions in the cases to which they related, he neglected to insert the records of evidence on which alone the law rquired me to act!

In advance of the impeachment proceedings thus instigated, "news" outgivings by members of the immigration committee and publicity agents of the Department of Justice started a newspaper cyclone of misrepresentation which did not wholly subside until the impeachment proceedings had collapsed. This cyclone derived its greatest impulse from a speech purporting to have been delivered from the floor of the House of Representatives by the chairman of the immigration committee. Chairman Johnson's speech preceded his committee report by more than a week— the testimony of the sergeant-at-arms and the raft of records which constituted that report not having been given to the committee until April 21, whereas the chairman 's speech got into the Congressional Record as early as April 12. Though this news-making speech appears to have been delivered from the floor of the House in eight minutes, the official report of it filled considerably more than eighteen columns— long, wide and closely printed in small type. It was evidently one of those "silent speeches" that Congressmen are allowed to insert in the official record of proceedings in the lower House. They are not delivered from the floor, are in fact no legitimate part of the official proceedings, and are printed in the Congressional Record so as to permit the mailing of copies without limit, without postage, and without personal responsibility for false statements.

There had already been some degree of newspaper publicity, enough to set the (amgijcacy going. About three weeks earlier a somewhat sensational newspaper "beat" had gone out, poorly disguised as an official "leak," and this publicity prepared the way for further publicity. On the authority of those newspaper revelations, editorial comments assumed, to quote from one of them in the New York Times, that "Assistant Secretary Post, in charge of the Department [of Labor] had cancelled the deportation warrants of more than 1000 Reds arrested by the Department of Justice and had let loose on the country these public enemies, some of them fugitives from justice." This mention of " fugitives from justice" must have alluded to the solitary fugitive from the dominions of the Czar whom I refused to extradite, first because there was no evidence of any kind against him, and second because extradition proceedings were not within my jurisdiction. A few days later the same paper published further "news" on the subject, but it was not until Chairman Johnson of the immigration committee had given to the press copies of his "silent speech" of April 12 that the newspaper cyclone began to whirl in earnest.

On the day following the official publication of that silent but stirring speech, such headlines as this indicated with approximate accuracy the contents of most of the newspaper reports: "Post scored as friend of enemy aliens— Johnson, House immigration chairman, cites cases of alleged favoritism—tells of Reds freed— declares he believes Labor Department is 'being bored from within/ " Another headline announced that "the Assistant Secretary of Labor is charged with being easy on Reds"; another that he "is accused of friendliness to radicals and said to have blocked deportations." And so the cyclone whirled. The news reports to which such headlines related were not as a rule calculated to minimize the sinister suggestiveness of the headlines, although there were notable exceptions. To careless news readers it must have seemed that I had betrayed my trust. They could not have guessed that I had offended by deporting only such aliens as were proved guilty and releasing the others, instead of pitching all of them out of the country indiscriminately. Nor did such cautions reservations as "it is charged" or "he is accused" at all modify the misrepresentations in the minds of the general run of newspaper readers.

By some of my fellow citizens newspaper publicity of, that kind is valued as second only to newspaper praise. Theirs is an estimate which appears to have originated with Phineas T. Barnum, the great advertising showman of my boyhood, who made no secret of his preference for vituperative publicity rather than silence. But I confess to a shrinking from such publicity. At any rate I prefer the kind of vituperation that keeps within hailing distance of the truth, over that which depends for its abusive vigor upon misrepresentation. Frankly, then, I did not enjoy the gyrations of that newspaper cyclone as it swept across the country. For nearly seven years I had served the Government of the United States as Assistant Secretary of Labor. I had not sought the office and was averse to taking it. It came to me in the Spring of 1913 without solicitation on my part, directly or indirectly, and I accepted with reluctance after twice definitely declining. During my incumbency I administered its varied and often perplexing and harassing functions as well as my abilities permitted. And now, as my second term was drawing towards its close, I took no delight in being caught up in that cyclone of vituperative newspaper publicity. But "news" was news, and the press of the country emitted blast after blast of second-hand falsehoods and machinemade editorial censure which must have deceived hosts of fair-minded readers.

In the midst, however, of this unwelcome newspaper attention, I had many experiences to be grateful for. The Secretary of Labor, for instance, assured me of his hearty approval of the policy I was pursuing. Several newspapers and other periodicals treated it with reasonable fairness, some with thoughtful commendation. Among them were the Chicago Evening Post, William Allen White's Emporia (Kansas) Gazette, the Johnstown (Pa.) Democrat, the New Republic, the Seattle Union, the New York Evening Post, the Nation, the San Francisco Star, Reedy 's Mirror of St. Louis, the Fargo (North Dakota) News, the Hearst papers, the Christian Science Monitor, the Springfield Republican, the Nebraska State Journal, the Fort Wayne JournalGazette, the Chicago Record-Herald, the Chicago Tribune, the Survey, the New York World, the New York Call, the Washington Searchlight (now the Spotlight), Labor (published at Washington), the Advocate of Peace, the San Francisco Call, the NonPartisan Leader.

One Washington newspaper man, Gilson Gardner, wrote on the subject to President Wilson, saying, "I have had reason to make a personal investigation of the so-called raids on the reds and the handling of these cases after the raids. If half of the facts were known to the public there would be a demand for the impeachment, not of Mr. Post, but of Attorney General Palmer and Mr. Caminetti." Through one of the news letters of David Lawrence—all of which were considerate, substantially accurate and quite impartial in spirit — I was encouraged by an unmistakable indication of President Wilson's favorable attitude toward the policy I was trying to pursue. In this letter, Lawrence reported the President as having given "the impression to his Cabinet that, while the Government should proceed relentlessly against disturbers of industrial peace and deport aliens found guilty thereof, he wanted the judicial process to be painstakingly fair."

Nor do I overlook the important historical fact that Congressman George Huddleston, a democratic Democrat from Alabama, followed Congressman Johnson's "silent speech" of April 12, 1920, with one on April J23 on his feet and on the floor, in which he vigorously denounced the lawlessness that Johnson had sponsored. "You gentlemen," said Mr.

Huddleston, "have read in the newspapers about the raids upon aliens — unconstitutional raids, raids without process of law, raids in which American citizens, women as well as men, were arrested, were beaten, were dragged to jail and held without legal process... A well-informed gentleman from New York City said to me that a great many of those who were arrested did not so much as know the difference between 'bolshevism' and ' rheumatism.' They were illiterate, they were poor, they were friendless aliens, many of them, and away from home. They were not voters and they had no money; they had no voice, and so they had no champion... Only a few if any of those caught in the raid had any real knowledge that they belonged to a party which advocated force. Numbers of those arrested were women. Numbers of the men arrested were American citizens. They were beaten and dragged off to jail. The citizens were subsequently weeded out and released, as were also a great many others who had been arrested and against whom no charges whatever were made.

Many private houses were invaded without legal process, furniture was broken up, wives and children insulted and husbands dragged away. Private papers were seized and carried off for evidence. There was no process of law for any of this, but the newspapers had been tipped off so as to get big stories... These raids on ignorant aliens no doubt had an effect, but what effect? Do they love America more? Is it yet their ideal as a place of refuge and justice? Will they make better citizens t Do they respect our Government more? Will they work harder? They may not strike, but will the output come from the factory where they are employed? Oh, a lawless people is bad enough, but a lawless government is infinitely worse. "

Although the newspaper cyclone kept on whirling, it began to lose force as the facts got. to the attention of responsible newspaper men. David Lawrence's letters led the way, but the New York Survey, especially through its publication of an absolutely accurate summary from the pen of Francis Fisher Kane, contributed immeasurably. So did the New York World when it realized, as a World correspondent explained its editorial impression to me, that I "was not getting a fair deal." So many, indeed, were the newspapers and periodicals which, indirectly or positively, after the facts came to light, sustained the policy for which I was by others denounced, that it grew difficult to realize the earlier violence of the cyclone.

Rumors of impeachment had followed close upon the nation-wide echoes of Chairman Johnson's "silent speech*" Those rumors were quickly verified by the formal introduction of an impeachment resolution. This lightsome burden was assumed by a Kansas Congressman of the name of Homer Hoch, a youngish man of forty-odd. He had been tempted by more seasoned members who assured him that his connection with the impeachment case as prosecutor would make in that delirious time excellent material for his second Congressional campaign. His impeachment resolution having been prepared for him, this young Kansan rose in the House to offer it. But he was headed off by his party floorleader, Mr. Mondell of Wyoming, who in an undertone suggested: "I would not do that now." So Mr. Hoch sat down.

Informal caucuses were immediately held in whispering groups here and there on the floor of the House and an understanding was evidently arrived at. The participants in these conferences were reported as fearing that a point of order might be raised if the impeachment resolution were offered from the floor. At any rate, later in the day on which his party leader had obstructed him, Mr. Hoch introduced his impeachment resolution by unostentatiously handing it to the clerk of the House under a standing rule for the informal handing in of bills, resolutions and memorials. It did not come openly before the House at all.

This resolution provided for a preliminary inquiry by the Committee on the Judiciary. But the Speaker referred it to the Committee on Rules. The political wisdom of this course may be the better understood if considered in the light of the fact that the Judiciary Committee is a judicial branch of the House. It could not gracefully dispose of such a resolution without reporting its judgment. But the Committee on Bules is a political branch which could, without any breach of judicial deportment, smother the whole proceeding if it discovered that the impetuous Mr. Hoch had gone off on a false scent. Like the nearsighted hunter of the familiar anecdote, the Speaker aimed to hit if the object were a deer, but to miss if it were a calf.

### Chapter XXVI: An Angel from the Business Skies

The impeachment resolution disturbed me seriously in only one particular. I was financially unable to make the vigorous fight which the circumstances seemed to require. While my adversaries would draw at will from the public treasury for the cost of waging their side of the contest, I should have to defend at my own expense. To meet the unfair challenge in good fighting form would exhaust my official salary for a year— it might be for two.

Several offers of financial support came to me, but they were from friends no abler financially than I. In the aggregate their contributions might have made a nucleus for a defense fund, but I shrank from "passing the hat." There were offers, too, of personal service without pay, which I sincerely appreciated and in some instances was able to avail myself of. I received also an offer of free professional service from one of the best lawyers of the District of Columbia, Jackson H. Ralston. But I was not inclined to accept free professional assistance. Yet the alternative was to make my defense unsupported and unadvised. I had, indeed, the specific assurance of Secretary Wttstm that he- would consider my impeachment his impeachment; but he was no better off financially than I to enter fullarmed into such a contest.

While brooding over those circumstances late one afternoon alone in my office after the Homer Hoch impeachment resolution had been referred to the Committee on Rules of the House of Representatives, and before I had decided upon my course in defending, a visitor was announced whose coming turned out to be like that of a winged angel descending through a dark cloud.

This very human angel interrupted my despondent reflections by telling me of a conversation about my impeachment to which he had been a party the night before, that convinced him of the seriousness of my situation. "In these circumstances, " he added, "you will need money, need it bad, and I am here to tell you to draw on me at sight for ten thousand dollars. "

The name of my visitor was E. T. Gundlgish. I had known Mr. Gundlach since the trying days of the World War. One of that flock of "dollar-a-y ear-men" who descended upon Washington from the dizzy heights of profitable business, he was not only of the few who earned their salaries but of the very few who earned them highly multiplied.

Perhaps I should explain that Mr. Gundlach was not a radical of any type— except in the sense of not being a person of superficial mentality; that he was not in sympathy even with my type of radicalism, the Henry George variety, which regards the social values of natural resources as in their nature public property; that he was not a Socialist, nor an anarchist even of the Tolstoy type, nor a Communist, nor an I. W. W., nor in any way connected with labor unions, nor a bolshevist in any of its multitudinous meanings in the epithetical vocabulary of the malicious and the vulgar. On the contrary he was a pronounced conservative, though without the bigotry that conservatism so often breeds in conservatives. Neither was Mr. Gundlach a pro-German, as the ultra suspicious might infer. Although of German descent, he actively agitated for our entry into the War as an enemy of the Kaiser during the two years just preceding our entering it, and was among the first to go to camp when we did enter. Nor could he have had any hidden political motive in making his generous offer to me. He was in no way interested in political preferments— a fact which all his acquaintances know and which is emphasized by his having resigned from the public service and gone back to private business as soon as the fighting ended. Since then his only connection with politics has been to cast his vote at elections. The motive for Mr. Gundlach 's offer (which would not even now be revealed as his but for my insistence in order to forestall prejudicial misrepresentation) sprang out of his sense of public duty.

I did not hesitate long in deciding to accept his gift in the spirit in which I knew he was offering it. This offer seemed like a providential solution of the problem I was struggling with when his visit interrupted my meditations.

### Chapter XXVII: Opening of the Impeachment Fight

Having accepted the Gundlach offer to finance my defense in the pending impeachment proceedings, I retained Jackson H. Ralston as my counsel.

Mr. Ralston began his career as a lawyer in 1878. Twenty years later his firm— Ralston and Siddons —represented the Filipino Republic. After that he was American agent and of counsel in the first international dispute before the Hague tribunal and then the American umpire with reference to Italian claims against Venezuela. He is author of a valuable legal work, "International Arbitral Law and Procedure," and of a later one suggestively entitled "Democracy's International Law." In addition to his activities as a specialist in international law, Mr. Ralston 's practice had been extensive and included an active career at the bar of the Supreme Court of the United States. Since Mr. Gundlach had given me carte blanche as to expenditures I gave like freedom to Mr. Ralston, instructing him to make the best fight he could, regardless of expense within the bounds of Mr. Gundlach 's guarantee, and this he did. The total expenditure was $5,200, all of which Mr. Gundlach paid, refusing to allow me or anyone else to contribute, in view of the fact, as he said, that he was only keeping his word.

With my hearty assent Mr. Ralston called to his aid two associate counsel, Charles T. Clayton, who had preceded Hugh Eeid as my official private secretary and was thoroughly familiar with immigration law and the immigration records, and Frank F.

Nesbit, a promising member of the younger bar. These three jointly prepared the impeachment defense in every possible detail. Not only were they armed for the preliminary hearing before the Committee on Rules, but they had anticipated and prepared for every adverse movement by which that hearing might in regular course be followed, even to the possibility of a trial before the Senate. Nothing in the way of complete preparation remained to be done when the Committee on Rules finally granted me a hearing.

That I might have notice of all hearings was a formal demand of my counsel upon the chairman, who replied that there would be no hearings until the proponents of the impeachment resolution asked for them. Was it very unreasonable to assume from this reserved reply that a star-chamber proceeding might be contemplated? Four days went by and newspaper reports began to indicate that the committee was disposed to ignore the impeachment resolution in favor of a resolution recommending my removal from office by the President. Was it then unfair to anticipate a trick? Such a trick, whatever might be thought of its decency, would have been at least a shrewd one. Let me turn aside for a paragraph about it.

A resolution laid bv the lower House of Congress before the President demanding my removal, would have obliged me out of respect for him and his office to save him from embarrassment by resigning. Thereby I should have yielded my cause unless he had courted embarrassment by refusing to accept my resignation. Nor would my own resignation have been alone involved. Secretary Wilson's would have gone to the President with mine.

I say this because, at an earlier date, when I proposed to him that I resign so as to quiet the disturbance that Attorney General Palmer was making in the Cabinet, Secretary Wilson urged me not to do so, explaining that if he transmitted my resignation to the President without his own he would seem to be rebuking me for my decisions in the "red" deportation cases, whereas in fact he was in perfect sympathy with me in the course I had pursued.

"For that reason," he added, "and irrespective of all others," your resignation, if you resign, would "go to the President in company with my own." In those circumstances it was manifestly my duty not to resign.

But the circumstances would have been radically different if Congress had demanded my removal. I should then have been obliged to resign even if by doing so I made it seem to Secretary Wilson necessary that he also resign. Yet Secretary Wilson's resignation might have augmented any embarrassment of the President over a Congressional demand for my removal. By accepting Mr. Wilson's resignation as Secretary of Labor, the President would have seemed to place Attorney General Palmer on a pedestal in the Cabinet; by declining it— which would have necessitated his declining mine also— he would have challenged a powerful business and political combination of which Mr. Palmer was then the pivot. I am therefore of opinion that the most astute move the combine in Congress could have made in connection with the proceedings for my impeachment would have been the one which they appear from the newspapers of the day to have contemplated — abandonment of the Hoch impeachment resolution and a recommendation to the House to demand that the President remove me.

To make that move effective, however, a unanimous report from the Committee on Rules was imperative. A mere majority report would have been discredited by a minority report though from only one member— discredited to the extent, at least, of subjecting the majority report to inspection, criticism and possible disaster in the House. But unanimity, if it had been arranged for, came to grief under circumstances I have yet to describe. For the present my story is concerned with the preliminary sessions of the Committee.

The first session was held on the 27th of April, 1920. My counsel having been notified, they were in attendance. The Chairman laid the impeachment resolution before the Committee and called upon Representative Hoch as its proponent to explain it; but Mr. Hoch, reserving his explanation temporarily, gave way to Chairman Albert Johnson of the immigration committee.

Mr. Johnson thereupon laid the basis, for the impeachment proceedings by introducing a public document, then "just printed," as he explained, by a subcommittee of his immigration committee. He characterized it as "a report of its investigations in Communist and Anarchist deportation cases." It was that subcommittee report the making of which by Chairman Johnson and his sergeant-atarms I have already described.

When Mr. Hoch, following Mr. Johnson, addressed the Rules Committee as the proponent of the impeachment resolution, he made an admission to be remembered. "My own information/' he said, "is based almost solely upon the hearings and findings before the immigration committee which you now have before you." In truth it was based upon nothing else.

Statements of no evidential value were volunteered by Congressman Tincher of Kansas and Congressman Siegel of New York. Nor was anything at all substantial added by anyone or in any way to the pretentious but utterly empty report of Congressman Albert Johnson's subcommittee.

At the same preliminary session of the Committee on Rules one of my counsel, Mr. Clayton, exposed the emptiness as evidence of that report; and my leading counsel, Mr. Ralston, emphasized Mr. Clayton's exposition. But the members of the Rules Committee, like the proponent of the impeachment resolution and his volunteer aids who sought to share his conspicuity, were so intent upon something else that for a time these plain explanations glided through their minds without making any impression. They remained under the spell of Congressman Albert Johnson's baseless notion that in my cancellation decisions I had overruled somebody who had a legal right to advise dictatorially. At the second preliminary session of the Committee on Rules, which was held on the 30th of April, Mr. Ralston J s explanation of the case encountered volleys and also "snipings" in the form of question from members of the Rules Committee, from members of the immigration committee, and from various interested Congressmen, which indicated that the questioners were not primarily intent upon getting the facts. That the Rules Committee intended to make its investigation ex parte as nearly as possible, was manifest.

Until May 5, no meeting of the Committee was called after its session of April 30 when it had adjourned subject to the call of the Chairman; but meanwhile the Chairman was reported in the Washington evening newspapers of May 3 as having on that day said that there was no unanimity among the members of the Committee as to whether there should be further hearings. The day foUowing those reports, I demanded in a letter to the Chairman, prepared for me by my counsel, that the hearings "be not closed without an opportunity expressly afforded for my personal appearance and for the production of witnesses on my behalf "; and in consideration of the reported purpose of the Committee to bury the Hoch impeachment resolution under a resolution demanding my arbitrary removal by the President, this letter closed with an explicit v notification that my action had been "the action of an Executive branch of the Government, and that my official judgment, save for criminal violations of law," could "be challenged only by impeachment." The letter had its intended effect. On the sth of May the clerk of the Committee informed me that the Chairman of the Rules Committee had directed him to notify me that the Committee would have a meeting on the morning of May 7, at which I would "be given an opportunity toTßeTteard by the Committee'" if I car; i to appear.

I did care and I did appear. After giving my testimony— refusing in the course of it to be interrupted by the nagging which is so common with Congressional committees when they have an objective, but yielding to legitimate interruptions for incidental information— l submitted myself fully to cross-examination by anyone and everyone at the discretion of the Committee.

### Chapter XXVIII: Before the House Committee on Rules

Nobody can quite realize what he looks like or acts like, or the impression he makes, in such an ordeal as that through which I was passing before the Rules Committee of the House of Representatives in May, 1920. So I shall not venture upon any description myself. Inasmuch, however, as my appearance and behavior are doubtless part of the deportations story, I shall let others tell about it — one friendly witness and two unfriendly ones.

One of my two unfriendly witnesses is the Spokesman-Review, a conservative daily paper of national repute published at Spokane, Washington State. In its issue of May 8, 1920, this newspaper heralded its Washington correspondence with the following headlines: "Palmer's find in all raids only four pistols, two 22 — Post hurls broadside at foes, in self-defense— Wins round one— Lands i terrific wallops' on critics of his policy in ' deporting reds'— ls 71, full of fight— Flays Attorney General's work of persecuting men found innocent of wrong."

Those headlines were followed in the Spokesman-Review—and the Portland (Ore.) Qregonian carried the same report— with a summary of my hearing before the Committee on Rules in which the following descriptive passages occur:

" Louis F. Post, 71, Assistant Secretary of Labor, mentally supple, quick-witted, in his own defense today before the House Rules Committee was a living exhibit of vigor and sustained power. Despite his 71 years he seemed 25. He pounced upon Attorney General Palmer one minute, then hurled a charge of 'non-lawful' performance at Anthony Caminetti, Commissioner. General of Immigration, and landed a terrific wallop here and there to the House immigration committee of which Albert Johnson of Washington is chairman.

The impression created by Mr. Post was altogether favorable to himself. He calmly waited for his accusers to exhaust themselves before replying, and when he did there was a feeling in the room that he certainly had 'shoved something over 1 on Attorney General Palmer, and that he had made some explanation necessary from the immigration committee of the House.... * Cancelling a deportation warrant,' Mr. Post said, 'is nothing more than finding a verdict for the defendant.' This information impressed the Rules Committee because the idea had been general that the cancelling of a warrant meant that an order wa& issued to free the alien after he was found guilty and ordered deported."

Those descriptive passages from the columns of two unfriendly papers would seem at least to give color to the report of the friendly witness, Mrs. William Hard in the New Republic, from which I now qnote:

"I suppose the burneraihad audiences.

The hearing-hearers of Washington sometimes seem to be their lineal descendants. A large collection of them thickly lined the room of the Committee on Rules of the House to hear Louis Post baited. And they remained to see the torturer tormented. At the head of the table sits the gentleman from Kansas, Mr.

Campbell;... there stands opposite the gentleman from Kansas the short shaggy figure of the Accused. But hardly a half score of questions had been given before it thrilled electrically through the hearing-hearers — even the most blase of them— that this Accused was of a new and different variety. He anticipated attack, he welcomed it, he ran to meet it with every weapon of fact, of humor, of legitimate pride. So that by the end of the second afternoon Congressman Pou emitted an unexpected word. He confessed that the testimony had convinced him that Mr. Post had i acted throughout the deportation cases with a high sense of duty as a conscientious officer.' The emotions that charged the air in that little committee room were identified with no party. They were the recognizable ones of fear and ignorance.

Against these fears stood Louis Post, steadily adducing evidence whose predication was calm judgment upon proved fact, under the responsibility of executive decision.... Quite clearly and soon it became manifest that out of the thousands of deportation cases which have passed over his desk, there were a large number of * border-line* cases in which, if a man were to be anything but an automaton in office, he must find and hold delicate scales for judgment. Quite clearly and soon it became manifest that, whether of foreknowledge or not, the original report of the immigration committee upon which the present hearing before the Rules Committee is largely based, contains highly significant omissions of evidence.

Memoranda and letters from agents of the Department of Justice, of the Department of Labor, of Mr. Post himself, are put in apparently conclusive juxtaposition. But the actual evidence upon which Mr. Post came to his final decision has in many of these cases been omitted. Again, the inference was permitted to broadcast itself that Mr. Post had somehow taken away from Mr. Caminetti certain prerogatives of punishment. In Mr. Post's testimony it soon became manifest and clear that those prerogatives had never belonged to Mr.

Caminetti any more, as Mr. Post said, than they would have belonged 'to an office boy, or to a secretary of the Secretary employed to do clerical work.'... There were passages at arms in which somehow Mr. Post refused to remain in the role cast for him by the Congressional committee— that of an Unpatriotic Worm crossed with an Impractical Theorist,— and became instead the Guardian of old-fashioned Americanism, and the Curator of Facts. At such times the hearing-hearers were treated to dialogues like this: The Worm— ' As this committee might have found, the evidence and the memoranda did not tally.' The Committee— ' During the years when only physical cases for deportation came before the Commissioner his report was accepted, but when political cases began to appear, you began to review?' The Worm—' l see your implication. I did not.

Long before that I had been going direct to the evidence to make my own decision on it.'

The Committee— 'But the change was not made until there were political offenders involved?' The Worm— i The change was not made till I was convinced the Commissioner General was exceeding his authority.' ~\.. The Worm 'You have to define whatHihe prisoner means by Anarchy. Often he will call himself an Anarchist when his meaning falls under no accepted definition. I fancy all of us here in this room are Anarchists in the sense that we need no law, no enforcement of law, to prevent us from picking each others' pockets/ Mr. Pou — 'I beg to be excused from being called an Anarchist under that definition or any other.' The Worm — 'I excuse you.'... Agitated gentlemen kept going into corners and emerging with a new question from another side of the room.

Memoranda of unknown origin seemed to appear.

But the shaggy blackhaired figure with its seventy-three years borne so lightly on its square shoulders and shown not at all in voice or bearing kept on refusing to be a Worm.

As he stood there, unbowed, ungrayed by his seventy-three years, there seemed to pass forms, shadowy, real. They were the figures of the ignorant, the hampered, the misunderstood, the Aliens. Back of them were the terrified upholders of our Government. And back of them there seemed, shadowily, to be the Committee of Americanizers that sit in high places.

But in the foreground, unterrified by the unreined emotionalism of either, stood a little man, cool but fiery, who set his belief in the Constitution of the country above all fears, and who could amass facts.... It was the very strong impression of the confirmed hearings-hearer that whatever the Campbell committee may finally decide to report, when it went away from that room in the Capitol on the afternoon of the eighth of May, it had very much the aspect of a group of gentlemen who had picked up a very hot poker and were looking for some place to cool it."

On the occasion referred to in those descriptive excerpts I began my testimony with an exposition of the immigration sub-committee report that had been introduced as the basis for my impeachment, stating that it was the work of some persQZL possessed of very slight knowledge of even the procedure in alien deportations. Outlining then the lawful procedure and the kind and degree of authority reposed in the different officials concerned, I explained that the deportation function was vested by law in the Secretary of Labor and in the Assistant Secretary (the Solicitor in certain circumstances) under the Secretary's instructions or in his absence, and that the Commissioner General of Immigration had no authority whatever except to execute the decisions of the Secretary, or, as the case might be, of the Assistant Secretary or the Solicitor. In making this explanation I was helped rather than hindered by the stubborn questions with which the Chairman interrupted me. Apparently, however, I made it so clear at last that the obstinate pretense that I had overruled some one with authority to decide, faded and withered and vanished. Having disposed of that phase of this delirious impeachment proceeding, I drew the Committee's attention to the false pretenses with which the deportations delirium had been "doped." "In the course, " I said, "of the development of the newspaper publicity— the newspaper drive that was made in order to create a great terroristic scare in jhisj3ountry — the newspaper publicity bureaus, the men fEat take a newspaper salary and another salary to misrepresent and to put false lights on situations and to create sentiment, took advantage of_ the discontent that is afloat in the country to make a terror, " in consequence of jsrfiich "Ihe general public must liave a "vision of a great conspiracy to throw bombs and att that sort bTthmgT 7 But "who were arrested?" From the moment that the Secretary of Labor decided that membership in the Communist Party brought the member within the purview of the statute if he were an alien, "hardly an arrest was made upon any other ground than membership in the Communist Party.... But in all these sweeping raids over the country in which men were arrested at midnight and taken out of their beds at three o'clock in the morning in their homes, without warrant, in which their houses and their persons were searched without warrant— not by the Department of Labor; I do not undertake to say by whom, but that is the fact— these were the people afterwards brought to us, great raids made upon meetings and men rounded up and taken to police stations and a large proportion of them discharged before morning because they could not prove anything against them. With all these sweeping raids all over the country, there have been three pistols, I think it is, brought to our attention in the scores of cases that have come to us." And not only, I continued, were "nearly all the cases" that came to us cases of mere membership, but it was "almost impossible to believe that the men know they were doing anything but going to school or going to a social club of men from their own country and of their own speech." I then explained that out of the total of those membership cases, only about 40 prisoners, or possibly 50, were shown, either by confession or proof, to have been advocates of or believers in violent methods for governmental changes.

Advancing then to a consideration of individual cases, I read my official opinion in the case of Thomas Truss of Baltimore which has been already fully described in these pages. Before I had gone far I was interrupted by an inquiry from Mr. Garrett, a member of the Committee, whose questions, if hostile, were nevertheless always considerate ami rational. He asked if in my statement of principles of decision I had not applied to the administration of the deportations law the essential and fundamental principles of criminal law. Replying to this inquiry I said: "I cannot answer that question categorically, but I will state to you what principles Ido apply. The Supreme Court has held that Congress has turned this whole matter over to our administrative department of the Government; that the question of whether an alien shall be allowed to continue to reside in the United States is a question of sovereignty and belongs on the Executive side of the Government and not on the judicial side. Consequently the courts have refused, on writs of habeas corpus, to interfere with the decisions of the administrative side of the Government in these cases unless there is absolute lack of jurisdiction. Where there is no evidence at all to support the case for deportation, the courts will interfere on habeas corpus. But they will not review the merits of the case, because, they say, it is a question of sovereignty turned over to the Executive department of the Government and they have no right to cross the line. Now, that is the attitude of the courts. My contention is— l will not undertake to say it is the Secretary of Labor's, but I have not any doubt of it; I have followed what I believe to be his judgment as to all of these cases for the last five or six years^my contention is that when the Executive department of the Government is the absolute judge of whether a resident alien shall remain in this country or not, and the courts will not interfere, we should see to it that no injustice is done him. If he has lived here ten or fifteen years he has a domicile here, he is entitled to the protection of our Constitution, of our laws, and we should be all the more careful in judging these cases because he has no redress in the courts when an administrative judgment is given. And therefore I say that there are principles of the criminal law which ought to be applied by the administrative department of the Government unless there is the strongest reason, in each individual case, for not applying them. And that is the reason — not that I am applying absolutely the criminal law to administrative process, although I think the principles of criminal law, the protections of the criminal law, ought to be accorded; yet I know that we cannot accord them as criminal law. But I can take from the criminal law its humane, its just, its American, its Constitutional principles of protection to the liberty of the individual and apply them when I am acting for the Executive department of the G-overnment. That is my mental attitude. JHaving found that tlie fate of the man is in thTThands of the Executive department of the Government if there is the slightest scintilla of what may be evidence I consider that we should be all the more careful; that we should not apply to cases involving human rights the rules exclusively that would apply to property questions under administrative process. Administrative process is a very dangerous institution for a country like ours to adopt with reference to personal liberty, and it is pretty near time that some check was put upon its development so far as it affects rights to personal liberty. "

Recurring after that interruption, to the Thomas Truss case, fully described in an earlier Chapter, I called the Committee's attention to the fact that in this case, as in nearly every case relied upon for my impeachment, the evidence on which alone I had any lawful authority to make decisions, and on which I had decided those cases, was omitted from the bundle of evidence against me. Thereupon I laid before the Committee a verbatim copy of the official record of all the evidence in the Truss case. And inasmuch as the argument of the immigrant inspector who prosecuted the case and recommended deportation was already in the record as part of the immigration committee's accusatory report, I asked for the insertion of the counter argument of the attorneys for Truss — the law firm of ex-Judge Alfred S. Niles of Baltimore— which request, not without reluctance, the Rules Committee finally granted. The substantial parts of the record in the Truss case being then before the Committee on Rules, I discussed the merits of the case.

In the middle of this discussion the Committee on Rules adjourned from the 7th to the Bth day of May. Resuming my testimony in the late forenoon of the latter day, I thought it well to emphasize the point already made, that the real issue in these deportation cases was "not whether the Secretary of Labor should deport men who came within the proscription" of the Act of Congress, "but whether he should deport men who do not come within that proscription." But the Chairman, still haunted by the ghost of the defunct notion that I was bound by the extra-legal judgments of the Commissioner General of Immigration, plunged again into a discussion of that matter. He came out of it no better than before; and, his interruption over, I took up other individual cases upon which the impeachment proceedings were based.

One was the Wirth case. In this case, as in the Truss case, tEef evidence was missing from the impeachment proof as presented to the Committee by the chairman of the immigration committee and the proponent of the impeachment resolution. I had it inserted in the record of the Committee on Rules. A full explanation of the case has already been given in these pages.

Another ease to which I called the Committee's attention was that of John Dudinsky. The evidence in this case also was missing from the record upon which my impeachment was demanded, and I secured its insertion. This is one of the cases which T have described in a previous Chapter, but it played a part in the impeachment proceedings which necessitates a slight repetition. Dudinsky had refused to answer questions at his first hearing before an immigrant inspector. The evidence against him in the record when it came to me was slight, but enough, as I thought, to put him to his proofs, and on March 22, 1920,— note the date,— l so decided, sending the case back to the immigration inspector for further hearing. Five weeks later the record of re-hearing came to me, showing that the alien still refused to answer questions. So I ordered his deportation. A day or two after my decision ordering Dudinsky's deportation, Congressman Davey of Ohio publicly denounced me for not having ordered it. A similar attack had been made a day or two earlier by Congressman Tincher of Kansas. Mr. Davey 's attack was of course a boomerang, for it denounced me for not doing something which I had already done. But Mr. Tincher insisted before the Committee, and with an insolent air, that his attack had been made just before I acted, and with an insinuation that my action had been prompted by it. The insinuation was pointed but not piercing. Mr. Tincher found himself in the same frail boat with Mr. Davey, for my effective decision had been made long before either of them knew there was such a case as Dudinsky's. It was made on the 22d of March— five weeks before Tincher delivered his denunciation— when I granted the rehearing, deciding that there was " enough lawful evidence in the record to make a prima facie case, and thereby to put the alien upon his defense. " As the alien did not make any defense at the re-hearing I granted him for that purpose, his deportation followed as matter of course. My decision to this effect was made before I had even a hint of the existence of Congressman Tincher — or of Congressman Davey either, except as the author of a peace-time sedition measure in Congress (not enacted) that would have delighted George HI.

Turning from the Dudinsky to th^ Magor^case, of which much had been made in the^way of denunciation, and which I have already described, I discussed it in some detail before the Committee. This was one of those exceptional cases in which my accusers had inserted the evidence on which my decision was based, as well as the extraneous memorandums, recommendations, " decisions, " etc., on which, totally regardless of the evidence, they had relied in nearly all the cases on which they based the impeachment proceedings. In the Magon case the sole question was whether or not this alien was an "anarchist" within the meaning of that word in the deportation law. At his hearing before the immigrant inspector he had so labeled himself; but upon cross-examination by the inspector he explicitly described his opinions about government, and according to his unchallenged description he was distinctly a governmentalist and therefore not an anarchist.

The next individual case I discussed before the Committee was that of Bosco r the circumstances of which are fully presented in a previous Chapter. This case also was one of the very few regarding which my impeachers had submitted the whole record. So I was able to discuss it before the Rules Committee without first inserting the evidence on which mv criticized decision had been made. On the question of bail I explained that here was a man "entitled to an opportunity to be free until he could be deported; and in order to enable him to have an opportunity to be free, bail had to be fixed at a reasonable amount; fixing bail at $10,000 meant that he would remain in prison; fixing it at $1,000 meant all the security that we needed, just to get that man to attend the hearing of his case, and to be present for deportation when we had the opportunity to deport him, instead of being locked up at our own expense and at the expense of the personal liberty features of our Constitution." Then I put into the record of my hearing, the Eighth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States, which provides that " excessive bail shall not be required," explaining as I did so, that this Constitutional guarantee applies as well to aliens as to citizens.

Having finished with the Bosco case I took up a case in which, instead of cancelling the warrant, as the Commissioner General of Immigration advised, I had endorsed upon the record these words: "Let the Court decide." The contention against me seemed to be that, in order to prevent an embarrassing precedent I ought to have forestalled an adverse court decision in a case of deportation ordered by the Solicitor as Acting Secretary. In this case the Commissioner General had recommended deportation, and the Solicitor, acting in due course as Secretary at the time, had approved the recommendation. The alien sued out a writ of habeas corpus on the ground that there was a total lack of evidence to support the deportation order which the Commissioner General had recommended.

When the habeas corpus proceeding came before a Federal judge and he had heard arguments and read the record, he informally notified the U. S. Attorney that he would have to sustain the writ and free the prisoner. This information came informally to the Commissioner General, who submitted to me a memorandum in which he recommended that I cancel the warrant because the Judge "has already indicated what his decision will be, that he will sustain the writ," having "deferred decision with a view to affording the Department an opportunity to further consider the case." Instead of cancelling the warrant, as recommended, I substituted for the word "cancel" at the bottom of the Commissioner General's memorandum the words: "Let the Court decide." The Court did decide, by freeing the alien.

In connection with that case, the charge against me was to the effect that I was keen to let "red" aliens off until a judge was about to sustain a writ of habeas corpus in favor of one, and that then I refused to prevent an obstructive court precedent by cancelling the warrant. Outlining this charge to the Rules Committee, I went on testifying: "I will say very frankly that that was my reason, and I will tell the Committee why I had that reason. Sometime ago a cargo of men ordered to be deported abroad was brought on from the Pacific Coast to Ellis Island. The chairman of the immigration committee has referred to that before this Committee— he did yesterday— to the fact as he stated it, and as it has been stated by others, that when these condemned anarchists or reds or I. W. W.'s or whatever they were, were brought to New York, to Ellis Island, the Department of Labor released a large number on their own recognizance, and thereby turned a lot of criminals loose in the city of New York who had been imported from Seattle, and other places perhaps on the Pacific Coast. And that was used by the newspapers, and it was not minimized by the committee charged with investigating these things; but the Department of Labor was held responsible for having released these guilty men on their own recognizance — turned them loose in New York, though they had been convicted. The fact in that case — I did not learn it until afterwards, but I knew it when I signed this order to 'let the Court decide'— the fact in this case was that habeas corpus writs had been procured in New York before a Federal judge, and the Federal judge called in the Assistant U. S. Attorney and the representative of the Department of Labor 1, and he said to them: 'I cannot but sustain these writs. If you do not cancel these proceedings, or if you do not do something to let these men free, I shall have to let them free. ' And the Assistant District Attorney, or at any rate the representative of the De1 In testifying that the Judge called in the representative of the Department of Labor along with the Assistant U. S. Attorney, I was in error. He called in the Assistant U. S. Attorney, and the latter repeated to the representative of the Department of Labor the substance of the Judge's communication as I gave it in my testimony.

partment of Labor, communicated with the Bureau, and through the Bureau with the Department, asking that, in order that the Judge might not set a precedent by discharging these men on habeas corpus, we should release them on their own recognizance. The Department released them on their own recognizance, and they became, a few months later, a butt for the attacks of these men who now denounce me for having refused to cancel a warrant under those circumstances. I said in effect, 'We have had one experience with cancelling warrants where the Court has intimated that he will sustain the warrants', meaning, of course, the writs of habeas corpus; 'this time let the Court decide. We have carried the bag once; we will not carry the same bag with the same cargo again/ And that is the reason I decided that case in the way I did— 'Let the Court decide;' and the Court did decide. He ruled that our warrant was utterly without jurisdiction, and absolutely released the man — which would have been done in the other cases, about a year ago I think it was, when that great exploitation regarding them was made."

At this point I was interrupted by Congressman Johnson of South Dakota—not Albert Johnson of Washington State, chairman of the immigration committee, who had been especially loud in denouncing the Department of Labor for the release in New York a year or so before—who courteously asked for the names of witnesses to the circumstances I was narrating — the circumstances that led to those releases in New York through the intimation of the Judge. To the South Dakota Congressman's inquiry I replied, in the presence of the Rules Committee and as part of my hearing: "I can give you the name of the man— he is no longer in the Department— a perfectly reliable man; and if he says I am wrong about it I will yield myself to what he says. Ido not know the facts myself; I was not there. But Ido know that the thing was done. His name is Parker— A. W. Parker. If you will get A. W. Parker, whatever he tells you will be right, because he was there representing the Department. And I say in advance if Mr. Parker contradicts me in any respect in those particulars, I ask leave to withdraw the statement that I am making now to that extent. Mr. Parker knew about it and he is the only man that I know of that did distinctly know about it, representing our Department.

"

I discussed no other individual cases before the Rules Committee. It wasn't necessary. None of the others were worth discussing upon my initiative; and although the inquisition was open to all members of Congress, as well as to members of the Rules Committee, no other individual cases were brought to my attention. But a bunch of cases had been thrust into the bundle of miscellaneous charges. It was identified by the immigration subcommittee as "Cancellation of 38 Cases by Telegraph."

The essence of that charge seemed to be that I was indifferent to the interests of the Government because I had cancelled warrants in the cases of a whole group of aliens arrested as anarchists, "without awaiting the records of hearing in order to determine the nature of the evidence adduced to support the original charge."

Upon reading this accusation I was at first startled. Had I been asleep or in a trance when I wired that wholesale order? I had no recollection of ever having cancelled warrants by wire or otherwise without awaiting hearings. Upon reading the records in that bundle, however, I found facts which the immigration sub-committee, its sergeant at arms and the chairman of the whole committee had either incompetently overlooked or maliciausly concealed— probably the former. The accusation proved to be as flimsy asa" spider 's web.

A request by the Immigration Commissioner at Boston, H. J. Skeffington, had been wired to the Department proposing release on their own recognizance of all the aliens in the group. Asked by wire for his reasons, Commissioner Skeffington replied by wire that the examining inspector and the agent of the Department of Justice were "satisfied that the warrants should be cancelled."

Commenting before the Rules Committee upon those telegrams and the rest of the record, all of which had been omitted from the sub-committee's report which constituted the impeachment specifications against me, but all of which I laid before the Rules Committee, I said: "Now observe that they were satisfied that the warrants should be cancelled. Presumably they had taken evidence. The record showed that they had taken evidence, for the evidence was furnished in the records weeks after this telegraphic correspondence, and is here now. Then there came from the Bureau, prepared for my signature, a telegram which read as follows 'Your telegrams March 2 and 8. Eelease on their own recognizance all aliens concerned.' That was prepared for my signature by the Bureau of Immigration. I struck out the code word for release on their own recognizance' and inserted the code word for 'cancel,' in that telegram. I did not see then, and I do not see now, why men should be released on their own recognizance who had been brought before the Commissioner at Boston, the examing officer, and against whom no evidence had been produced, and with reference to whom both the examining officer and the representative of the Department of Justice were of the opinion that the warrants should be cancelled."

Having thus disposed of the startling charge that I had acquitted thirty-eight alleged anarchists without waiting for the evidence, I closed my testimony by reading extracts from a decision of one of the Circuit Courts of the United States, to which one of my counsel, Mr. Nesbit, had referred me. The case was "on all fours," as lawyers say, with the cases in which I had made the decisions for the making of which I was then threatened with impeachment for high crimes and misdemeanors.

Commenting as I read, I introduced the decision by calling the Rules Committee's attention to our Constitutional guarantees that "ne person «halt be deprived of life, liberty or property without due process of law, " and that "excessive bail shall not be required," and added: " Those quotations underlie the whole course of my proceeding in these matters on the basis of which it is proposed to impeach me before the Senate of the United States.

They also underlie this decision I now call to your attention and hope the Committee, or members of it at least, will carefully read from beginning to end." Then I quoted clauses from the syllabus which stated that "an alien as w.ell as a citizen, is protected by the universal principle that no person shall be deprived of life, liberty or property without due process, and his hearing must be in accord with the fundamental principles which inhere in due process of law;" that the "indispensable requisites" of an immigration-hearing "are that the course of proceeding shall be appropriate to the case and just to the party affected; that the accused shall be notified of the charge against him in time to meet it; that he shall have such opportunity to be heard that he may, if he chooses, crossexamine witnesses against him; that after all the evidence against him is produced and known to him, he may have time and opportunity to produce evidence and witnesses to refute it; that the decision or order shall be governed by and based upon the evidence at the hearing, and that only; and that it shall not be without substantial evidence taken at the hearing to support it;" that although the rule of the Secretary of Labor "be fair and just in appearance, yet if it is applied and administered by public authority with an evil eye and an oppressive hand, so as to deprive a person of his fundamental rights, it is inconsistent with law and void;" that "the fixing by the inspector of the time when an alien may inspect the warrant, or the time when he may have counsel, so late as to deprive him of a substantial opportunity of the benefit of an inspection of the warrant and of the benefit of counsel, is an abuse of discretion which renders the hearing unfair."

As I was about to read an excerpt from the court's opinion in that impressively American decision, Mr. Garrett, one of the Committee, again interrupted me with a request to know, before I went farther, on what ground deportation was attempted in the case I was citing, and I replied: "They were prostitutes and men found in a house of prostitution—aliens found in a house of prostitution— who fell under the prostitution clause/' which "is precisely the same in all other administrative respects as any of the other clauses."

Resuming then my presentation of the substance of the decision I was citing, I quoted at length from the opinion in the case, written by Judge Sanborn for the whole court of three judges 1 and with those quotations I concluded my direct statement to the Committee and informed the Chairman that I was "ready to try to answer any questions" that might be asked.

Some were asked by Mr. Garrett; they were courteous inquiries for pertinent information. Some were asked by Chairman Johnson of the im1 Whitfield versus Hanges, 222 Federal Reporter, page 745. migration committee; they were more humble in tone than Mr. Johnson's " silent speech" in Congress of a month before. Many were asked by the Chairman. Some were asked by Mr. Pou, who, when he got his answers, brought confusion into the Committee and crumpled up the original program by what he said, followed by what he did.

This incident, however, will be more appropriate at a later stage in my story.

### Chapter XXIX: Attorney General Palmer on the Defensive

The Committee on Rules adjourned at the close of my testimony on the Bth of May, and until the Ist of June nothing came out of it relative to my impeachment. On that day there was what might be fairly characterized as an interlude. Attorney General Palmer appeared before the CommilteeT He did not appear as a witness in the impeachment proceedings. He testified to no important fact within his own knowledge. All he did was to make a defensive speech.

His speech he read from manuscript prepared for him, except its literary and oratorical features, by the secret-service auxiliary of the Department of Justice. His reading occupied a two-days J session of the Committee.

When he finished, the Committee was addressed by Mr. Ralston, my leading counsel in the impeachment proceedings, who began:

"Mr. Chairman, I think, before the Attorney General goes, I ought to make one brief observation. It will take only three or four minutes, I think. On May 13—-"

Congressman Pou here interrupted Mr. Ralston with a motion that the Committee go into executive session; whereupon the Chairman, addressing another member of the Committee about another matter said: "We are going into executive session on your matter and on other matters here that must have action by this Committee in order to complete the work of the Congress. "
