Upon that announcement, Mr. Ralston explained his purpose. "I do not want," he said, "to disturb the plans of the Committee. I assume, then, that as soon as this evidence is printed I will have an opportunity to go over it and make suitable reply." "Oh, undoubtedly," the Chairman assured him.

But Mr. Garrett interposed: "You will have an opportunity to go over it, but I do not know about the other."

Thereupon the Committee went hurriedly into executive session.

Mr. Garrett 's significant doubt was subsequently confirmed. No opportunity was given my counsel to make any response to Mr. Palmer's speech.

I am inclined to believe that the Committee acted appropriately. Mr. Palmer's speech was in truth no part of the impeachment proceedings. Not only had he been invited by the Chairman in an informal and friendly way to appear, if he wished, merely to "refute" charges incidently made against his own Department, but the title page of the Committee's official record of his speech showed him as having appeared in his own case and not in mine.

Of the merits of his speech as distributed by him to the newspapers in advance, the New York World called it a "smoke screen."

The New York Evening Post said of it editorially: "If Attorney General Palmer had in hand any real defense against the charges brought against him he would not have required 30,000 words in which to set it forth. He has chosen to bury such defense of himself as he attempts to make in a labored and vicious attack upon Assistant Secretary Post that must long remain one of the curiosities of the Government archives.... The simple truth is that Louis F. H Post deserves the gratitude of every American for his courageous and determined stand in behalf of our fundamental rights. It is too bad that in making this stand he found himself at cross-purposes with the Attorney General, but Mr. Palmer's complaint lies against the Constitution and not against Mr.

Post."

### Chapter XXX: Collapse of the Impeachment Proceedings

The incident alluded to at the close of Chapter XXVIII is now appropriate. It occurred near the close of my testimony before the Rules Committee at my impeachment hearing, and the Committee's program was crumpled by it into an unrecognizable wad.

When upon closing my direct testimony I offered to answer questions freely, one of the prominent members of the Committee, Congressman Pou, a Democratic partisan of Mr. Palmer's whose attitude as a member of flie^Ooiiniliftee had been frankly hostile, surprised me with this question:

"Mr. Secretary, your political convictions have been represented in all sorts of ways, so that I believe I will give you an opportunity to say what your politics are, if you desire to do so."

"What do you mean by 'politics,' " I asked him; "party politics, or general political opinions?" "Well, both," he replied.

While I had no desire to describe my politics, neither had I any objection. Therefore I explained quite fully. In the Committee's record of hearing my explanation covers about three pages; but Mr. Pou asked me several supplementary questions, the report of which with the replies occupies about three pages more. Immediately upon my reply to his last question a colloquy occurred which I reproduce here from the Committee's record:

"M. Pou: I want to say, Mr. Secretary, that my feeling is that in what you have done, speaking for myself, I believe you have followed your sense of duty absolutely.

"Mr. Post: Well, I thank you, Mr. Pou, for that. You are the first man that has said that who comes from the opposite side, and I thank you for it. "Mr. Pou: I don't admit that lam on the opposite side here.

"Mr. Post: Whose sympathies are on the opposite side. Let us put it that way.

"Mr. Pou: I am probably not in sympathy with some of your views; surely I am not; but in your administration of the office, whereas my decisions if I had been there might have been different, I feel that it is due to you, feeling that way about it, that I should say that, and I do say it with pleasure." Almost before I could again thank Mr. Pou for thus emphasizing his frank and fair assurances, and acknowledge to him and the Committee, as I did, that I was "perfectly willing to concede that if there had been a tribunal of three men they would have disagreed frequently in these cases," he rose quietly from his chair and as quietly but deliberately walked out of the Committee room in the midst of the session.

For an appreciable interval the silence was impressive. The effect upon the Committee of what Mr. Pou had said and so significantly done seemed electrical. A reason was not far to seek nor difficult to find. It had been apparent for a considerable time that the Committee on Rules would not recommend impeachment. They could not. But faces must be saved, and the plans laid for saving faces involved in all probability, what the newspapers had forecasted, the subtitution for my impeachment of a resolution demanding my arbitrary dismissal from office. Inasmuch, however, as that course would have been suicidal without a unanimous report from the Committee recommending it, unanimity had been at least hoped for. This hope was shattered by Mr. Pou's words and the action with which he followed them. They were plain notice that no report from the Committee on Rules recommending to Congress a resolution demanding my removal by the President would be unanimous. The collapse of the impeachment drive became then and there complete. No other course remained to the Committee, after providing an opportunity for the Attorney General to make his self -defensive speech—to which Mr. Pou was evidently not opposed, and there was no reason why he or anyone else should have been,— than to pigeon-hole the proceedings. That is what it did. In no way has anything further occurred in Congress or any of its committees with reference, directly or indirectly, to Congressman Homer Hoch's impeachment resolution. With Congressman Pou's discouraging gesture the whole proceeding collapsed.

### Chapter XXXI: An After-Play

When the impeachment drama had been played to its anti-climax, the detective auxiliary of the Department of Justice staged an afterplay. It purported to be, but was not, an investigation by the American Legion, supplemented with what purported to be, but was not, the Legion's recommendation to President Wilson to dismiss the Assistant Secretary of Labor ignominiously from office.

Care had been taken not to present this recommendation to the annual convention of the Legion.

There might have been discussion there. But immediately after the convention it was laid before the new executive committee of the Legion which directed the National Commander to "take all steps necessary to secure the dismissal of the said Louis F. Post at the earliest possible moment."

A special committee having been appointed by the National Commander in compliance with the directions of his executive committee, a statement bearing suspicious marks of composition in the secret-service division of the Department of Justice, was by this special committee laid before President Wilson. Through Secretary Tumulty the President referred the statement to the Secretary of Labor, who returned the following draft reply, which was later given out at the White House by way of response:

"Dear Sir: Replying to your letter of December 31st, relative to Louis F. Post, Assistant Secretary of Labor, I am in receipt of the following statement from Secretary of Labor Wilson: 'Loiiis F. Post is one of the truest Americans I have ever come in contact with.

He is a thorough believer in democracy. He has no sympathy with an alien or American who believes in, advocates, or teaches the use of force to overthrow the Government of the United States. He has, however, a profound belief in and reverence for the Constitution of the United States, including the part that provides for its own amendment. He has, therefore, faithfully carried out the instructions given by me as Secretary of Labor concerning deportation cases. They are in substance as follows: (1). No person shall be arrested except upon sworn affidavit based upon information and belief. (2). No person when arrested shall be held incommunicado. (3). Every person arrested shall be entitled to counsel immediately upon being taken into custody. (4).

They shall be confronted with the evidence, and shall be permitted to furnish testimony and be heard in their own behalf. (5). Excessive bail shall not be required. These safeguards of American liberty having been provided, if it is then shown by any evidence that any alien is deportable under the so-called anarchistic laws, we will deport him with as little compunction as we would attack an invading enemy. We will not deport any one simply because he has been accused or because he is suspected of being a Red. We have no authority to do so under the law. No two men, even though they may have the same general viewpoint, can examine the same set of facts and give the same weight to each item. Consequently, in that class of cases that constitute the twilight zone, they might come to entirely different decisions. Mr. Post has faithfully carried out these instructions, and I am satisfied ranks amongst the ablest and best administrative officers in the Government service.' "

Secretary Wilson's letter for the President and its publication from the White House, dropped the curtain on the Legion faction's afterplay.

### Chapter XXXII: The Salsedo Homicide

Several episodes of special interest attracted public attention during the deportations delirium. The most startling was the Salsedo homicide. It came to my attention through newspaper reports on the day on which I began testifying in the impeachment proceedings before the Rules Committee of the House of Representatives. According to those reports an alien of the name of Andrae Salsedo had jumped to the street from a window high up in a building on Park Row, New York City, where he was in close custody as an alien charged with a deportable offense.

Knowing that the Department of Labor occupied no building there, I at first supposed that the tragedy did not involve the administration of this Department. Yet as one of the newspaper reports stated that at the time of his death Salsedo was in custody under a warrant of the Department of Labor, I sent to the Bureau of Immigration for any file we might have affecting any alien of the name of Salsedo. Realizing from the file that came to me then that Salsedo 's imprisonment as a deportable alien, if it had nothing else to justfy it, was lawless, I telegraphed to Ellis Island for the record in Salsedo 's immigration hearing. The reply explained that Andrae Salsedo had never been in the custody of the immigration authorities. Subsequently it appear that the Secretary's warrant of arrest had been issued by myself. It was addressed I 'to the Acting Commissioner of Immigration, Ellis Island," New York Harbor, "or to any immigrant inspector in the service of the United States/' and required the official executing it to grant his prisoner a hearing— that is, to take him to an immigrant station where he could be examined and could defend himself according to law before a duly authorized examining immigrant inspector. Under that warrant, if strictly considered, no one but an immigrant official had any authority to arrest Salsedo. Yet his arrest by some other official, or even by an unofficial person, could not have been seriously objected to if the person making the arrest had delivered him without delay to the immigration authorities. But this was not done. From the hour of his arrest to the moment of his death, Salsedo was in the custody, all unknown to the Department of Labor, of Department of Justice detectives. Consequently this lawlessly imprisoned alien wars given no hearing such as the law required and the warrant of arrest commanded. There was an attempt to make it appear that a lawful hearing had been given him, but that attempt was absurd. The hearing required by the law and commanded by the warrant of arrest was an examination with opportunity to defend before a duly authorized immigrant inspector, not a secret inquisition by Department of Justice detectives such as Salsedo received. An explanation of Salsedo 's captivity has been adopted by ex- Attorney General Palmer, whose secreT service agents were responsible for it. According to this explanation Salsedo was detained, along with one Robert Elia, in the Park Row building from which Salsedo 's body tumbled, these two prisoners and their lawyer consenting to the detention. "Subsequent to the issuance and before service of the warrants could have been effected," Mr. Palmer explained, "Salsedo and Elia disclosed some very important information in connection with the bomb plot of June 2, 1919, and with their consent and the consent of their attorneys they remained in the Department of Justice building in New York City until the information furnished by them could be verified and other persons interested in the bomb plot could be located." The "important information" referred to by Mr. Palmer is still a secret.

The warrants which, according to his secondhand statement, had been issued but were not yet served, were issued in February and early March and were in the hands of the Department of Justice for execution from that time until May, both prisoners being meanwhile in the custody of Department of Justice agents. And although Mr. Palmer loyally accepted the explanation of his secret-service advisers that the prisoners were in custody by their own consent "subsequent to the issuance and before the service" of the Department of Labor warrants, it is documentarily evident that his advisers themselves were not mentally at ease over this novelty of imprisonment by consent. Note the circumstances. Both Salsedo and Elia were still in the custody of Mr. Palmer's detectives when the Salsedo homicide occurred, which was two months after the detectives received the warrants of arrest. Both were held by them in secret custody until the homicide. No steps were taken meanwhile toward bringing the prisoners to an immigx^nt station for lawful custody and the requisite legal hearing. But no sooner had the Salsedo homicide occurred than those same Department of Justice detectives hustled Elia out of their custody and into the custody of the immigrant officials at Ellis Island. This spasmodic delivery of Elia to his lawful custodians is not easily reconcilable with a whole-hearted acceptance of the theory that for the preceding two months he and Salsedo had been prisoners by consent—even if that kind of imprisonment were not a legal novelty. And the pretense of the Department of Justice agents, for which Mr. Palmer became sponsor to the public, and which has gone the way of all the other thrilling pretenses of secretservice agents about those bomb plots, is hardly consistent with the refusal of the Department of Justice to co-operate with State officials in prosecuting Elia, nor for its urgency in demanding his speedy deportation.

That the long detention of Salsedo and Elia by secret agents of the Department of Justice in their building in New York was a gross violation of law, or at the least a gross abuse of authority under the law, is clear enough. Whether or not this lawlessness was accompanied with " third degree" cruelties culminating in Salsedo *s dramatic death, is something which only the detectives who had him in their custody could reveal; and no scrutinizing investigation appears to have been made, either by Federal or by State authority. Nothing is clear about that homicide except that Salsedo was lawlessly a prisoner, that he was held incommunicado in a secret prison controlled by detectives of the Department of Justice, that this prison was fourteen stories above the street, and that his body struck the pavement with an impact that turned it to pulp.

### Chapter XXXIII: Case of the Russian Ambassador

\ Complaints of "third degree " maltreatment of aliens arrested for deportation by Department of Justice detectives were not infrequent, as this story in its course has occasionally indicated. Some of these complaints came to the Department of Labor through regular immigration hearings. They did not lack probability, but the secret-service agents of the Department of Justice denied the accusations, as secret-service detectives always do. One reported victim of these outrages was the private secretary of Ludwig C. A. K. Martens, whose maltreatment as whispered about was peculiarly malignant and abhorrent. There was reason to anticipate similar treatment of Mr. Martens himself were he to fall into the hands of the secretservice auxiliary of the Department of Justice; and there was a certainty, judged by the spectacular performances of that industrious and ingenious group with reference to Mr. Martens during the winter of 1920, that he would be made the subject of humiliating newspaper publicity. His subsequent experience is another important episode of the deportations delirium with which my narrative is concerned.

Mr. Martens had been appointed by the soviet government of Russia as its ambassador to the United States, to take the place of Boris Bakmetieff, who had been recognized by the United States as ambassador from the Kerensky government of Russia, which had displaced the government of the Czars, and in October, 1917, had given way to the government of the Soviets. But our Department of State refused to recognize Mr. Martens. It continued to recognize Mr. Bakmetieff y until the summer of 1922. Its refusal to recognize Mr.

Martens rested upon no personal objection, so far at least as publicly appeared, but was attributed solely to the fact that his official reception would have acknowledged international relations with the de facto government of Russia. Mr. Martens thereupon established himself at New York as the agent in this country of the Russian government which had sent him here as its ambassador.

Whether or not his activities were in fact legitimate, they did not prove to have been illegitimate to the extent of illegality. Had such proof been available, Mr. Martens would surely have been prosecuted in New York where his headquarters were and where he was pursued by vigilant and weariless and resourceful agents of powerful business and political interests that were hostile to Russia. Nor even to the extent of bringing him within any of the proscriptions of our deportation laws, did his activities prove to have been illegitimate. After an extended hearing in the Department of Labor upon a warrant of arrest charging deportation culpabilities of all probable kinds — a hearing conducted by an immigrant inspector recommended by the Department of Justice for his special qualifications, a hearing that was prolonged over several months by adjournments solicited by the Department of Justice for the purpose of piecing out testimony — not a shred of proof of any deportable conduct of a personal\kind by Mr. Martens was produced.

As the decision in his case was made by the Secretary of Labor in person, the pertinence of the case to my narrative is related less to the decision than to the prior proceedings. It will be enough here to say of my own connection with the decision, that I participated in an advisory capacity at and after the hearing and agreed with the Secretary in his conclusion. The anterior proceedings, however, are distinctly pertinent to my story.

Upon arriving at my office on the morning of the first business day of the year 1920, I found upon my desk a file containing an elaborate brief from the office of the Attorney General in support of an application by the secret-service division of the Department of Justice for a warrant of arrest for Mr. Martens. The cause proposed for the arrest was, as I remember, membership in the Communist Party of Russia. I had finished reading the brief when the Commissioner General of Immigration, Mr. Caminetti, came into my office to confer with me about it. At the conclusion of our conference I told him that if the matter were submitted to me in the absence of the Secretary of Labor, I should give instructions to issue a warrant if the proposed grounds of arrest were properly brought within the deportations law, but would prohibit and prevent any spectacular arrest. I also alluded to the fact that Great Britain was according polite treatment to the Russian ambassador to that government, whereas we were being importuned to treat the one to ours with indignities; and in connection with this allusion I said I should hesitate to issue a warrant for Mr. Martens, even if probable cause were shown, without consulting the Secretary of State. Mr. Caminetti suggested that I confer with Mr. Abercrombie, the Solicitor, who was then acting for the Secretary of Labor in immigration matters. He explained that Mr.

Abercrombie had already conferred with the Secretary of State. So I called in Mr. Abercrombie, who told me that the Secretary of State had in person assured him that the* arrest of Mr. Martens for deportation would not embarrass the State Department. Upon this assurance I reminded Mr. Abercrombie that he was for the time in charge of immigration duties for the Secretary of Labor and assured him that I would not interfere unless in response to his request for instructions in the Secretary's absence; but that if I were called upon for instructions I would direct that a warrant of arrest issue for Mr. Martens, if probable cause for deportation under the law were properly proved, but would forbid its execution under circumstances calculated to serve sensational purposes. To this Mr. Abercrombie replied that perhaps I had better return the file without instructions, and I did so. My understanding of his motive in making this suggestion was then and still is that a request from him for instructions might seem like thrusting upon me a responsibility which he felt he ought to bear himself.

I heard nothing further about the matter for more than two months. Meanwhile Mr. Martens came to Washington to testify before a committee of the Senate. Prior to appearing before this committee and being paroled by it, as he was when he appeared, he kept his whereabouts in Washington a secret, apparently to avoid humiliating arrest by Department of Justice detectives. Through their publicity agency they were filling the newspapers with stories of the audacious things they would do to Martens as soon as they could lay hands on him. This pursuit necessarily ceased when Martens appeared formally before the Senate committee and received the protection of its parole pending his examination as one of its witnesses.

Although the sensational reports of attempts to arrest Mr. Martens indicated that the arrest was to be by authority of a Department of Labor warrant, I gave no official attention to the subject because I was not then acting for the Secretary of Labor in immigration matters, the Solicitor being still in charge of this phase of the Department's work. But soon after the Solicitor's temporary withdrawal from the Department, in consequence of which the immigration work came under my immediate supervision, my official private secretary, Mr. Reid, informed me that he had learned that at the expiration of the Senate committee's parole, Martens was to be arrested in a sensational manner by Department of Justice detectives under a Department of Labor warrant. According to Mr.

Reid's further information, three or four of these detectives were to make a conspicuous seizure of Martens in a public place staged for the tableau. Photographs for newspapers were to be taken on the spot, moving picture cameras were to be at hand and at work, and telegraphic material for full-page headlines was to go out to the press of the continent. Mr. Reid's sources of information were such that no room for doubt remained.

What was to be done with Martens after the arrest can only be inferred from what was credibly alleged to have been done to some aliens arrested on Department of Labor warrants by Department of Justice detectives. Taken to secret places instead of immigration stations — to secret places under the permanent or temporary control of the Department of Justice — they afterward complained of having been there subjected to various grades of the "third degree, " from gruelling cross-examinations to nameless physical torture.

Regardless, however, of what was to be done with Mr. Martens after his arrest, my information as to the arrest itself indicated that it was to be a species of public entertainment for which the Department of Labor could not decently allow itself to be responsible. I therefore took steps to frustrate, not the arrest, but an abusive, lawless, indecent and scandalous method of making it.

Having first assured myself that a Labor-Department warrant of arrest was actually in the hands of Department of Justice detectives, I ordered its withdrawal from them and its transmittal to me. This order having been obeyed, I arranged to have the warrant of arrest executed decently by an immigration officer. Communicating then with the senior counsel for Mr. Martens who had in charge his case before the Senate committe — ex-Senator Hardwick of Georgia^^ soon afterwards elected Governor of his State — I indicated to him that if Mr. Martens were arrested by the Department of Labor upon his release by the Senate committee, a suggestion for paroling him to his counsel pending his hearing would be favorably considered. Meanwhile I arranged with Senator Moses, chairman of the Senate committee which then held Mr. Martens under parole, to notify me long enough in advance of Mr. Martens 's release to enable me to make concurrently a decent arrest — an arrangement which Senator Moses entered into with an expression of his agreement with my view of the decencies involved. In due time he notified me that his committee would release Mr. Martens within a few hours; and almost at the same moment ex-Senator Hardwick informed me that he would come to my office with his client at such hour as I might set for the preliminaries of a parole to the Department of Labor. I therefore made no at-, tempt at arrest until Mr. Martens and his counsel arrived.

Strictly according to his promise, ex-Senator Hardwick brought Mr. Martens to my office. When they arrived I made the arrest myself in legal form under the Department of Labor warrant which I had kept in my possession ever since withdrawing it from the Department of Justice; and, having made the arrest, I forthwith heard and granted Mr. Hardwick's application for parole of his client to himself pending decision of the case. The terms of the parole required Mr. Hardwick to produce Mr. Martens at any time in the future upon demand of the Secretary of Labor.

When that preliminary arrangement had *been made, I directed Inspector A. P. Schell, as examining inspector, to take charge of the regular immigration-office hearing, instructing him to proceed in every respect in the usual manner under the immigration law and the Departmental rules of procedure.

Mr. Schell was then unknown to me either personally or by reputation, although he had been responsibly connected for many years with the immigration service. I selected him because I had seen in the Martens record a request by the Attorney General for his appointment as examining inspector in the Martens case on the ground that he was especially well qualified for the kind of investigation which that case required. It is due the Attorney General's advisers to say that their judgment in that instance turned out to be a sound one, and to Mr. Schell that he proved to be a thoroughly competent and fair-minded examining inspector.

When the record in the Martens case came back to the Department several months later, T referred it to the Secretary of Labor for his decision in person on the merits, he having then fully resumed his official functions. A lengthy argumentative hearing was accorded by Secretary Wilson, at which three counsel for Mr. Martens (Hardwick, Hourwich and Recht) and three for the Attorney General, (Hoover, Stone and Grimes) had ample opportunity to present every phase of the case. Considered as a case against a dangerous "red 11, such as Mr. Martens had for months been paraded in inspired newspaper stories as being, the presentation against him was frivolous. If he had not been an official of the unrecognized government of Russia, he could not have been deported under any law of ours.

In an extended opinion, Secretary Wilson explained that although Mr. Martens was not proved to have done anything unlawful as an individual, yet as he was an official of the Soviet Government of Russia, which was conducting a propaganda in the United States for the purpose of creating an insurrection to overthrow the Government of the United States by force and violence, he was "a member of or affiliated with an organization that entertained a belief in, taught, or advocated the overthrow by force or violence of the Government of the United States;" and that this fact, taken in conjunction with his expressed belief in and approval of the Third International, also proved, appeared to demonstrate that Mr. Martens "believed in the overthrow by force or violence of the Government of the United States."

It was those inferences, and those alone, that either necessitated or permitted the deportation of Mr. Martens. But as they did necessitate it, Secretary Wilson decided, correctly in my opinion, that Mr. Martens must be deported from the United States to Russia.

Supplementary to that decision, however, Secretary Wilson permitted Mr. Martens to return to Russia voluntarily at his own expense; and upon receiving proof of his actual arrival in Russia, ordered cancellation of the warrant of arrest. This concession was fully justified upon grounds of public policy. Had Mr. Martens been forcibly deported, he could never lawfully have returned to this country. No matter what the circumstances, he would upon returning have been subject to prosecution and imprisonment for felony, supplemented with deportation anew. In the event, therefore, of a reversal by the then incoming Presidential administration of the policy of its predecessor regarding the relations of the United States with Russia, the status of Mr. Martens as an ambassador who had been ignominiously expelled from the United States and as therefore incompetent to enter it again even officially (the cause of his expulsion being his official status in that very Government) might give rise to diplomatic embarrassments in the practical adjustment of relations between the two countries. Those were Secretary Wilson's reasons for cancelling the warrant of arrest after Mr. Martens had voluntarily returned to Russia. There was no warrant of deportation.

### Chapter XXXIV: Lawless Meddling with the Mail

Another notable episode of the deportations delirium had to do with lawless violations of the sanctity of the United States mail. Detectives of the Department of Justice were discovered to be interfering, without the least shadow of lawful authority, with the postal rights of aliens held at Ellis Island for deportation.

Complaints of this unlawful censorship had come to me, but not officially nor in substance or form sufficient to warrant official action. They disturbed me, however, and I requested the Solicitor of the Department (Eowland B. Mahany at that time) to make informal and judicious inquiries. Not long afterwards I received from Frederick A. Wallis, the Commissioner of Immigration then at Ellis Island, an official letter in which he asked for instructions regarding the opening and censoring by agents of the Department of Justice of letters to and from aliens in his custody. This letter I referred to Solicitor Mahany with an official request for legal advice on the subject. Informing me in response that there was no legal authority for censoring Ellis Island mail except such as the Department of Labor might have as custodian of Government territory used as a place of detention, he advised, quite in harmony with my own judgment, that inasmuch as letters are not in the mail until actually in the possession of the Post Office, and are out of the mail upon delivery at their respective destinations, the censorship authority of the Department of Labor might be regarded as similar to the limited authority in that respect which State officials exercise in prisons. Thereupon I requested the Solicitor to proceed in the matter as Acting Secretary, and he did so.

Upon investigation he learned that the mail of aliens held at Ellis Island for deportation under Department of Labor warrants had been habitually censored by an employee of the Department of Justice attached to New York headquarters of that Department's Bureau of Investigation — its detective bureau or division. This detective visited Ellis Island regularly for that purpose. Among those who complained of his practice was a lawyer who stated that confidential communications from a client were being examined and sometimes confiscated. Another instance involved a letter from an imprisoned alien at Ellis Island to the Department of Labor.

No employee of the Department of Labor had been authorized to exercise any such supervision, and, so far as appeared, none had attempted to do so. Commissioner Wallis had impatiently tolerated it on the part of the agent of the Department of Justice under an impression — a reasonable impression in the circumstances — that the Department of Justice was acting by lawful administrative authority. In fact, however, there not only was no law to authorize the conduct of the Department of Justice agent, but the Department of Labor, from which alone any authorization to censor prisoners' correspondence could lawfully come, had never given him or his Department the slightest token or color of permission.

An examination of Department of Labor files disclosed no information as to the origin of the practice; nor did they furnish evidence of so much as a request from the Department of Justice for permission to pursue its inquisitorial handling of mail matter. The whole procedure was an unwarranted usurpation of authority by irresponsible detectives.

When he had satisfied himself as to the facts, Mr. Mahany as Acting Secretary, put an end peremptorily to this phase of the reckless "red" crusade of the Department of Justice and its group of private detectives publicly employed, by issuing specific instructions to Commissioner Wallis, which Mr. Wallis was not at all disturbed at receiving nor reluctant to obey, and which were in these terms: "It is the policy of the Department not to permit a continuance of such censorship by any person or official outside its own jurisdiction" and "all censorship" must "be restricted to an irreducible minimum even when exercised under your own personal control or that of a trusted official especially selected by you to exercise this War function, and for the legal period of the War only. I need not point out to you that any tampering with the privacy of mails, however advisable it may seem as a War measure, has a tendency to familiarize officials with the idea and thus indirectly argue in their minds for the continuance of the practice in times of peace. The sacredness of the mails is one of the great pillars of civil liberty comparable to and coequal with the sanctity of the private dwelling. Encroachment upon either right is to the degree of the encroachment destructive of civilization itself, and cannot lightly be permitted. Only the gravest considerations of national danger will justify the opening of a single letter. Under no circumstances are letters to be molested while in the jurisdiction of the postal authorities. You are therefore directed to abolish at once outside censorship of the mails at Ellis Island, and to conduct such censorship as you may deem advisable, in accordance with the considerations above set forth. "

### Chapter XXXV: Indictment of the Department of Justice by Twelve Eminent American Lawyers

Among the extraordinary episodes of the deportations delirum, the most important in character and effect was the publication of a vigorous indictment of the Department of Justice by a voluntary jury of twelve high grade lawyers. This indictment was based upon the personal rights clauses of the American Constitution, which it charged the Department of Justice with violating.

The lawyers who composed that unconventional jury were twelve in number. The chief member of the group was Roscoe Pound, dean of the Law School at Harvard University, and a man who for his professional attainments and abilities was already famous in the legal fraternity throughout the United States. Dean Pound was supported by Zechariah Chafee, Jr., also a professor at the Harvard Law School and now further distinguished as the author of a highly important American law book, 1 and by Felix Frankfurter, Harvard professor of administrative law. The Middle West was represented in the group by Ernst Freund, professor of jurisprudence and public law at the Uni1 Freedom of Speech. By Zechariah Chafee, Jr., Professor of Law in Harvard University. "Give your mind sea room." New York: Harcourt, Brace and Howe, 1920.

versity of Chicago, and Tyrrell Williams, a professor in the Law School of the Washington University at St. Louis. Frank P. Walsh of Kansas City, New York City and Washington, D. C, a legal practitioner of national eminence, was also one of the group, as were Swinburne Hale of New York City, David Wallerstein of Philadelphia and R. Gratz Brown of Memphis. Baltimore was represented by ex-Judge Alfred S. Niles, and Washington, D. C, by Jackson H. Ralston. The distinguished exUnited States Attorney for the Eastern District of Pennsylvania, Francis Fisher Kane, completed the group.

This voluntary jury of high-grade lawyers, some of national and all of local distinction in their profession, had been impaneled by the National Popular Government League, a non-partisan body organized nationally with headquarters at Washington long before the World War, and which from the beginning has been served by Judson King as its executive secretary. Its controlling purpose has all along been to promote legislative measures tending: to a further democratization of the political mechanism of the American republic. When we entered the War some of our War-legislation excited alarm in the League lest it become permanent after the War need for it should have passed away. This alarm seemed to be in great degree justified by peace-time sedition bills introduced in Congress under the influence of the Department of Justice, the administration of which, if they became law, as fortunately they did not, might very easily have subjected public printing and speaking to a censorship as repressive as that which prevailed in Russia under the Czars. The National Popular Government League therefore concluded that its policy of advancing the principles of popular government to higher levels of democracy must for a time give way to, or at least be fortified with, a policy of actively defending the levels of popular government already achieved and for which the American Constitution is our written guarantee. The necessity for such a policy was emphasized by the autocratic conduct of the Department of Justice in its "red" crusade.

True, then, to the American ideals for the promotion and preservation of which the League had been organized years before and for which it existed, this organization assembled twenty of its officers and members to consider the threatening situation and to recommend a policy. By that conference the League was advised to publish widely a responsible report upon the methods of Attorney General Palmer in his "red" crusade, and in consequence of that advice the lawyers' indictment of the Department of Justice for crimes against Americanism was secured and published by the League. It had a wide circulation and produced a profound impression.

Although this indictment covers 67 printed pages, the text occupies but seven. The remainder of the pamphlet is composed of exhibits of which there are thirty-one. 1 The entire document was addressed to the American people.

It classifies in six counts the illegal acts with which it charged the Department of Justice and of which it offered proof in its thirty-one appended exhibits. The six counts were enumerated in this order:

First Co unt.— Cruel and unusual punishments contrary to the Eighth Amendment of the American Constitution, namely, third-degree tortures.

Second Count.— Arrests without warrant, contrary to the Fourth Amendment of the Constitution, namely, raids upon public meetings.

Third Count.— Unreasonable searches and seizures contrary to the Fourth Amendment of the Constitution. Fourth Count.— Use of provocative agents, such as were familiar in old Russia and Spain, who have brought about meetings in order to make wholesale arrests thereat.

Fifth Count.— Compelling persons to be witnesses against themselves, contrary to the Fifth Amendment of the Constitution.

Sixth Count.— Misuse of his office by the Attorney General, and deliberate squandering by him of public funds, by going into the field of propaganda instead of confining his activities to advising the Government on questions of law, and prosecuting persons who violate Federal statutes.

1 Copies of this document may be had of the National Popular Government League, Judson King, Executive Secretary, Munsey Building, Washington, D. C.

This indictment of the Department of Justice by the group of distinguished lawyers named above and whose signatures were attached to it, closed with these pregnant words: "It has always been the proud boast of America that this is a government of laws and not of men. Our Constitution and laws have been based upon the simple elements of human nature. Free men cannot be driven and repressed; they must be led. Free men respect justice and follow truth, but arbitrary power they will oppose until the end of time. There is no danger of revolution so great as that created by suppression, by ruthlessness, and by deliberate violation of the simple rules of American law and American decency. It is a fallacy to suppose that, any more than in the past, any servant of the people can safely arrogate to himself unlimited authority. To proceed upon such a supposition is to deny the fundamental American theory of the consent of the governed. Here is no question of a vague and threatened menace, but a present assault upon the most sacred principles of our Constitutional liberty. "

### Chapter XXXVI: The Department of Justice at the Bar of the Senate

Through Senator Thomas J. Walsh of Montana, a Constitutional lawyer of high national repute, the Lawyers' Indictment of the Department of Justice, told of in the preceding Chapter, came before the Senate of the United States. Being there referred to the Senate Committee on the Judiciary, its accusations were brought to a hearing. For the purpose of conducting this hearing the Judiciary Committee created a sub-committee consisting of Senators Thomas Sterling of South Dakota, William E.

Borah of Idaho, Le Baron B. Colt of Rhode Island, Thomas J. Walsh of Montana and William H. King of Utah. Two of these Senators were party Democrats—Walsh and King. The other three were Republicans.

At the sub-committee hearings the principal participants were Senators Sterling (chairman of the sub-committee), Senator Walsh and Senator King. Beginning on the 19th of January, 1921, the hearings ended on the 3d of March following. For more than a year after the hearings had ended, the chairman of the sub-committee, Senator Sterling, took no steps to bring the proceedings of the inquiry or its results to the attention of the main committee, the Judiciary Committee, so that the testimony might be laid before the Senate along with a committee report. Meanwhile Senator Walsh, his requests for sub-committee action ignored by the chairman, prepared a report which he brought before the main committee, at the same time making a motion to discharge the sub-committee. The sub-committee was accordingly discharged by the main committee; but the sub-committee chairman, Senator Sterling, was given time, at his own solicitation, to prepare a report for the sub-committee as a substitute for that proposed by Senator Walsh. When Senator Sterling presented his proposed sub-committee report, Senator Walsh submitted a supplementary report by way of reply. The subject matter of those three reports reposed undisturbed, along with the reports, in the pigeonholes of the Senate Committee on Judiciary of the Senate until February 5, 1923,— 0ver two years after the sub-committee hearings began and one year and eleven months after they were closed —when Senator Walsh forced the matter upon the attention of the Senate.

From the Congressional Record of February 5, 1923, at page 3051, it appears that the Judiciary Committee of the Senate had refused to adopt the Walsh report as the report of the Committee, the motion to adopt it being defeated by the votes of Senators Dillingham, Sterling, Ernst, Shortridge, Overman, Shields and Nelson, against those of Senators Walsh, Norris, Ashurst and Borah. Thereupon the Committee resolved to make upon the subject no report at all. Consequently Senator Walsh asked in open session of the Senate that all the reports submitted to the Judiciary Committee be printed in the Congressional Record and that the Judiciary Committee be discharged from further consideration of the subject. There being no objection, the Committee was accordingly discharged and the reports were printed in full in the Record of February 5,' 1923, between page 3051 and page 3073. Those reports as there printed make interesting reading. They consist, as indicated above, of Senator Walsh's proposed sub-committee report, of Senator Sterling's proposed sub-committee report, and of Senator Walsh's report in reply. The last concludes with a stirring appeal to the true principles of Americanism. Referring to the excuse for official lawlessness, which the Attorney General advanced and Senator Sterling adopted, that feeling had run high on account of a "general apprehension of the spread of bolshevism, because of the dastardly bomb outrages and the activities generally of the 'reds,' " Senator Watefs proposed but buried report replied: "It is only in such times that the guarantees of the Constitution as to personal rights are of any practical value. In seasons of calm, no one thinks of denying them; they are accorded as a matter of course. It is rare, except when the public mind is stirred by some overwhelming catastrophe, or is aghast at some hideous crime, or otherwise overwrought, that one is required to appeal to his Constitutional rights. If in such times the Constitution is not a shield, the encomiums which statesmen and jurists have paid it are fustian. "

### Chapter XXXVII: Summary and Conclusion

The most important facts of a portentous fragment of American history are assembled in the foregoing personal narrative of a peculiar official experience. Following immediately upon the close of the World War, a popular delirium spread over the United States. It expressed itself in frenzied demands for deportations of alien residents. It rose to its peak in 1920. This delirium derived its distinctive characteristic from reports of mysterious crimes attributed to aliens. With nerves unstrung, the public easily saw anarchy spooks in every shadow. A postal explosives incident of the year before, closely followed by a hideous bombing of private residences, including the Washington home of the Attorney General, excited terrifying suspicions. Although the origin of those crimes has never been fixed, nor even indicated with reasonable probability, the crimes themselves were outstanding facts and stimulants of fear.

Were it asked why the delirium concentrated upon alien deportations, the answer that there is never a "why" for a delirious impulse might satisfy but it would not be the best. A better is indicated by the fact that bombing crimes, real and imaginary alike, were associated in American thought with alien "reds." The "red" epithet / f had been suggested by the color of the flag of socialistic internationalism. Intended originally to symbolize world brotherhood, a hint from the Apostolic declaration — "of one blood all nations of men," — this flag had been interpreted by hostile opinion, not as a declaration of blood unity but as a threat of blood sacrifice. That sinister interpretation was reinforced in the United States with a bitterly patriotic aversion to the red flag as an alien menace to the red-white-and-blue. These mental associations of "reds" with foreigners, and of "red" foreigners with destructive conspiracies, were intensified by sensational reports of revolutionary horrors in Soviet Russia where the "red"

flag waved. Color hatreds sometimes cut strange capers, not only in bull pastures but also in political regimes. Thomas Jefferson was a "red" to Federalists early in our national history, and Abraham Lincoln was a "black" to pro-slavery Democrats sixty years later.

A further reason for the concentration of our recent national delirium upon alien deportations, lay in the prejudice against immigrants which had been in process of cultivation here for nearly forty years. It was essentially very much like the bitter anti-Irish prejudice of our Knownothing period. There was this important circumstantial difference, however, that in the Knownothing period foreigners could not be penalized without a judicial trial, such as citizens were Constitutionally guaranteed, whereas at the period of this delirium a process of arbitrary deportation by Executive authority had been established.

With suspicions of bomb-throwing thus centered upon aliens, and with arbitrary deportations of resident aliens by administrative process as a ready method of penalization, the force of the delirium turned in the direction of a deportations crusade with the spontaneity of water flowing along the course of least resistance.

Doubtless, too, this delirium was accelerated by a war frenzy which survived the World War. War gets on the nerves of a people. It breeds popular hysteria as naturally as excessive whiskey-drinking breeds imaginary monsters. The proof of this which the aftermaths of all wars afford, has been overwhelmingly confirmed by the wild behavior of nations in Europe following the World War. Is it then to be wondered at that the people of the United States, though habitually inclined to boast of their poise, also fell victims to one of these after-war deliriums?

In stimulating the American delirium, a conspicuous agency was the Department of Justice, one of the Executive departments of the Federal Government — not, however, in its regular and legitimate organization, nor under the regime of Attorney General Gregory, but through a privatedetective adjunct which Attorney General Palmer attached to it. Public money appropriated by Congress for "the detection and prosecution of crimes against the United States " was lavishly used by this detective adjunct of that Department as if the detection and prosecution of crime were legally synonymous with the detection and deportation of unwelcome aliens. The two functions are different in law from the ground up. But that irregularity was trivial in comparison with the anti-American spirit and the lawless procedure with which those detectives carried on the deportations crusade.

Comparing their victims to " moral rats"

menacing the community — a denunciation they were able to verify in but few instances, and too feebly in nearly every one for any kind of criminal prosetion,—those detectives indulged the mischievous notion that in catching their "moral rats" the effectiveness of the traps was more important than their lawful construction. It was a new version of the long-ago discarded rule that "the end justifies the means"— new in this, that it regarded the end itself as needing no justification.

So detectives of the Department of Justice ruthlessly invaded peaceable homes, in the small hours of the morning, without warrants but upon a pretense of imminent danger to the community, and arresting inmates in their beds, searched their rooms, seized lawful private property, and hurried their prisoners to police stations where, before the sun had risen, they subjected them to "third degree" examinations in efforts to discover evidence of a guilt that apparently did not exist. So, also, those detectives made sweeping arrests of whole audiences at public meetings, rounding up citizens and aliens without discrimination, and standing them against meeting-room walls, searched them threateningly after the manner of highwaymen robbing groups of travellers. After the search they usually turned citizens loose, but the aliens they marched off to prison, and at least once conspicuously in manacles and chains. They jailed crowds of these prisoners in quarters so close, and held them there so long, that Nature protested with sickening odors. They kept prisoners incommunicado, old Spanish fashion, for days at a stretch, lawlessly intercepting their letters in the mail, depriving them of the help of friends and the services of lawyers, placing them beyond the reach of writs of habeas corpus, and hiding them so that their families were in distress from ignorance and fear. All that and more like it, or worse, the secret-service agents of the Department of Justice, recruited from private detective agencies, did with scornful indifference to the personal liberty clauses of the American Constitution. To know the story is to recall the revolting secret-police operations of Germany and Russia before the World War had abolished kaisers and czars.

And the victims, those " moral rats," what were their offenses! Were they criminals? In almost every instance, No. Had crime been the accusation, there would have been no excuse for releasing citizens; crime by a citizen is not less criminal than crime by an alien. The accusations related only to conduct which, under our deportation laws, terminates the residential rights of aliens and authorizes their deportation by mere administrative process. Nothing but membership in an organization of a proscribed class— a membership the significance of which might be unknown to the accused person and in most instances was in fact unknown to him, a membership in which citizens might participate without question— was the dangerous conduct charged against nearly all the " moral rats" caught in those lawless traps of that "red" crusade.

Some of the proscribed organizations were finally found to be lawful with reference to aliens no less than to citizens. About them all there was as to their lawfulness disagreement between Federal courts. Yet unsophisticated and well-disposed % foreigners who for some reason, usually a legitimate one, had become members of one or another of those organizations, were lawlessly arrested and imprisoned. Many were not even nominal members, while many more had been transferred without their knowledge to unlawful organizations by organizazations conceded to be lawful.

The records of accusation and evidence for which the Department of Justice made itself responsible through its secret-service auxiliary, and which are now in the files of the Bureau of Immigration by the thousands, would disgrace the judgment of any lawyer who dared cite them in vindication of the epidemic of arrests to which they relate. Only slightly more than a third of all the aliens arrested were legally deportable upon the evidence submitted. Nor could many of these have been legally deported but for their own admissions of unpopular opinions or of membership in proscribed organizations supposed by them to be lawfully educational or social. Except for the membership clauses of the deportation laws there could hardly have been, out of the thousands of aliens charged with deportable offenses, more than a canoe load of deportees. As to crime, actual or contemplated, the proofs submitted to the Secretary of Labor were so deficient that convictions in a criminal court would in almost every case have been impossible. Somewhat roughly, but quite accurately, a competent Federal officer who officially investigated the deportations campaign in all its principal geographical centers, succinctly described it as a 1 l headhunting jamboree. ' '

So far did the lawless spirit of the Department of Justice carry its detectives and their coadjutors, that they directed their machinery of intimidation at every official who in the performance of his duty was trying to administer justly the law of the land in the "red" deportations. For refusing in the great majority of those cases to order deportations because the law and the facts did not warrant it, the Assistant Secretary of Labor, who alone (the Secretary of Labor being absent) was charged by statute with making the necessary decisions of guilt or innocence, was denounced as_ a_ sympathizer with lawless agitations and criminal agitators. These denunciations were manifestly instigated by secretservice agents whose indefensible purposes he had foiled. With similar malice the denunciations were repeated, though ignorantly and crudely, by a subcommittee of the immigration committee of the lower branch of Congress. They were recklessly made also from the floor of the House. So deceptive were they that an innocent Congressman from Kansas was gulled by them into fathering a resolution to impeach the Assistant Secretary, of Labor for high crimes and misdemeanors. When the truth came out, these impeachment proceedings collapsed. In contrast with the impeachment fiasco, one of the most gratifying and reassuring of all the events developed by the deportations delirium occurred. Twelve American lawyers of honorable distinction — from the North, the West and the South — with the eminent Dean of the Law School of Harvard University at their head, publicly indicted the Department of Justice, on Constitutional grounds, for its lawlessness in connection with the deportations crusade. Their indictment was brought to the attention of the United States Senate by one of the ablest Constitutional lawyers in that body, and the Senate ordered an investigation. The investigation having been made, the facts disclosed were by Constitutional tests damning— damning to such a degree that the protagonists for the Department of Justice on the investigating committee delayed for months, nearly or quite a year, any action whatever. It was their evident purpose to smother the Lawyers' Indictment in a blanket of Senatorial silence. After this long delay, however, they were forced into the open by the commanding persistence of a minority member of the Committee, the Senator who had brought on the investigation. He challenged further trifling by offering in behalf of the Committee a report of Ms own, which, upon the evidence before the Committee, condemned the conduct of the detectives of the Department of Justice and the policy of the Department itself. This proposed report drew from the reluctant chairman of the investigating committee a report of the kind commonly called "whitewash." Virtually conceding the condemnatory facts, the chairman's report excused them by theories of Constitutional construction that might have charmed the "Feds" who railed at Thomas Jefferson. Such theories, if accepted, would cut a destructive swath through the Constitution's Bill of Eights. They were thoroughly refuted by a report in reply by the author of the first proposed report. Nearly two years after the completion of its investigation, the Committee was excused by the Senate from further action in the matter. The proposed reports, however, were by the Senate put into its own record as part of its proceedings.

More important considerations even than those relating to the Senate's investigation of the Department of Justice, are involved in my story of the deportations delirium. Our laws for the protection of our Government from aliens conspiring for its forcible overthrow, had been so drawn as to necessitate deportations for harmless expressions of harmless opinions. Alien residents were consequently persecuted to the extreme of expulsion for opinion's sake. This was excused on the theory that resident aliens are merely guests, with no right to propagate opinions. But a nation devoted to liberty of opinion and freedom of speech cannot deny either right to resident aliens without launching a tendency to deny both rights to citizens also. Such a tendency would in those circumstances be in harmony with the nature of things political. In this historic instance it became manifest through measures introduced in Congress directly in response to delirious outcries for further legislation regulating opinion and restraining speech. The scope of this proposed legislation was not limited to resident aliens. Citizens also were within its purpose. Like our alien deportation laws, these more inclusive measures. were cleverly drawn. While they seemed to penalize only such opinions and such speech as were intentional incitements to crime, they would have furnished pretexts for punishing protagonists of any kind of change in government, however peaceable the method advocated, if it were repugnant to prevailing habits of partisan thought among the powerful.

One of them, for instance, would have made it a crime for any person to " display or exhibit at any meeting, gathering or parade, or in any other public place, any flag, banner, emblem, picture, motto or device which tends to incite or indicates a purpose to overthrow, change,"— note the word change,— "or defeat the Constitution of the United States and the laws and authority thereof." From this clause the element of force or violence was omitted. Nor could that element have been imported by construction from other clauses into this one. A display such as any of those described in the proposed law, would have subjected every person responsible for it to punishment, if the display indicated a purpose, however peaceable the method, to change the Constitution of the United States. Had such a measure been in force as law when the income-tax, the liquor-prohibition, or the women 's suffrage Amendments to the Constitution were proposed, every display of emblems indicating a purpose to make any of those changes, though by Constitutional methods, would have been a crime until the Federal courts had cancelled it as un-Constitutional. Absurd? It would seem so. No more absurd, though, than the possibility of our "red" deportations would have seemed in Jefferson's day, or in Lincoln's, or even as late as McKinley's. That manifest absurdity aside, however, those sedition measures were so cunningly drafted that their enforcement clauses, though nominally limited to agitations in support of violence against the Government, could have been aimed with deadly effect at legitimate speech and legitimate print. Every person, for example, would have been subject to criminal prosecution for teaching, advising or defending "the destruction of human life, or the injury of any human being, or the injury or destruction of any private property, as a means of changing the Constitution, laws or Government of the United States or defeating the authority thereof." On the surface this reads somewhat like legitimate lawmaking, but in actual operation it could have gagged the advocates of every progressive change. For such a law would put public speakers for popular freedom at the mercy of zealous detectives and hostile witnesses. Nothing could be easier of misrepresentation, for instance, by a "swift witness"

than a public speaker's legitimate demands for the overthrow of predatory property-systems. How could the difference between overthrow by regular revision or repeal, and overthrow by violent revolution, control the imagination of witnesses whose pay for detecting revolutionary crime depended upon criminal convictions? Vigorous metaphors in common use might easily serve in their imaginations to transform legitimate agitations into agitations for revolutionary violence. Quite suggestive in this connection is the anecdote of the American newspaper correspondent abroad, who, having cabled to his editor a piece of news about Lloyd George which he discovered immediately afterwards to be incorrect, was taken into custody by a European secret-service bureau for filing a supplementary message telling his editor to "kill Lloyd George." For similar technical, slangy or otherwise freakish uses of English speech, and even for higher-grade metaphors, more than one resident alien was arrested during the deportations delirium for deportation as an alien "red."

Such drastic laws would not only have endangered legitimate liberties, but in the interest of order they were absolutely needless. The Federal laws already included ample provision for penalizing sedition. They provided that "if two or more persons in any State or Territory, or in any place subject to the jurisdiction of the United States, conspire to overthrow, put down or to destroy by force the Government of the United States, or to levy war against them, or to oppose by force the authority thereof, or by force to prevent, hinder or delay the execution of any law of the United States, or by force to seize, take or possess any property of the United States contrary to the authority thereof, they shall be fined not more than five thousand dollars or imprisoned not more than six years, or both." Under this law, the Department of Justice, with its huge appropriations for the detection and prosecution of crimes against the United States, could have prosecuted, convicted and punished any of the victims of its "red" raids, citizens or aliens, if in truth it had possessed the proofs of guilt which it pretended to have.

With that law in the Federal statute books there was no legitimate need for further laws to protect our Government from forcible assault by conspirators within its borders. The evident purpose of those marvelous Congressional measures described above, and the only need for them if it may be called a need, was as a subterfuge for destroying freedom of speech and assembly in the United States.

Do any Americans really wish to destroy freedom of speech and assembly in the United States?

Does any genuine American wish even to hamper either of those traditional American liberties? Are they not in all candor the best possible conservators of social order? These questions get their American answer in the quoted words that follow:

"We must fearlessly and always stand for the free speech and free press and free assembly guaranteed under our Constitution.

What have we to fear in the free discussion of anything? If our institutions will not bear discussion something ails them. If any of our citizens, native or foreign-born, or even any aliens among us, have other institutions to recommend, let us look them over. If they obtain support we can vote on them. Only through such processes can a democratic people advance.

"That is the English way; they are not afraid of open discussion. All through the War — a war which pressed infinitely closer to their life than it did to ours — the great rallying ground for open-air meetings in London (Hyde Park Corner) was utilized by all sorts of oppositions, and even by those which preached violence. The pacifists, the Socialists, even the Sinn Feiners, spoke there to crowds without interference. An American is reported to have asked a policeman in amazement if the authorities were not going to stop a Sinn Fein meeting then in progress before his eyes. 'No,' said the bobby, 'hit don't 'urt us, and hit does 'em good to git it out o' they systems.'

From an American paper of May 7, 1920, the Akron (Ohio) Beacon Journal, I have taken the following: 'The American and the English Way. The British Socialist Party a few days ago held a convention in the town hall of Bethnal Green. The Mayor presided. Among the speakers was one Mr. Hodgson. We quote from the London Times: "Mr. Hodgson said that when the opportunity came they were going to lead the working class to seize power, and that if there was any opposition they were going to fight. He thought the best and easiest way to avoid excessive bloodshed was to make it clear now that they were going to fight for power. What they were setting out to do could neve* be done except by force of arms.

The working classes must arm themselves for the great day when they must rout the capitalist class by force." Nobody was arrested or deported. The British Empire has not crumbled away. Property has not perished at the end of the torch. In other words, England is once more pursuing her historic policy of letting her radicals talk their heads off to anybody that will listen to them? "Moreover, if we are to create a great solidarity out of diversities — a Nation that functions as a Social Man — we must hear and ponder what all the various elements have to offer." 1 As the Congressional measures for obstructing freedom of speech and assembly in the United Xs 1 From an Address by Alice Thacher Post before the National Alliance of New-Church Women, at Brooklyn, New York, June 4, 1920.

States, which are recalled above, did not secure Congressional sanction, freedom of speech and assembly are not yet subject to censorship. But a revival of the delirium of which my story tells, or the outbreak of another like it, would with probable ease lead on to their enactment, if not by title and to the letter, at any rate in substance. They were reactionary measures, and reactionary legislation is characteristic of popular deliriums. Such aberrations help to turn the clock backward. Such legislation makes breaches in the barriers that democracy erects against autocracy. If it were to revive and to persist, the time might come nor be long delayed, when the American people would realize the significance of that grim story of the French passenger on a ship coming into New York Harbor, who, upon having the towering Statue of Liberty pointed out to him from the deck by a fellow passenger of the patriotic American variety, solemnly remarked: "Yes; we also erect statues to our illustrious dead."

Our deportations delirium of nineteen-twenty resembled very closely the social aberration that gave to Tory England the repressive "Six Acts" of a century before. Frightened by democratic organizations which sprang up in England after the Napoleonic wars, the aristocratic classes there demanded and of course obtained legislation for the protection of their privileges from public criticism and possible revocation. By those "Six Acts" of 1819, meetings of more than fifty persons were forbidden unless held upon six days' notice to a magistrate from seven householders, penalties for seditious publications were increased, and the democratic press was loaded down with stamp duties. Coming as they did a quarter of a century after the French Revolution, from which the delirious fear of democracy got its impulse in England, the "Six Acts" indicate a long persistence there of that delirium. Truly it did persist long. We have the authority of Sydney Smith for saying that it began with the beginning of the century and lasted until the death in 1828 of Lord Liverpool, who appears from his efforts at suppression of democratic discontent to have been the chief tory factor in keeping the fever of the delirium high. During this period of twenty-eight years, so Sydney Smith declared a few years later, persons in England who entertained liberal opinions and were too honest to sell them for judicial or ecclesiastical preferment, were sure to be assailed with all "the billingsgate of the French Revolution— Jacobin, leveller, atheist, deist, socinian, incendiary, regicide, " being "the gentlest appelations used." 1 Even as the antique billingsgate that Sydney Smith quotes was hurled at every type of democrat in England in his day,lfeo billingsgate of more modern design — red, radical^ socialist, anarchist, communist, bolshevik, atheist, freelover, — was at i Volume 3 of "British Essayists". Reprinted in "The Works of the Rev. Sydney Smith" (page 5 of Preface), published by Phillips, Sampson & Co., at Boston in 1856. the period of our deportations delirium hurled indiscriminately at every type of social reformer in the United States. Even Jane Addams, who rightly ranks among theHbest citizens of this Republic, was docketed as a dangerous " radical" by the super-, visors of American thought who sprang up at that time. But may not the epithet after all have been an unintentional tribute? "Radical" is an epithet that once honored the politics of Great Britain, and one which the Republican Party of the United States was in the sixties proud to bear.

The spirit of our deportations delirium is further identified with that of the British delirium of a century ago through an allusion with which the eminent British essayist from whom I have just quoted supplemented his samples of tory billingsgate. "Not a murmur was permitted," he wrote, "against any abuse which a rich man inflicted or a poor man suffered." That social insanity, then, was characterized as was our recent one, by an idolatrous reverence for property privileges. As the British delirium of Sydney Smith's day was precipitated by agitations which threatened the property privileges of an aristocracy intrenched in political power, precisely so was our deportations delirium of nineteen-twenty precipitated by agitations which seemed to threaten the property privileges of a similarly intrenched plutocracy. Exquisitely appropriate to both seems to be the sentiment of Tennyson's "Northern Farmer: New Style"— "Dosn't thou 'ear my 'erse's legs, as they canters awaay? Proputty, proputty, proputty — that's what I 'ears 'em saay. Proputty, proputty's ivrything 'ere, an', Sammy, I'm blest If it isn't the saame oop yonder, fur them as 'as it's the best. Tis'n them as 'as munny as breaaks into 'ouses an' steaals, Them as 'as coaats to their backs an' taakes their regular meaals.

Noa, but it's them as niver knaws wheer a meaal's to be 'ad. Taake my word for it, Sammy, the poor in a loomp is bad. Coom oop, proputty, proputty — that's what I '.ears 'im saay — Proputty, proputty, proputty — canter an' canter awaay." When "the communism of pelf", as President Cleveland called it, which could not thrive but for predatory property privileges, falls a victim to revolution anywhere in the world — as in France in Bobespierre's day or in Russia in ours — every opinion in hostility to property, whether righteous property or predacious property, looms up in fearhaunted minds as a social menace. These were the minds, studiously stirred by cooler ones, that caused and fostered our deportations delirium. Originating with manipulators of property interests of the predacious kind, it was spread among folk who had only prospective or possible interests in property of any kind; and out of the confusion leaped angry denunciations of social agitators, and frantic appeals to paganistic patriotism. As to agitators who happened to be aliens among us, my story tells the rest.

In characterizing the patriotism of the deportations delirium as paganistic, I use no epithet and intend no reflection upon citizens who, loving America for her democracy, adore our flag as her emblem. But between that kind of patriotism and mere pride of nationality as of a family tree, or such adoration of our flag as a pagan gives to his gods of wood and stone, there is a wide gulf.

We have reason to be proud of our federated republic as the great pioneer in democratic government. May we always be proud of her for that — all of us; better yet, may we always have that reason for our pride. But patriotism of this lofty kind does not lend itself to measurement by financial percentages. It can be judged only by the tests that Lincoln applied in his classic address at Gettysburg. Government of the people there must be> but let it be by the people and for the people. These patriotic principles, so succinctly phrased by Lincoln, are embodied in our Constitution, which gives legal form to the spirit of our Declaration of Independence, that "all men are created equal and endowed with certain unalienable rights, among whicli are life, liberty and the pursuit of happiness. ' ' This is of the essence of genuine American patriotism. And our Constitution, which vitalizes our Declaration, not only acknowledges its own origin as from the people, but it provides for its own amendment at their command. Under its amending clause we are at liberty to make our Government as democratic or as autocratic or as communistic or as anarchistic or as socialistic or as plutocratic as we collectively wish. Revolutions are not needed here to substitute newer and better, or worse, governmental conditions for those we t> have. Nothing is required ttjit the will of the people expressed in the Constitutional way.

It follows that no agitation whatever for changes in our Government, even to the point of complete abolishment, is unpatriotic, provided the method proposed is by Constitutional Amendment in accordance with the terms of the amending clause.

Nor will any truly patriotic American regard the amending clause as less sacred than any other clause of that great American document.

Many of those clauses are sacred, indeed; may they ever be so esteemed by the American people. Among them are the provisions against arbitrary arrests, against unwarranted searches and seizures, against abridgments of freedom of speech and press and peaceable assembly, and against exactions of excessive bail. Are not those provisions essential to a people's government! Our Constitution invites no sympathy with fears of "dangerous thoughts," nor tolerates any anxiety about dangerous words divorced from violent designs. Its primary concern is to secure to all the people freedom to think and speak and write and publish, and to establish personal liberty of each consistently with the personal liberty of all, wherever our flag flies.

Except as our flag is the emblem of that governmental ideal, what is it but a strip of cloth? To idolize the cloth itself regardless of what it symbolizes, the combination of mere fibre and color and form with no thought of its emblematic significance, what is this but fetish worship! To cherish it even as a symbol, but a symbol only of democratic achievements of the past, is to let our patriotism rot in a graveyard. Unless our flag means to us something different from that, at least something more than that, we deaden civic conscience by making it an object of popular adoration; and we supply handy equipment for demagogues and plutagogues. If, on the other hand, we revere the flag as our symbol of liberty triumphant in government, remembering that Liberty is not an empty word to fall trippingly from the tongue, but is the vital force of a growing social organism, the halo of a new glory will bathe that flag in resplendent light.

Flag worship of this kind is patriotic. But what can be said for the American who noisily, sometimes riotously, worships our national emblem without a spark of loyalty to the ideals it symbolizes? Many do. Some worship it hypocritically, for the profitable part their hypocrisy may play in their business or in their political maneuvers.
